THE RED RAGE.
“Convict Once and Other Poems.”
11.
WE have seen (May 11) that “ Convict
Once,” by J. Brunton Stephens, is
rather the work of a sentimental
Pedagogue than of a Poet. Apart
from its dramatic failure, apart from its flaws of
rhythm and rhyfne, of style and phrase, it is
essentially a stiff, scholastic performance. It
contains many fine lines, even verses, but their
quality is less, poetical than eloquent, sonorous.
Vital emotion is absent, and the thought is undis-
tinguished. The beauty of the best passages is
chiefly beauty of intellectual ingenuity, dependent
upon language rounded by a recitative pen. Con-
sider these, for example:
Linger, oh Sun, for a little, nor close yet this day of a
million ! '
Is there not glory enough in the rose-curtained halls of
the west ?
Hast thou no joy in the passion-hued folds of thy kingly
pavilion ?
Why shouldst thou only pass through it 1 Oh rest thee a
little while, rest!
Why should the Njght come and take it, the (wan Night
that cannot enjoy it,
Bringing pale argent for golden, and changing vermilion
to grey ?
Why should the Night come and shadow it, entering hut
to destroy it ?
Rest ’mid thy ruby-trailed splendours ! Oh stay thee a
little while, stay 1
Rest thee at least a brief hour in it l Tis a right royal
pavilion.
Lo, there are thrones for high dalliance all gloriously
canopied o’er 1
Lo, there are hangings of purple, and hangings of blue
and vermiKon,
And there are fleeces of gold for thy feet on the diapered
floor ! v
Linger, a little while linger. To-morrow thy heart may not
sing to thee!
This shall be yesterday, numbered with memories, folded
away.
Now, should my flesh-fettered soul be set free ! I would
soar to thee, cling to thee,
And be thy rereward Aurora, pursuing the skirts of to-
day !
This is agreeable rhetoric, not flawless ; but the
poetical emotion is slight, and is made slighter by
the rhetoric. The fact is that “Convict Once”
is set in a rhetorician’s metre, well fitted for the
display of “stunning words,” but not well fitted
for the expression of poetical emotion. Even
when a better hexameter is better handled, as in
William Watson’s “ Hymn to the Sea,” it leaves
an impression of the exotic, the artificial; and,
though some of Brunton Stephens’s stanzas are
admirable, they end by becoming monotonous.
Take another example of the piece at its best —
Die then, sad memories, leaving behind you no token nor
relic !
Hark how the tremulous night-wind is passing in joy-laden
sighs.
Soft through my window it comes like the fanning of
pinions angelic,
Whispering to cease from myself, and look out on the in-
finite skies.
Out on the orb-studded night, and the crescent effulgence
of Dian ;
Out on the far-gleaming star-dust that marks where the
angels have trod ;
Out on the gem-pointed cross, and the glittering pomp of
Orion,
Flaming in measureless azure, the coronal jewels of
God ...
Oh, summer-night of the South! Oh, sweet languor of
zephyrs love-sighing !
Oh, mighty circuit of shadowy solitude, holy and still 1
Music scarce audible, echoless harmony joyously dying,
Dying in faint suspirations o’er meadow, and forest, and
hill !
Here there is a little more poetical exaltation;
the writer disengages a little more emotion ; but
the verse is still essentially rhetorical —less the
work of a Poet than that of a Pedagogue “in-
toxicated with the exuberance of his own ver-
bosity.” On the low poetical plane, it is admirable,
commendable; on the high plane, it does not
exist. Occasionally we get the rhetoric, and the
slight emotion, and greater strength of idea—
This is not life, to be bowed in the heart-hush of worship
for ever,
Softly asleep on my shadow to glide o'er a summer-lit
sea.
Life is not passionless calm, but the turbid delight of the
river.
Give me the billowy Jordan ! . . . enough now of blue
Galilee.
Soul cannot march to the bleating of sheep and the low-
ing of cattle.
Rather the war-blast of passion were thrilling again in
mile ears !
Oh for a touch of the palpitant world ! for the glory of
battle !
Show me once more the proud wave of the banners, the
gleam of the spears !
Stanzas like these kernel the best of “ Convict
Once,” and it is chiefly in virtue of these that the
piece remains a considerable performance in Aus-
tralian verse—despite its narrative and dramatic
failure, despite its pedantic stiffness and metrical
monotony.
Brunton Stephens’s, qualifications for poetry are
energy, a little fancy, a little power of thought
npt very new or original—the qualifications of his
Scottish nation. He lacks imagination and
strength of emotion Poetry’s most essential
requisites; he lacks, technically, the finer quali-
ties of Taste ; and the Poet in him is hampered by
the continual obtrusion of the Pedagogue. E.g.
First Claes in Botany stand up (p. 41)—
How I have smiled at my school-bred compatriots lan-
guidly viewing
Ivy-clad relics caducous, and morbidly learned in
decay. . . .
First Class jn Mechanics—What is the meaning
of “centripetal ”?
Even as the shedder of blood ever fleeing the dread scene
of slaughter,
Yet by centripetal charm ever drawn to the spot where
the hand
Points from the shuddering earth. . .
And so on. Instead of emotional fervour, Brun-
ton Stephens gives us intellectual images—that is,
his mental field is essentially in the domain of
Page- references to the Australian edition of “ Convict
Once and Other Poems ’’ (Geo. Robertson, 1S88).
P< r m? e ’ f, 110l 10 ? in the domain of poetry. Even in
-L he Dominion of Australia ” —a good piece, in
spite of stiffness and verbosity—the intellectual,
spholastie metaphor intrudes. After a fine verse,
closing with a fine image—
Already here to hearts intense,
A spirit-force, transcending sense,
In heights unsealed, in deeps unstirred,
Beneath the calm, above the storm,
She waits the incorporating' word
To bid her tremble into form.
Already, like divining-rods, men’s souls
Bend down to where the unseen river rolls ;
Stephens does not rest satisfied until he has
made the image geologically complete by an
accurate reference to artesian water-supply and
its value in drought-time—spoiling his original
impression and overloading his poem. Never in
the realm of Imagination, he is even never wholly
m tlie lower realm of Fancy : he has always one
solid scotch foot on the solid ground of fact,
sometimes he has two : even when his head is in
the clouds he gets rheumatic twinges in his
poetical feet from the earth-damps. Thus in the
verses for “ Adelaide Ironside” he is stiffly ex-
pounding an intellectual thesis. Lines like—
There, too, I led thee to feed thee, with prescience and
keen imitation -
Of the art-adjuvant Grace that hath given thee a love-
gift, to me—
simply schoolmaster’s prose in wooden metre
.hard, pounding, gentlemen,” as Wellington
said at Waterloo. And a sense of humour would
have saved Stephens from this glorification of in-
fantile art, bordering on the ridiculous—
Nothing that came from thy hand, Oh, Love, made void,
cut off, evanescent, —
From the infantile essay that strove with the weapon of
outline alone,
To the Angels thou lovedst to portray with luminous
plumes iridescent,
Till thy soul drew so near unto us that we took thee for
one of our own.
But Brunton Stephens, though witty, has not that
subtle perception of the incongruous which is the
basis of humour. Purely intellectual contrasts
appeal to him always; emotional contrasts, or
intellectual-with-emotional contrasts, rarely or
never. Considered on the intellectual basis, as
clever verse, his work stands high. His verbal
quips are as good as Hood’s ; his verbal ingenuity
as great as Calverley’s, though his rhythms are less
fluid, less flawless than Calverley’s. The punster’s
hand is seen even in “Convict Once,” when he
says of Death (p. 103) —
’Tis but the clasp of the die on the coin, which the mould
must once cover,
Ere it shine forth with the bright superscription and
image of God.
In “New Chum and Old Monarch” the pun is
more fitly placed—
“ Enter, sire ; no longer linger ;
Cease thy signals grandly dumb :
Point not thus with royal finger
To thy hungry vacuum.
“ Though thy pangs are multifarious,
Soon they all shall pass away ;
Come, my begging Belisarius—
Belisorious, I should say.”
Superb; and Brunton Stephens can repeat these
triumphs to our head’s delight. Here is the
predominant Pedagogue at home, scintillat-
ing and splendid. A collection of Brunton
Stephens’s witty verses would well deserve
perpetuity ; and it is by these successes, and
not by his serious, emotional failures, that
he will gain -Whatever perpetuity is allotted
to him. The rhetoric which dazzles the ignorant
will be seen in the light of Time to be usually stiff
and tawdry, poetically barren ; but the verses
“To a Black Gin,” “ A Piccaninny,” and similar
work, will be valued as by far the cleverest work
of the kind done in Australia, and ranking,,
despite some hardness, some surplusage, with .the
similar work of Calverley, Hood, and Praed. The
Darwinian theory has never been more skittishly
hit off than in Stephens’s verses on “The Power
of Science
. . . Through the Museum-window played
The light on fossil, cast, and chart;
And she was there, my Gwendoline,
The mammal of my heart. . . .
She leaned against the Glyptodon ;
She fixed her glasses on her nose ;
One Pallas-foot drawn back displayed
The azure of her hose. . . .
I showed how sense itself began
In senseless gropihgs after sense
(She seemed to find it so herself,
Her gaze was so intense.)
And how the very need of light
Conceived, and visual organs bore ;
Until an optic waftt evolved
The spectacles she wore.
How headless molluscs making head
Against the fashions of their line,
On pulpy maxims turned their backs,
And specialised a spine.
How landward longings seized on fish,
Fretted the type within their eggs,
And in amphibian issue dif-
Ferentiated legs. . . .
I hopped the quaint marsupials,
And into higher mammals ran,
And through a subtle fugue I stole
From Lemurs up to Man. . .
I turned to other, brighter themes,
And glancing at our different scales,
I showed how lady beetles are
Bobuster than the males.
I sang the Hymenoptera ;
How insect-brides are sought and got,
How stridulation of the male
First hinted what was what. . .
And when—perchance too fervently—
I smote upon the chord of sex,
I sawi the tardy spark of love
Blaze up behind her specs. . .
A mighty impulse rattled through
Her well-articulated frame;
And into one delighted ear
She breathed my Christian name. . .
She raised me from the enchanted floor,
And, as my lips her shoulder met,
Between two asthmas of embrace
She called me marmosette.
I strove to calm her down ; she grew
Serener and serener;
And so I won my Gwendoline,
My vertebrate congener.
Lovely! Here is the Pedagogue at school,
triumphant. Apart from verbal unction, the root
of value is the contrast between Romantic and
Prosaic to which Brunton Stephens appeals
always for his best effects. It is a contrast which
heightens the quality of the Pedagogue’s verse,
and often spoils the quality of the Poet’s. Fancy
draws the Pedagogue’s verse up; Fact drags the
Poet’s verse down. And through his literary life,
impelled by the craving for the Romantic
which he could not master, Brunton Stephens
has subordinated the Prosaic which he could
master—for want of tliat perception of incon-
gruity, that nice power of valuation of tones
and shades, which is the essence of critical
judgment of one’s own work and others’. When
the craze for horse-poetry swept over Australia in
the wake of Gordon, Stephens, like Kendall, at-
tempted to please the public with a poem about
horses. Kendall’s result was a grotesque mingling
of lyrical and profane ; Stephens’s energy pro-
duced “ The Godolphin Arabian,”a piece of clever
verse, but less, clever than Byron’s “Don Juan” —
taken as a model; and memorable only as an in-
tellectual tour de force. For the rest, Stephens
has written several plays, of which one,
“Fayette,” has been produced and published.
It has little value as literature ; none for
dramatic performance. Had be chosen, Brunton
Stephens might have written valuable prose, for
his fragmentary work in prose is shrewd, sane,
and brightly expressed ; and he had the persistent
force to execute a high literary or historical con-
ception. But always the rooted Poet in him has
been craving for the light, and always the stony
soil of the Pedagogue has prevented all but a few
stray tendrils from appearing. So the best of his
artistic output is the verso in which his natural
faculty has found free expression, the verse such
as that which is now borrowed to adorn this page.
B.
A PICCANINNY.
Lo by the “ humpy ” door, a smockless Venus !
Unblushing bronze, she shrinks not, having seen
us,
Though there is nought but short couch-grass
between us.
She has no polonaise, no Dolly Varden ;
Yet turns she not away, nor asketh pardon ;
Fact is, she doesn’t care a copper “ farden.”
And yet, her age her reputation spareth ;
At three years old pert Venus little careth,
She puts her hand upon her hip and stareth;
All unabashed, unhaberdashed, unheeding,
No Medicean, charmingly receding,
But quite unconscious of improper breeding.
’Tis well; it smacks of Eden ere came sin in,
Or any rag of consciousness or linen,
Or anything that one could stick a pin in.
Could boundaries, be neater ? posture meeter ?
Could bronze antique or terracotta beat her ?
Saw ever artist any thing completer ?
A shade protuberant, beyond Contesting,
Where this day’s ’possum is just now digesting,
But otherwise, all over interesting ;
Trim without trimming, furbelow, or bow on ;
Was ever sable skin with such a glow on ?
So darkly soft, so softly sleek, and—so on ?
Was ever known so dark, so bright an iris,
Where sleep of light, but never play of fire is—
Where not a soupgon of a wild desire is ?
Oh swarthy statuette ! hast thou no notion
That life is fire and war and wild' commotion ?
A burning bush, a chafed and raging ocean ?
Hast thou no questioning of what’s before thee ?
Of who shall envy thee, or who adore thee ?
Or whose the jealous weapon that shall score
thee ?
Hast thou no faint prevision of disaster—
Of dark abduction from thy lord and master—
Of aliens fleeing, kindred following faster ?
No faint forehearing of the waddies banging,
Of club and heelaman together clanging,
War shouts, and universal boomeranging ?
And thou the bone of all the fierce contention —
The direful spring of broken-nosed dissension
A Helen in the nigger apprehension ?
Nay, my black tulip, I congratulate thee,
Thou canst not guess the troubles that await thee,
Nor carest who shall love or who shall hate thee :
Recking as little of the human passions
As of the very latest Paris fashions,
And soaring not beyond thy daily rations !
Die young, for mercy’s sake ! If thou grow older,
Thou shalt grow lean at calf and sharp at shoulder,
And daily greedier and daily bolder;
A pipe between thy savage grinders thrusting,
For rum and everlasting ’baccy lusting,
And altogether filthy and disgusting ;
Just such another as the dam that bore thee—
That haggard Sycorax now bending o’er thee !
Die young, my sable pippin, I implore thee !
Why sbouldst thou live to know deterioration?
To walk, a spectre of emaciation ?
To grow, like that, all over corrugation ?
A trifle miscellaneous like her, too,
An object not “de luxe” and not “de vertu
A being odious even to refer to ?
Her childhood, too, like thine, was soft and
tender ;
Her womanhood hath nought to recommend her ;
At thirty she is not of any gender.
Oh, dusky foundling, let the warning teach thee !
Through muddiest brain-pulp may the lesson
reach thee.
Oh, die of something fatal, I beseech thee !
While yet thou wear’st the crown of morning
graces,
While yet the touch of dawn upon thy face is—
Back, little nigger, to the night’s embraces !
Hope nought: each year some new defect dis-
closes ;
As sure as o’er thy mouth thy little nose is,
Thy only hope is in metempsychosis.
Who knows but after some few short gradations,
After a brace or so of generations,
We two may have exchanged our hues and
stations?
Methinks I see thee suddenly grow bigger,
White in the face, and stateiy in the figure,
And I a miserable little nigger !
Should this be thus —oh come not moralising !
Approach not thou my humpy poetising !
Spare thine lambics and apostrophising !
Let subtle Nature, if it suit her, black me,
Let vesture lack me, bigger niggers whack me,
Let hunger rack me, let disaster track me,
And anguish hoist me to her highest acme—
Let me bear all thine incidental curses,
Nor share the smallest of thy scanty mercies,
But put me not —oh, put me not in verses !
She grins. She heedeth not advice or warning,
Alike philosophy and triplets scorning.
Adieu, then. Fare thee well. Ta-ta. Good
morning.
ON A FORK OF BYRONS.
Like any other fork.—No mark you meet with
To point S6me psychological conceit with.
An ordinary fork. A fork to eat with.
No individuality of fashion :
No stamp of frenzy fine, or poet-passion :
An article in no respect Parnassian.
No Muse “ with ivy sere ” hath ever decked it:
In fact, it would be foolish to expect it.
I question if the Muses recollect it.
A plain straightforward fork ; yet interesting,
As to the world in general attesting
That poetising hinges on digesting.
A fork not standing on its merits merely,
But, being Byron’s, testifying clearly
That verse and victuals are related nearly.
Quite genuine; crest and all; a fork to swear by:
Some poet-stories doubtless hanging thereby,—
Associations such as gold can ne’er buy.
For ’twixt the fork and the divine afflatus
The links are perfect; there is no hiatus ;
Fork, stomach, brain, pen,—all one apparatus.
So when the food that on the fork ascended
Grew into verse as with the brain it blended,
The fork wrote just as truly as the pen did.
For though the fork the earlier resource is,
Between the two no violent divorce is,—
I hold them to be correlated forces.
Perchance the unsuspected ministration
Of this same fork first set in circulation
The coinage of his rich imagination.
Perchance this very fork could give the clue to
What many of the famous thoughts were due to,
That now are part of me, and part of you too.
And if its prongs administered the fuel
That working duly unto brain-renewal
Kindled the thoughts that even yet fire you all, —
This very fork, —(unless I quite astray be,
And you of unimpressionable clay be), —
A factor in your moral product may be.
And musing thus, does not this fork begin to you
To seem as consanguineously akin to you
As if the bard himself had stuck it into you ?
’Tis ever thus : what Genius consecrateth
The nearest with the most remote collateth,
Till meanest use on highest issue waitetli. r
This salad, now, in which the fork I’m pressing,
Wherein are all sweet savours coalescing—
What subtle shoot of genius efflorescing
Flowered in the flavour of so rich a blessing?
Is it that Byron’s fork, some charm possessing,
Transmutes the—stop a bit.—Who did the dress-

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Forest.
Ye who are dwellers in streets where pain of
existence is sorest,
Come with me, kinsmen of mine, and leave Care
in the city behind —
I am T*™ f her of Trees, made free of the life of
the Forest
Innocent, thrilled with the sun and alive with the
songs of the Wind.
Forth from the city I pass, and I laugh at the
fetters that bound me ;
O but the Forest is green, and my blue-domed
world it is fair,
Delicate bird-life and bee-life piping and humming
around me,
Laughter of light in the leaves, and gladness
of life in the air.
Stately they stand in their ranks, my kinsmen,
the high and the noble,
Princes and chieftains in plumes, and a people in
garments of green ;
They with their valour of heart, and their courage
that laughs at mv trouble,
Knowing the Tree that I was and remembering
what I have been.
Beautiful Lady Acacia, with glimmering laughter
and gladness,
Shaking your head to a tune that is known but to
you and to me ;
Dear immemorial music and dearest green days of
sweet madness,
When you were the Lady Acacia and I was your
Lover the Tree.
Come with me, come with me, kinsmen! and pass
through the wonderful portals:
Deep in the heart of the Forest the mystical story
is told ;
Luminous shadows of gods, they are there, and
the pine-crowned immortals,
All of the Heroes and Stories, and all of the
Legends of old.
N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Night in the Streets.
The thraldom and the wizardry of sleep
Were on the City, and it came to me
That life and thought and spirit in a boundless
deep
Of dim, mysterious Silence lay and breathed un-
easily.
The straight, black pavement underneath my feet
Stretched on and on—it seemed stretched end-
lessly ;
And ever while I walked, as from some alien
street —
Some place where spectres hurried past—foot-
echoes came to me.
The city where I walked is no wise old,
No storied ruins crumble in her ways ;
How came it then that all her houses seemed to
hold
An age-old secret at their hearts—a tale of bygone
days?
How much of us is of the past ? And when
Sleep comes, what shapes of uncouth aeons start
From ancient burial to'try the souls of men—
The while the choked cry may not rise, nor break
the menaced heart ?
The unremembered lives we lived arise,
Their fear or fury chills or fires our blood,
Perchance we mark our red-mouthed foe with
timid eyes,
Or, with blood-hunger at our hearts, howl in a
midnight wood.
The thraldom of the Night—the demon thrall
Sinks earthward to its grave when stars grow wan,
And, faintly to our ears, there comes the Morning
call —
The challenge of the waking world, the cry of
man to man.
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
On the Banks of Vaal.
This is the chant heard by the sombre Vaal,
Sullenly flowing to the brooding sea.
For the dead in the trenches, row on row ;
For the anguish that flickered in glazing eyes
As the fancied echo of loved-ones’ cries
Gave a pang to the last wild dying throe;
For the long, vain hopes ; for the dull, swift blow
That widowed the wife and that orphaned the
child;
For the schemed-for gold, with blood defiled ;
May God in His justice curse you, Foe !
For Grief’s wild tears; for the sobs of Woe
Fierce wrung from a myriad bleeding breasts;
For the flames that flaunted their smoke-crowned
crests
As the homestead crackled and blackened below;
For the wrongs that never redress can know;
For the toll of a butchered nation’s knell;
For the smiling land you turned to hell
May God in His justice curse you, Foe !
Q.

In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
ei, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of\\d. postage, the subscription
to Tun Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease of in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage—lid. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with portage added where required—

SATURDAY, AUGUST 3, 1901.
An Elective Ministry.
It is something like meeting with an old
dead acquaintance to hear that the Elective
Executive Bill has been again introduced
into Maoriland House of Representatives.
It is born dead, as might be expected—in
fact, it seems to be even deader than it has
been in the five or six previous years. Sed-
don is against it, which practically seals its
fate. King Richard of Kumara is so accus-
tomed to be Premier of Maoriland as a
matter of course that the proposal to adopt
the Swiss system of formally electing the
Premier by Parliamentary vote is, in his
eyes, a personal insult, as casting a doubt on
his Gon-given right to the job and salary.
It is practically certain that he would be
elected, but the insult lies in the idea that
he needs formal election. As a matter of
fact, Maoriland does not require the Elective
Executive reform very badly just now. There
are only two parties, or one party and a
dishevelled smattering, in present Maori-
land politics, and ivhere there are only
two parties the old system of accidental
government works in a sort of fashion.
Where there are three, if the three
arc about equal in force and wholly diverse
in politics, it works very badly. Where
there are four it doesn't work at all. For
nearly 30 years South Australia was the
awful example in this continent, but S.A
politics have again consolidated on the two-
party basis, and the old machine is lumber-
ing along respectably once more. New
South Wales is the awful example to-day.
The system which is based on two parties—
one Government and one Opposition—has
gone to wreck. The Parliament which sits
in Sydney has consisted, for the last 10
years, of three parties, and now it has gone
a step further and split into four. And as
there can’t be three Governments and one
Opposition, or two Governments and two
Oppositions, or one Government and three
Oppositions, things are going wrong.
* ' • * *
The capsized political machine is based on
the theory that, say, 55 per cent, of the
House has a reasonable or unreasonable be-
lief that the Premier is the only infallible
man' to run the country, and, say, 45 per
cent, has a similar faith in the other man.
The Premier keeps his billet till, through
his mistakes or their own, enough of his fol-
lowing changes its opinion and transfers its
faith to the other man to place him in a
majority. It is taken so absolutely for
granted that a full majority of the House
must have a complete, boundless, all-round
faith in the Government, that when the
Government fails, on any one question of
importance, to command a majority, it has
to go. It has to be, in the opinion of the
House, incapable of doing wrong—like God,
or the Pope, or a sea-captain; when it
ceases to be so its career is ended. But the
N. S. W. Assembly is now split into four
sections—Government, Opposition, Labor
and Independent. They are not far from
equal, and no leader has the confidence of a
majority. There has been no leader for the
last ten years who really had the confidence
of a majority, which makes it highly advis-
able that the great confidence-trick was
abandoned. It never worked exceedingly
well, even under the two-party system,
which merely signifies that one crowd
says everything the Government does is
right even if it is wrong, while the other
party says that everything the Govern-
ment does is wrong, even when it is right.
It works still worse under the triangular
system, as lately practised in N.S.W., where
a Premier keeps in office because a minority
of the House thinks (probably through
mental incapacity) that he is the best man
for the position ; and another section which
doesn’t pretend to think he is the best man,
agrees to let him remain in office in return
for certain concessions, and to keep him in
till his opponent offers larger concessions.
The only redeeming feature, so far, is that
the third party has confined itself to asking
concessions which it regarded as good for the
country. The advent of a fourth party—or
rather a great, scattered crowd of Indepen-
dents, with no known leader or policy —
suggests that the time may be coming when
a Premier will have to make terms with
people who want concessions for themselves,
rather than for the country.
* ♦ * *
At present, almost anything which John
See proposes will be supported by his own
following, as a matter of course ; it may be
wrong, but it will be supported all the same.
It will be supported by the L ibor-party, if
he buys that support at a price in the shape
of concessions to Democratic principles—and
the country should be able to get such re-
forms as Manhood Suffrage, Old Age Pen-
sions, Early Closing, a State Land Bank, and
the like, without supporting John See’s
ruinous borrowing policy and his other follies
by way of payment. It will presumably be
supported by many Independents, if they
receive sufficient inducement, and what shape
their inducement will take is not yet known,
but if it assumes the old road-and-bridge-
and-culvert shape, the expense will be heavy.
And it will be opposed, openly or covertly,
by the Opposition, as a matter of course,
no matter how good or wise or bene-
ficial it may be. If the Labor - party
struck the greatest and noblest and wisest
idea yet devised in politics John See would
probably adopt it, even though he didn’t
believe in it, on condition the Labour mem-
bers kept him in office. That would be the
price See paid for their support. And the
price which the Labour members would pay
for See’s support would consist, as usual, in
winking at his enormous loans, and his in-
sane finance, and his systematic process of
running the country to ruin. (The Bulle-
tin here assumes that the Labour party has
sufficient conscience not to approve of these
things in its heart.) It would pay this price
because it wanted the votes of See’s follow-
ing to crush the opposition of Lee s follow-
ing. Yet it would be always possible that
so many of Lee’s following approved of the
idea in their hearts that it could be carried
without the Labour party buying anybody’s
votes with the wages of political iniquity—
if only the party system was abolished
and every proposal had a chance to go
on its merits. The Labour party, during
its 10 years existence, has been largely
responsible for many great reforms—but
some of them were not worth having at the
figure they cost. It purchased them by
winking at.follies and iniquities (presumably
it had sense enough to know that they were
follies and iniquities) that even a great re-
form would hardly pay for. It bought the
support of both Reid and Lvne at a price
that the support wasn't always worth—for
the Labor party bought their support quite
as much as they bought its.
* * * •
The most promising remedy seems to be,
as The Bulletin has often said before
(though it has been too busy about other
matters to say it very much of late) in the
adoption of the Swiss system whereby the
Ministry is elected for a fixed term by the
Assembly. Even in an Assembly of four
parties, or 24, some five or six individuals
must get a majority oyer the other five
or six or 97 candidates. The one difference
between that kind of Ministry and the kind
we are accustomed to is that it can get on
just as well without the confidence of a
majority of the House as with it. There-
fore, it is the only kind adapted to a Parlia-
ment where no one leader has the confidence
of a majority. The Ministry thus created
doesn’t need to buy support with concessions,
nor do sections of the House require to buy
concessions with support. If the Ministry
has the confidence of a majority, the House
probably passes its measures as they were
drafted ; if it hasn’t, the House probably
rejects the measures or alters them out of
all resemblance to their former shape.
Either way, the Ministry keeps its place till
its term is up, unless it resigns of its own
free will. In Switzerland it hasn’t even
a vote in the House. Under such a
method the Labor party, or the Indepen-
dent party, might vote to put See and
O’Sullivan in office if it thought they were
the best men, but it couldn’t do so because
they had promised any concessions in return.
A Ministry without a vote and with no
slavish following of its own has very little
power to grant concessions. Nor would See
and O’Sullivan want to buy with concessions
the Labor members’ connivance at their and
their party’s financial iniquities, because they
would have no party as the term is now
understood, and the connivance would do
them no real good in any case. When
an Old Age Pension Bill or some such mea-
sure was introduced under these conditions
there would be no See party supporting it
(possibly against its conscience) in order to
keep See in office, because its defeat wouldn’t
throw See out of office. Nor would there
be any Lee party opposing it (possibly
against its conscience) in order to get See
out of office, because its defeat wouldn’t*
throw See out of office. The Old Age Pen-
sion Bill would pass on its own merits, and
not because the Democratic party had pur-
chased the support of a shady Government
by . agreeing to be conveniently blind to
things that it should be fully aware of, in
order to vote down the hostility of a shady
Opposition which wanted the Democratic
party to purchase it instead—on the same
terms.
* * * *
There are no motions of want of confidence
under this system. The Government hasn’t,
at a crisis, to buy with billets or reckless
loan expenditure the half-dozen members
whose votes will save it from extinction, for
it isn’t affected by any crisis. No majority
condones the awful financial vagaries of a
G. Reid because it can’t condemn them
without throwing him and the Revenue
Tariff party out and putting the Protectionist
leader in ; it could amend Reid’s Estimates
and taxation proposals and everything else
that was his to any extent without throwing
him out. The only serious opening for cor-
ruption is that a prospective Minister may
make lavish and corrupt promises to secure
the votes that will put him into office, but
even this doesn’t amount to very much. He
has no permanent party of his own on whose
votes he can rely to enable him to keep these
promises. The men to whom he made them
can’t coerce him to keep them by threats of
desertion ; it doesn’t matter to him till his
three years expire if he is deserted, and
the member to whom the promises were
made is liable to disappear himself before
then. The system might often make a strong
Minister weaker in a desirable sense, by re-
ducing his power of dispensing patronage and
favors : it would certainly make a weak
Minister stronger by saving him from the
necessity of constantly buying with loan
moneys and grants and jobs and direct or
indirect bribes the half-dozen votes which
stand between him and political extinction.
But its great advantage is that it enables
every question to be considered on its merits
so far as the conscience of Parliament goes,
and the conscience of Parliament, in a com-
munity with the suffrage which New South
Wales enjoys, is as good a conscience as the
community deserves. It altogether elimin-
ates the ulterior considerations that the
rejection of a dubious measure may bring
about the fall of the Government, and that
will bring about other things, or that the
acceptance of a good measure may postpone
the fall of the Government, and “ our ” turn
at the loaves and fishes will be so much the
longer of coming.
Weights and Measures.
A comparatively obscure member of the Federal
Parliament, Edwards of N.S.W., whose plain
horse-sense is apparently as large as the public
knowledge of him is small, has procured a, com-
mittee to consider the advisability of establishing
decimal coinage. This is suggested as a step
towards the introduction of a complete metrical
system of weights, measures, and money in the
Commonwealth, though why the committee
doesn’t deal with the whole subject instead of a
fragment of it, is beyond comprehension. To
most people the metrical system is a mere abstruse
fad —a struggling of school-men after a useless
mathematical accuracy which is of no practical
interest or advantage to the multitude. In the
common opinion, the man who wants to introduce
it ranks with the person who wants to demonstrate
that the world is hat, and the person who believes
in the literal rendering of Revelations, and similar
learned debris of the human species. Yet the
man who succeeds in introducing that system in
Australia will be probably the greatest benefactor
this country has had up to date. He will remove
such a load of useless misery from the shoulders of
the rising generation as no Australian ever re-
moved before—and not only from the shoulders of
the generation that is rising now, but from those
of hundreds of generations which will begin rising
hereafter. He will get rid of a dull and cumbrous
stupidity that has blocked the path of education
for ages, and which has no single real advantage
to show by way of counterbalance to its useless-
ness. The present system of weights and measures
and money has only one alleged positive merit—
its antiquity ; and it has only one negative merit—
that the adult community which makes the laws
has got used to it in a sort of way, and finds it
easier to let things slide than to make them
better.
* ¥ k *
The weights and measures arid nidney which
Ausfralia inherited from Britain are such a sample
of the malice of inanimate objects as could only
exist in a community which had let things grdw
up mostly by themselves fdr 1000 years dr more.
It takes the average child a great part of its
time for from six to 12 months to learn them,
whereas the metrical system can be learned in as
many days. It takes years of wrestling with
arithmetic to become decently familiar with them
even after they have been formally introduced to
the mind. The central principle of the metrical sys-
tem—largely used in France, Italy, Spain, Belgium,
Greece, and other European States, and to some
extent in South and Central America and the
United States—is to have one measure of capacity
instead of about a dozen ; one measure of weight,
and one of length, and in all these tables (except
that of capacity, which goes up by hundreds), as
well as in the matter of money, each coin and
each measure contains one-tenth of the one imme-
diately following it. It practically reduces half
our arithmetic to a matter of adding one or two
naughts or shifting a decimal point. It simplifies
things so enormously that it saves something like
a year of every child’s educational life—a year of
hard labor, and tears, and floggings, and misery
over greasy slates and blotted exercise books.
And it can be remembered in after life by reason
of its simplicity. The British system is an un-
speakable chaos which nobody remembers, ex-
cept by scraps and patches, when once he has
quitted, school. After years of mental strain
in trying to master the gibbering British
arithmetic, how many educated men really know
when the information is wanted that there is a
difference of 800 feet between the length of a mile
at sea and a mile on land, and that one kind of
pound represents a weight of 2240 more grains
than another kind of pound. A system so intri-
cate that it requires enormous labor to learn it,
and so extra-intricate that nobody really remem-
bers it afterwards is a useless cruelty to the young
and a useless recollection to the old.
* * * *
The British arithmetical method is a fearsome
example of legislative non-interference. It grew
up sideways, endways, crooked, mixed and insane,
as the streets of Sydney, and the Chinese mone-
tary system, and the 2000 states of the old Ger-
manic Confederation did for similar reasons. It
is a quaint botch of a thousand local customs
mixed together for about the same number of
years. A British mile at sea is one thing and a
mile on land is another. There are three
separate tables of weights in daily use
(one of them—the avoirdupois—being further
disfigured by the fact that it contains two
weights, each called a stone, and representing
eight and 14 lbs. respectively) ; there are also
about half-a-dozen other tables of weights used in
separate trades. There are various dry
measures and various fluid measures, and
all these measures mount up on no regular
scale or system—two or five or three or eight or 15
or 60 of something make something else. There
is a measure of length in which seven inches and
ninety-two one-hundredths of another inch make
a link, and 5| yards or feet make a rod. Also,
there is a. square measure, in which 30| yards
make a rod. These also mount up by the same
chaotic process—nine of something makes some-
thing else, and 16 of another and 272 J; of a third
make two other things which progress in the same
course later on. The metrical system replaces all
these by three tables—in those of length and
weight everything progresses by tens ; in the table
of cubic measurement everything progresses by
hundreds, and great and intricate commercial
calculations can be made by adding a few
ciphers or shunting a decimal point to port or
starboard. It is a system which enables everyone
to know, as soon as he has learned simple addition,
multiplication, and division, almost all the arith-
metic that any business man requires, and it is
calculated to save a year out of everybody’s life in
the process of education. There will probably be
40,000,000 people in the Commonwealth some day
and 40,000,000 years, or even half-years, have an
appreciable cash value. Many boys could learn a
trade in the time which the metrical system would
save them, and yet know more arithmetic in the
end than they do now. As a matter of fact, the
metrical system is beginning to arrive of itself in
a small way. The American cental of 1001 b. is
familiarly quoted now in place of the ridiculous
British hundredweight, which doesn’t represent
100 of anything —but 112 out of pure cussedness.
And the American ton of 20001 b is also beginning
to be familiar enough to make it necessary to
learn two tons in place of one.
* * *
Of course, if the new idea comes the
cherished British sovereign will have to go. The
best known coins of commerce are the British
sovereign, which represents 20 shillings, each
containing 12 pence worth four farthings apiece
(the farthing, by the way, hasn’t even a symbol to
represent it and is shown by means of a fraction);
the American dollar of 100 cents ; and the French
and Belgian franc of 100 centimes, which is also the
standard, under various names, of Switzerland,
Bulgaria, Finland, Greece, Italy, Romnania,
Servia and Spain. The dollar is probably
the more useful, standard of the two. It
has this additional recommendation that it
is as distinctly a British coin as the sovereign.
The British empire is itself a proof that the
lamentable money table, whereby four of one coin
and twelve of apother and twenty of another make
three other coins, is a cumbrous failure. The
dollar is the standard coin of Canada, Hong
Kong, Singapore, Penang, Malacca, British Hon-
duras, and a few other scraps, comprising among
them nearly half the area of the empire. Also about
three-fourths the populationof the empire uses the
rupee, and' is practically a stranger to the
British gold piece. The British £ won’t fit into a
decimal system anywhere. This is regrettable, for
it is the only coin in the lot which has made an
effort to be sane by containing 20 shillings instead
of 21, like the old guinea ; but its departure is un-
avoidable. If it was retained, and subdi"ided
into tenths and hundredths, it would only mean
that instead of three principal currencies used by
the commercial white man —based on the pound,
the dollar, and the franc—there would be four, and
the existing confusion would become still more
confused. The dollar was the early Australian
currency—the first balance-sheet of the Bank of
New South Wales was expressed in that coin—
and to go back to it is merely reverting to a useful
custom of our early history. If it was adopted
together with the beautifully simple and con-
densed French system of weights and measures,
it would lift a load of superfluous study off the
shoulders of young Australia, and if the time thus
saved was devoted to other useful branches of
learning it would lift this country 10 degrees in
the educational scale, besides making it vastly
more accurate in its accounts all the rest of its
days.
* * * *
Of course it would be something of. a burden on
the 3,000,000 or so of adult Australians to learn a


new system. But still they are only 3,000,000 —
and the unborn Australians, whose education it
would greatly simplify, are thirty, or three hun-
d l l ' e( h or any other number of millions. The
change would also oast obscurity on a certain
amount of British literature—that is to say, Aus-
tralia would have to print its own school-books
and many of its own works of reference in the
future, to the improvement of its printing-trade
and the increase of its self-reliance in literary
matters. Commercially the alteration would pro-
bably be beneficial rather than otherwise. The
greatest stumbling-block in the way of British
commerce nowadays is that it is based on
the most intricate and incomprehensible arith-
metic (with the possible exception of that of
China) on this earth. The Briton’s price-list
(which, by the way, he seldom translates for the
benefit of foreigners) is about as incomprehensible
to the average outsider as a map of the stars.
And the system is almost as incomprehensible to
the Briton himself. Out of the thirty odd million
adults in the United Kingdom there are probably
not a quarter of a million who could set to work
to find out the cost of 12 tons, 3cwt., 2qrs., 27Albs.
at Is. per lb., and get the sum right at the
first attempt, or the seventh either. The British
tables are especially built so that a man of respect-
able education may spend 1-g hours of time, worth
2s. per hour, in getting a sum wrong which,
under the metrical system, a person worth only
s£d. per hour could get right in four minutes.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
More Westralian Slavery.
“ Carisbbook ” (W.A.) writes on the Westralian
native question :
W. Malcolmson (8., 22/6/’01) has a good grasp of the far
Nor’-West native question, and anyone who has lived in
these parts can fully bear out his statements. lam com-
pelled to write under a pen-name, as, should my name
be divulged, I would be served with a notice “ that
my services were no longer required ” for the posi-
tion I occupy. In a series of articles to the Mal-
don (Vic.) News, entitled “ Life in Westralia,” con-
tributed by me in Nov., 1899, I made these state-
ments among others, and I would like to repeat them
now :—“Slavery is existent to a very large extent through-
out the nor’-west part of Westralia; and I doubt if slaves
are so maltreated in any other country in the world. The
slaves are the aboriginals, more familiarly termed ‘ black
boys,’ _ and the slave-owners are the squatters. If an
ahoriginal is 50 years old, he is invariably recognised as a
‘ black boy,’ the same as if his age was only 15 years. The
‘ boys ’ are indentured to the squatters every 12 months,
and woe betide those who refuse to sign their mark on
the agreement drawn up. There are some 1200 of these
unfortunates employed on the various stations, and in
return for their services they receive daily just enough to
exist on. There are a few white men employed on each of
the stations who are known as slave-drivers. They are em-
ployed for no other purpose than to flog the unfortunate
blacks if they refuse to obey the orders of their owners,
or if they are dilatory in their work. Some of the
cruelties perpetrated on the blacks on the out-back
stations are disgraceful to any civilised nation. It
is a common occurrence for one to be flogged to
death or shot on the quiet, and the matter hushed up.
During my time in the Nor’-West I was aware of three
murders that had taken place, but which were all hushed
up. One case was a black boy about 14 years, who was
flogged to death by his master, a teamster. This the
teamster admitted to me. The boy had run away, was
captured, flogged, and died the same day. He was buried
along a road 40 or 50 miles from civilisation, and the
matter hushed up. Another case was an old aboriginal,
who was flogged by one of the white hands on a station
for losing half-a-dozen sheep out of a large flock which he
was shepherding. Two days afterwards the unfortunate
died. This was also hushed up. Another case was on a
station which has lately been attacked by natives.
Through ill-treatment a native ran away, was followed
into the desert, and shot through the thigh with a Win-
chester rifle. He was afterwards finished off with a
revolver shot. This was acknowledged to me by the
person who committed the offence.
“ There is less notice taken of the wilful murder of a
native in those parts than there would be of a dog.
Although dozens of these unfortunates have been mur-
dered during the last few years, yet no white man has
ever yet been hanged, or even found guilty of the charge
of murder, although two or three have been found guilty
of manslaughter, and sentenced to short terms of im-
prisonment. The juries are mostly composed of station-
hands, or other nigger-drivers, and their sympathies are
always with the white men. About 20 niggers have been
hanged during the last few years for offences against the
whites. These executions have been caused through
ill-treated natives turning on their owners and
attempting to spear them. The magistrate who
commits them for trial afterwards acts as Judge,
and pronounces the sentence of death when accused is
brought in guilty. The magistrate being one of the old
Groper sort; is also interested in the squatting stations.
I have seen these slaves sold from one teamster to
another, and it is a very common thing for the manager
of a station to hire them out at so much per week. Yet
we are daily boasting that Britons never shall be slaves,
that wherever the British flag waves its subjects shall be
a free and independent people. I saw a bullock-driver,
in a heated passjon, pull his black boy off a horse, tie his
hands behind his back, his legs together, and use his bul-
lock-whip on him till the blood was streaming from
his back. Another case of cowardly cruelty occurred
while I was on a visit to one of the largest and most
'mportant stations in that part of the State. The clerk
of the station, in my presence, horsewhipped two young
gins for fully ten minutes for a trivial offence they com-
mitted in their ignorance. Many readers might ask the
question—Why do not the aboriginal sympathisers take
their part when they are being treated so cruelly ? The
reason is obvious. The native sympathisers are few, and
it is a case of those few keeping their mouths closed if
they wish to live in that part of the State. The
squatters are the kings, and the Government is behind
them. The tyranny exercised by squatters, who are gener-
ally J’s.P., over native sympathisers makes life not worth
living in that part. The press of Westralia suppresses all
reports of cruelties perpetrated by squatters on their
niggers, unless the case is too glaring, such as the Ander-
son Bros.’ case, some two years ago, when they flogged
four natives to death at Ben Dhu station. One of the
brothers died in gaol while waiting for trial, the other
was found guilty of manslaughter, and sentenced to im-
prisonment for life— i.e., seven years’ imprisonment.
Seven years’ imprisonment for flogging to death two
black boys and two black females ; also flogging next
door to death two little black female mites, aged seven
and nine years respectively ! This is justice in Westralia.
I was shown a young gin’s back, who had been flogged by
one of these same two individuals, and the sores made by
a knotted rope were sickening to look at. If these unfor-
tunates ever run away, they are chased on horseback by
their owner with a revolver, brought back, tied up and
flogged, or taken before a J.P., who is generally another
squatter, and sentenced to three months’ imprisonment
for breach of agreement. They are forced to sign the
agreement, or woe betide them. Their signature is
always a mark, and they are quite ignorant as to the con-
tents of the agreement. The agreement sets forth that
they will obey the instructions of their masters, do any
work when requested, and in return for services they are
to receive rations—a little damper or weevily flour daily.
When a black becomes decrepit and worn-out through
old age, and the squatter can make no further use of him,
he is left to die like a dog. If a station becomes short-
handed, a party will generally go out into the desert,
capture and chain a few, and bring them in. These are
not always civilised, but the lash soon tames them into
civilisation. How much longer this slavery is to continue
God only knows.”

Hanging the Rebels.-
Last week the cable reported a rather extensive
hanging of Cape Colony insurgents, and the
passing of long gaol sentences upon many more.
The hangees were British subjects of Dutch
descent, who had joined their countrymen of the
Transvaal and the Orange Free State—and they
went the way such people naturally do when the
fortune of war goes against them. The rebel has
to take his chance in such oases. Just about 100
years age Britain seized the Dutch territory of the
Cape, and converted the Dutch population into
British subjects against their will. Its claim
upon their fealty is exactly the same as that of
Russia on Poland and Finland ; or the claim that
England iiad on Scotland m the days of Wallace,
or on F ranee in the days just before JOAN OF Akc, or
on Wales in the time of Llewellyn, or in Ireland in
the long dreary ages when Roderic O’Conor, and
Art ARMurrogh and Hugh O’Neil successively
propped upthefalling banner of national independ-
ence. Britain has at the Cape the same right the
Austrians had in Hungary and Italy, or Napo-
leon I. in Germany, and which the Normans had
in England, and which Britain lias, and Russia
hopes to have, in India—the right of the stronger.
There is no other way to build great empires. And
when any degraded rutfian and traitor like William
Wallace, who was hanged 600 years ago; or
Boer farmer Coetzee, who was hanged the other
day; or Kossuth, who would probably have ended
in the same way if Britain hadn’t given him a
shelter ; or Riel, whom the British “executed”
in Canada ; or Here ward the Englishman, whose
resistance to his lawful Norman annexer was
bitter and reprehensible; or Joan of Arc, or
Boadicea, or Washington, or Kolokotrones
rises against his (or her) rightful sovereign-by-
annexation, he (or she) calls for no consideration
from any right thinking individual. Some day it
would be worth while to. write a history of the
British empire-breakers, with denunciatory foot-
notes. Henry 11. laid the foundations,
by inheritance and conquest, of a great
British empire covering England, Scotland,
Ireland, Wales, and most of France, and
Edward I and his successors toiled to con-
solidate it. Philip Augustus captured the French
section of it. Those two base rebels against the
British empire, Wallace and Bruce, broke it
in two by severing Scotland ; Wallace was
hanged (just like Boer farmer Coetzee, the
other day), and Bruce only escaped execu-
tion by succeeding. Possibly he got it hot
in the next world to make up. Art McMur-
ROGH and others shattered the imperial power
in Ireland, and it wasn’t till 200 years
later that the real annexation followed. Du
Guesclin broke the second French empire, and
Joan of Arc the third. Washington broke the
empire again, and the conduct of that seditious
person, who somehow escaped his well-deserved
fate, calls for severe condemnation. Riel tried
to be a Washington in Canada, and died of the
attempt. And a handful of obscure Cape Dutch-
men, who had somehow never become reconciled
to the 100-year-agone conquest of their country,
took up their rifles last year and went out on the
well-trodden path of all these and other degraded
and reprehensible characters—of William Wal-
lace, Robert Bruce, George Washington,
Kosciusko (“and Freedom shrieked when
Kosciusko fell ”), of Joan of Arc, Kolokotrones,
Kossuth, of the Scotch Covenanters, of theTyrolese
hero Andrew Hofer, of the German rebels who
rose against Napoleon I, of the Spaniards whom
Wellington assisted in their great fight against
the same annexing potentate, of Herewakd the
Saxon, and of the Irishmen of ’93. They had
better luck than Joan of Arc, who was burned
alive by the English ; or-the men of ’93, who were
variously hanged, flogged, or scalped with that
weird contrivance, the pitch-cap ; or the captured
Covenanters who were often tortured before exe-
cution ; and about, as good luck as Wallace, who
was hanged by the English ; and not quite so
good as Kosciusko, the Pole, for though Freedom
shrieked when he fell, his captors doctored his
wounds and soon set him at liberty. Things like
these are necessary incidents in the building of
empires, and it is absurd to talk of them in any
unfriendly spirit. Still, it is shocking to the
mind of any right-thinking person, at this time of
general enthusiasm about Imperial unity, to learn
that a statue has been, or is going to be, erected in
the loyal city of Dunedin to William Wallace,
the rebel who did his best to break the first British
empire; and to hear disloyal Scotchmen holding
forth with enthusiasm about Robert Bruce, who
really did breakit —who broke it twenty times worse
than Paul Kruger ever attempted to do. Also,
the drinking of the toast of “ Washington ” on the
4th of July should be made a heavily-punishable
offence. These people may be dead, but they
represent exactly the principle for which Boer
farmer Coetzee was hanged last week, and it is a
principle that can’t be put down too low or hanged
up too high.

Philp’s Idea.
Queensland Parliament met the other day with
the spectre of the Half-Million Deficit in the
chair, and the first anxiety of the House was
naturally to know what Premier Philp was going
to do about it. A country with a revenue of less
than £4,100,000 and an expenditure of well over
£4,600,000 is in a bad way, and something cer-
tainly needs to be done. Apparently Premier
Philp doesn’t intend to do anything. He com-
menced by saying that he had every confidence
that “Queensland would soon see prosperity
again”—in other words he stuck to the good old
fiction that the tremendous boom lately created
with borrowed money was just the country's ordi-
nary state, and that the present condition was
one of unnatural and temporary depression. To
any rational individual, who wasn’t sodden with
the borrowing disease, it might rather appear that
Queensland is still in an inflated state through
borrowing and booming, and when it gets into an
ordinary state of prosperity the revenue—with
the taxes as at present —will be less than it
is now, rather than more. For the financial year
just closed —the one with the awful deficit: —
Queensland had, per inhabitant, about the largest
revenue in AustralasiaI—barring 1 —barring only Westralia,
which is in the midst of a still worse borrowing
boom. And yet Queensland doesn’t levy land and
income taxes, as the Southern States do. TJnder
these conditions the fair inference is that, if
Queensland only borrowed as much in proportion
to population as the other States, its revenue, in
times of ordinary prosperity, would be less than
it is now, unless extra taxes were imposed. Philp
is of opinion that if all the Syndicate Railway Bills
had been passed last session things would have
been better. In other words, Queensland has
already more railway in proportion to population
than almost any other place on earth ; and if still
more railways had been built the boom might
have been kept going at full speed a little longer.
Meanwhile Philp’s only idea is to increase land
sales. Theyare alreadygoingon at the rate of about
£200,000 a year. So they will have to be increased
to £700,000 or £300,000 to fill the gap, even if the
gap grows no bigger. And, on top of all, Philp
talks wildly of selling enough land to pay off the
public debt of about £36,000,000. Queensland has
about 400,000,000 acres still unsold, but when all the
desert areas are deducted this doesn’t amount to
quite as much as it looks. Still if Queensland de-
cided to pay off its liabilities, and to have no more
public debt, and held a great and successful clear-
ing sale of land for that purpose, it would save
£1,300,000 a year in interest. That is a big item.
On the other hand, it would lose most of its present
land revenue (used to cover ordinary expenditure)
of £600,000 a year, for the land from which it is
derived would "have gone in payment of the debt.
It would no longer have £900,000 or £1,000,000 of
borrowed money coming in. And it has
still to face the fact that its income is
£500,000 short of expenditure. Apparently if
Queensland cleared off its present debt by land
sales, and didn’t start any new debt, it would
want about £700,000 or £300,000 a year more
taxation, on the present system, to keep it out of
the poor-house. And even if it started to contract
a new permanent debt at the same rate as it is now
contracting the old one, it would only have a
surplus of £200,000 a year, and the interest on the
new debt would be big enough in six or seven
years to eat that up. The fact is that Queensland
has a huge public estate, but all of it that is good
enough to attract tenants is leased and bringing in
rent. In many cases it brings in very little rent,
but that is chiefly because it isn’t worth any
more ; certainly the rents aren’t sufficiently below
value to allow either the squatter or the Q.N. Bank
to make a fortune just now. And if the land is sold
wholesale to pay off the public debt the revenue
from rent naturally diminishes. The thing may
not be as broad as it is long, but it isn’t so far from
it as might be supposed. And unless better
seasons come—well, how much would all Queens-
land west of Longreach fetch in one lot !

Work for the Refuse-Shifter.
In January, 1900, N.S.W. Public Service Board
noticed a considerable smell arising from the
Health Office and the State Hospital at Little Bay.
The Board’s attention was called thereto by one
Glynn; probationary-assistant-storekeeper at the
Coast Hospital. Glynn was booted out of his
billet because his immediate superior, Willman, re-
ported against the confirmation of his appointment.
Glynn had been set to work there on the under-
standing that if he did his work well he would be
permanently employed. He placed the Board in
possession of facts which led to the discovery of
abuses which the matron swore had been going on
for seven years. On May 13, 1901 (letter M.
01/5414, paragraph 3), the Board reported :
. . . he (Glynn) had undoubtedly done good service to
the State, in that he was instrumental in exposing the
existence of a serious state of affairs at the Coast Hospi-
tal, and it was in view of this fact that the Board recom-
mended that he be paid salary for six months.
Furthermore, the Board propose to give Glynn other
employment when a position for which he is suitable
becomes vacant.
It took the Board 16 months to find a position for
which he was suitable, and then it was only a
temporary one, and worth about £25 per annum
less than Glynn’s original position at the Coast
Hospital—the one from which he was ejected for
“ having done good service to the State.” Then
the Board got to work on the men who had failed
to prevent the abuses through exposing which
Glynn was sacked, and after several months’
inquiring and investigation it submitted a report,
bits from the summary of which read like this: —
. . . The late contractor, Mr. Kidman, appeared to have
done pretty much as he liked. It was shown that time after
time the Medical Supt. reported that the supplies received
from Mr. Kidman were unsatisfactory, and that goods had
frequently to be rejected. Notwithstanding that the
terms of the contract provided stringent conditions by
fine, and, if necessary, cancellation, to meet such a con-
dition of affairs, nothing appears to have been done. In
justice to Dr. Ashburton Thompson the Board pointed out
that many of the complaints were not brought under his
notice. With regard to fish supplied to the Coast Hospital
the evidence showed that the Supplies by the contractor
were often very inferior, sometimes putrid and quite unfit
for food. On such occasions the hospital obtained what
was required from Oiiinnery, of Sydney, whose price was
4d. per lb., but Kidsian was allowed to submit a voucher
including the fish supplied by Oiiinnery and to charge Is.
per lb., his contract rate. In other words, Kidman re-
ceived back his rejected fish, and after the hospital
authorities had, by his action, been put to the trouble
and expense of sending to Sydney to obtain proper
supplies, he was paid Bd. for every lb. of fish so obtained.
For Pommery champagne Kidman was paid 9s. per pint—
the usual retail price being ss. or 6s. Mr. A. Kidman has
been largely overpaid on account of fish, fruit, rolled oats,
etc. The sum of at least £155 12s. (in what period not
stated) should be put down to that account. The Board
have come to the conclusion that Messrs. Willman and
Gullick have forfeited all right to the confidence of the
Govt. They have both shown that their word is not to
be trusted, and they have failed signally to properly safe-
guard the interests of the Dept. Both officers should be
called upon to show cause why their services should not
be dispensed with.
Accordingly, the Board appointed Dr. Norton
Manning, late Inspector-General for the Insane,
to hear the case. Gullick and Willman were
represented by counsel, and the Board were re-
presented by one Brodie, who had previously
made a fearful and wonderful investigation into
affairs at the Coast Hospital. Gullick was
charged with perfunctory examination of accounts
and vouchers, resulting in loss to the Govt.; unduly
favoring a late contractor to the Coast Hospital
and Quarantine Station, North Head; giving
evasive evidence, and general indifference to the
best interests of his department. And Dr.
Norton Manning decided that the perfunctory
examination of vouchers was proved as far as it
related to certain articles not in contract, but it
did not appear to be proved that Gullick had
favored the late contractor. Gullick and
the late contractor, Arthur Kidman, had
been school-fellows, and it was shown
that Gullick, while Kidman was a contractor,
had unwisely accepted some minor favors at his
hands ; but there was no imputation of anything
in the shape of bribery or venality in Gullick’s
dealings with the contractor, and it appeared that
Kidman’s accounts, which had been passed with
over-charges, had been so passed by reason of
carelessness or laxity, and not by design, or with
any idea of favoring the contractor. It was also
decided that Gullick did not appear to have
given dishonest evidence, although it was found
that his evidence was undoubtedly evasive and
unsatisfactory; and the court did not consider
that the charge of general indifference was fully
sustained. Willman was charged with perfunc-
tory examination of vouchers for supplies to the
Coast Hospital, Little Bay, leading to loss to the
Govt. : unduly favoring a late contractor for sup-
plies to the Coast Hospital, and obstructing the
late temporary storekeeper; giving dishonest
evidence, and general indifference to the best
interests of the Govt., and the decision was this ;
Charge 1: As it stands, not proved, but it is clearly '
shown that Mr. Willman’s action in regard to certain
supplies and the charges made therefor resulted in a loss
to the Govt.
Charge 2 : Not considered proved.
Charges: Practically admitted, but it is pointed out
that, except in this one matter, no exception appears to
have been taken as to the character and straightforward-
ness of Mr. Willman’s testimony.
Charge 4 : Not fully substantiated, although there are
matters, particularly in connection with the “ diet lists”
mentioned in the report, as to which Mr. Willman did
not display the interest and care which he should have
done.
Then it was announced that Willman and Gul-
lick are to be transferred to other departments
and suffer a reduction of £25 per annum each.
Willman, while at the Coast Hospital, was rated
as £250 per annum, but he was charged £3O per
year for rent, so his actual cash receipts were
£220. He is now to get £225. And Glynn, the
man who did good service by revealing the shame-
ful mismanagement of the institution also loses
£25 a year, in addition to losing his entire salary
for many months. The reward of good service in
N.S.W. is evidently a vast amount worse than
the reward of bad service.

Madden and Peacock.
“Vindex” writing to The Bulletin clearly states
the position :
Editor Bulletin. —Is the Victorian law, is the Victorian
executive, not sufficient for Victoria? Is the British
Government to perform executive functions in and for
Victoria? Judging from the manner in which the corres-
pondence between Madden and Chamberlain has been
treated in Vic. Assembly there seems to he no one left
there possessing sufficient knowledge of our constitutional
rights, and sufficient manliness, to bring Madden and
Peacock to task for their extraordinary conduct. Madden,
Lieutenant-Governor, sees in his morning’s Argus, in
heavy leaded type, the words “ Foul libel on the King,”
and reads that the little Tocsin has republished the
article which appeared in the Irish Peodle. Now is his
chance—the chance of a lifetime—to show servility to the
Colonial Office, to show contempt for “ that humbug,
Responsible Government—to show that he is the good
little boy, that he does not hold the principles for which
that grand old lion, Higinbotham, fought—the principles
which kept Higinbotham from being appointed lieutenant-
governor, but which have tended to keep our people free
from the meddling and muddling of the Colonial Office.
Madden —without seeing the condemnatory remarks with
which the Tocsin introduced the Irish article—summons
his Ministers to his awful presence, strokes his fieroel.v-
waxed moustache, advises them to advise him to seize all
that is left of the Tocsin —that ill-disposed paper, as His
Ex. condescends to call it—to seize it, not under any
Victorian law, but as agent for the British Ministers, if
the British Ministers will adopt his act as an “ Act of
State.” The Victorian law, forsooth, is not sufficient, in
his opinion, to deal with seditious libels ; therefore he
asks (not an amendment of the Victorian law, but) that
an outside authority, the British Ministry, shall act, and
act through him, under the mystic force of the cabalistic
phrase—“ an Act of State ” 1 Peacock, on the advice of
Madden, advises Madden to send the silly message to
Chamberlain. But Chamberlain, whatever he is, is not
a fool; and he has at his elbow one or two men
who know something of constitutional law, and of the
relations of the British executive to the Colonies.
He replies—“Defence of Act of State nob available
against British subject.” Of course, it is nob. We know
nothing of a droit administratif in our system, thank
goodness. Every officer of the King must keep within
the law; and, in a State enjoying Responsible Govern-
ment, he must keep within the law of the State. A
well-deserved snub to a Lieutenant-Governor who, as
Chief Justice, ought to have known better.
After reading this sickening despatch of the Lieutenant-
Governor, it is as good as a tonic to read the
historic letter written by George Higinbotham to Sir
Henry Holland (now Lord Knutsford) in 1887: “ You
will observe that I address you in j our private capacity
as an English politician interested in colonial affairs, and
not in your character of her Majesty’s Secretary of State
for the Colonies Her Majesty’s Ministers for
Victoria possess the power, and on them alone devolves
the supreme and the hitherto neglected duty of vindicat-
ing public law in the colony, and of protecting the ex-
alted office of the Queen’s representative from the indig-
nity of illegal invasion The creation by statute
of the system of Responsible Government necessarily in-
volves the vesting in the representative of the Crown of
all,powers and prerogatives of the Crown necessarj' in the
conduct of local affairs and the administration of the
law.” Higinbotham may have carried his antagonism to
the Colonial Office to an extreme. tjut he saw more
clearly than others that,.Australians must be treated as
men, not as schoolboy's, much less as whipped spaniels ;
and, therefore, his reputation
will gather strength of life with being,
And live hereafter more admired than now.
In the meantime, we are suffering from Madden and
Peacock. When will Australians learn to distinguish
true metal from tinsel—to admire public spirit and un-
selfishness more than smug self-complacency—to appre-
ciate simplicity of manner rather than garish gawds and
vulgar decorations ? Peacock —well, he is leader of the
so-called Liberal party. By tradition, that party is the
custodian of the rights of the people against the unlawful
aggressions of the Crown or the Government. But who
is to watch the guardians themselves ? When the leader
of the Liberal party does not appreciate this noble tradi-
tion, expels an alleged criminal from Parliament before
he has been tried for the alleged crime, and invokes the
aid of the British Executive to maintain law in the
State, it is time for the people to wake up.

Vic. Laborites talk of testing the legality of
Findley’s expulsion. They may as well save
their money. The question was tested in ’66. In
March of that year, on the rejection of the Appro-
priation Bill by the Upper House, the M'Cul-
loch Ministry resigned. Next day the Argus
observed that Mr. M'Culloch’s statement on
resigning fairly bristled with falsehoods.” For
this publisher Hugh George was sent to the
dungeon beneath Parliament Hous§. The Argus
raved and swore at large (also at Attorney-
General Higinbotham), and finally appealed to
the Privy Council, giving barrister K. 1). Ireland
a 1000-guinea fee to go and argue the case. The
Council decided that Vic. Assembly possessed
all the powers of the House of Commons in the
way of doing as it pleased with any subject
brought before it. That seems about comprehen-
sive enough to cover anything, and it isn’t good to
waste the money of the masses, made by hard
bootmaking and the like, in feeing their natural
enemy, the lawyer.
