The truth about Barnum-Powell and the bogus
siege of Mafeking ■ has at last reached England.
Sydney Telegraph, which gave a page of pic-
tures and letterpress to “ The Belief,” and mean-
dered down history past Gibraltar and Plevna
vainly looking for parallels to “ this tremendous
achievement,” now reprints from London
Morning Leader a returned M.L. trooper’s
account. Says this uncompromising warrior,
“referring to the fact that B.P. has not been
greatly rewarded for his services ” :
The truth is that the military authorities don’t think
much of Baden-Powell or his work. The whole of the
Mafeking business was a “ frost.” I was for four months
in the neighborhood with Plumer’s force, and Plumerwas
so anxious about the condition of the besieged, he was so
certain that they’ were starving, that he economised his
own provisions, and for months before the relief we were
on a biscuit a day . and half-rations. When Mafeking was
relieved it turned out that Baden-Powell had full provi-
sions for a year, and there had been no want. Plumer
was awfully mad. He sent a few waggons of food and
stores into the town just for appearance sake, and to
make a show for the paper men ; but he was awfully
wild. Out there we didn’t understand why Mafeking was
made so much of by London. Baden-Powell was no par-
ticular hero with the troops, and those behind the scenes
knew that there was little or nothing in the Mafeking
business. But he got his men into muddles once or twice
in the open as well; so there is not much difficulty in
understanding how London’s idol had no particular
rewards.
The M.L. trooper had more to say about the
famous London volunteers whom the Stock Ex-
change crocodiles wept over, and in whose honor
some scores of people were killed and injured on
the day of their drunken return —
On the subject of Tommies in general, our Maori-
lander was fairly flattering— i.e., he didn’t show up their
shortcomings as fighters, but paid a tribute to their
behaviour, which was wonderful, “when you think where
they are recruited.” Concerning the C.l.V.’s, the
Maorilander said a good deal that was very nasty. Boiled
down, his remarks came to this : The C.l.V.’s, weren’t a
ha’porth of good, and their home-coming was hurried up
because of their uselessness.
These things confirm The Bulletin’s oft-repeated
declaration that the cables disseminated by Jingo
agents in Africa, trimmed by military censors to
suit British tactics, and circulated by the Aus-
tralian press, are simply a mass of lies, either
direct or by suppression. Yet they are still swal-
lowed as fact by thousands of gullible Australians!


SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Royal “ Mrs. Dane’s Defence.”
Lyceum....; “ King of Crime.”
Criterion “ Dangerous Women.”
Her Majesty’s “Aida.”
Empire Fuller Co.
Tivoli Rickards Co.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “ Why Smith Left Home.”
Her Majesty’s “ Man and his Makers."
Theatre Royal “ The White Heather.”
New Opera House Rickards Variety Co.
“The King of Crime,” which opened on
Saturday night at Sydney Lyceum, comes to us
with a certain halo through the recollection of
Albert Marsh’s fine rendering of the character
some few years ago, and it still remains an excel-
lent melodrama at Anderson’s theatre in spite of
the actors and actresses occasionally getting in
the road. The “King” is an interesting man,
who has a craving to be a respectable citizen and
the head of a robber gang at one and the same
time. Hs inclination to be a secret assassin he
explains rather vaguely, by the statement that in
“ every man there is the instinct to kill,” and he
also remarks that he had no woman friend to tell
him not to. a man gets into the paths
of wickedness he generally contrives to put it all
on the woman that was there, and when she
wasn’t there he puts it on her for not being there.
The play starts when the hero is beginning to get
his two lives tangled up, so that he has to make
himself several other people in a hurry, in the
hope of smoothing things out. He ends by en-
gaging himself as a detective to hunt himself
down, in which character a traitor member of his
own gang gives him valuable information as to
his own whereabouts. There is also in this play
a much-travelled will, which sometimes brings
peace by being waved from the top of a tree, and
on other occasions binds up the broken heart by
being rescued j ust in time from the flames. There
is also a clock which behaves humorously, and mid-
night chimes which have a tragic effect. Besides
which, several women declare that nothing shall
part them from their husbands, when they immedi-
ately are parted without any difficulty whatever. A
considerable amount of shriek and sudden gloom
and stealthy forms groping about with dark
lanterns pervade the remainder of the drama, the
whole ending with a large pair of hands appearing
from behind a curtain and dragging the victim to
be choked at leisure behind the scenes and cast
back before the footlights when the business is
completed.
* * • *
Robert Inman, as the homicidal hero,
loses some telling points, notably where he counts
out members of the gang and lets them go till the
last one, the traitor, is left to be dealt with alone.
The scene ought to be as impressive as a funeral,
and consist of a considerable amount of silence
and shudder. Inman, however, calls his men
like a teacher going through the roll, and they
scamper out like schoolboys, there being no
impressiveness whatever until the hero disappears
cleverly into nothingness through the wall. There
is some good work by some of the subsidiary
characters, though Dora Mostyn does not suit
short skirts, and has more Australian cheek than
the necessary French “chic” in her manner.
Bert Bailey appears as a would-be son-in-law who
mesmerises pa into smiling on his suit, though the
mesmeric smile of pa is something to dream of,
not to tell. This is a good French touch. In
England, where girls have no dowries, it is some-
times the young man who has to be mesmerised
by pa into keeping on with his suit.
#« * *
The “ Dangerous Women ” continue to be
alarming in an aimless way at the Criterion
Theatre, Sydney. In this illogical literary
effort the nearness of the heroine to a lunatic
asylum appears, to the author’s judgment, to make
it quite feasible that anyone who has a down on
her should be able to put her in at once.
Several people who are wandering about the pre-
cincts that day are also brought inside, the idea
being that any person indulging in mild eccentri-
cities round an establishment of this sort, can be
collected and put in a cell without any preliminaries
whatever. Miss Roland Watts Phillips as a news-
paper boy is an excellent boy—not a stage-boy at
all, but the sort you could meet in Pitt-street,
and buy a “pyper” from any afternoon without sus-
picion. A good many shows are announcing their
more or less regrettable end this week, but
“ Dangerous Women ” isn’t one of them. It will
rage in its haziness and its public administration
of chloroform till further notice.
** * *
The Italian Opera season at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, starts with “Aida” for two sufficient
reasons. Firstly, it is a great spectacle, and the
dramatic action of the opera commends it to the
understanding of a public whose ear is unaccus-
tomed to the sound of Italian words. Secondly,
it introduces the biggest roof-raising voices which
are entitled, by law of etiquette, to get first hear-
ing in each city the company may visit. The two
declamatory principals, Signora Benedetto and
tenor Larizza, take precedence of Signora
Sapio and Dani, whose singing is just a
little more sweet and less powerful. The two
lighter sopranos, Dalia Bassich and the sprightly
Vaccari, don’t appear in any of the first
two or three operas. From a managerial
point of view there is only one objection to
“ Aida” as the pioneer opera of the season. The
brown face and short skirts of the name part
make it very hard for a plump little prima-donna
like Benedetto, to impress a new audience with
her singing and acting. She has to produce her
top note under the worst of conditions for herself
personally, and if she raises herself on her toes as
she lifts that mighty note it only makes the short
skirts look still shorter. Meanwhile it may be
mentioned in an irrelevant sort of way that the
“ Casino Girl ” will be still visible to-night
(Thursday) and Friday.
Robert Brough produces “Mrs. Dane’s
Defence ” at Sydney Royal on Saturday. As it
is the play’s first appearance in Australia this
country is still unaware of the nature of the
charge brought against the lady, but certain
London papers speak highly of the piece, and the
fact that it was written by Henry Arthur Jones
is a guarantee that it won’t be the worst kind of a
failure. Also, if Brough appears for the defence it
may be fairly assumed that Mrs. D. gets every
possible chance to clear her character, if she has
one to clear. Between Brough and Jones it
ought to be possible to get even J. Iscariot off on
a technical point, or at least to hire somebody to
swear an alibi. The “Second in Command”
finishes its over-long' career on Friday. Now
that the small, hesitating Duke of York
is off the premises it may be suggested
with comparative safety that the huge, breezy, and
very decided Duke of Hull, who comes, on behalf
of his father, the King, to decorate Major Bing-
ham, didn’t help the “ Second in Command.”
When a large section of the public had just been
disappointed in a small duke it didn’t want to
have the thing rubbed in by being rushed at by a
big duke. And the small section which was satis-
fied with the real duke probably resented the in-
ference that Brough knew better what size an
heir apparent ought to be than the heir apparent
himself. Brough ought to have remembered that
an heir apparent is always the right size. His
size becomes the right size through him being it.
We commence to suspect Brough of being a pro-
Boer.
*# * *
Brough’s other show, conjuror Bertram,
ends his career at Sydney Palace on Friday. The
final verdict is that he is an exceedingly clever
conjuror—as clever as any this country has seen—
but that most of his illusions have been seen here
before. The populace is no nearer finding out
how they are done than it was years ago, but that
isn’t the question. If the populace had been
around ip Genesis, when animals were being
created, it would probably have got no nearer
learning how to create new animals itself, but, all
the same, by the time the 75th animal was
created, it would have lost all interest in
the proceedings. If someone could invent a
number of perfectly new tricks, even though they
might be less clever than the old tricks, it would
give Bertram’s line of business a great lift.
mm m m
The Leslie Brothers are the latest addition
to the bill at Sydney Empire, and their musical
burlesques are always good for several encores.
The Leslies, somehow, never seem to catch cold,
as everybody else does.
** * *
At the Tivoli, Madame Lucretia, lately
from London, dances “speciality” dances, wears
nice frocks, and sings the latest Old World songs.
Whelan, the mimic, is in his last week, and will
leave shortly for Fogtown, where much affluence
should be available for a man of his talent.
** * *
The Adelaide public gave a moderate sup-
Eort to Rignold in melodrama at the Royal, but
as rushed Hawtrey in the “Message from Mars.”
Meanwhile the Tivoli, having got over its birth-
day, is going steadily on with a strong bill, in-
cluding Latona the Tramp, Austin Rudd and
Lizzie Kirk.
** « •
This is a revolutionary week at Melbourne
theatres. The Italian Opera Co., which took pos-
session of Her Majesty’s on June 1, passed out
with the winter on July 31, unless something
happened to break the word of the advt. Last
Saturday added a successful “ Rigoletto ” to
the other good results of this foreign invasion,
and brought the season’s record up to seven operas
—counting “ Pagliaeci ” and “ Cavalleria” as one
show. A mixed bill was to play the Italians off
the premises on Wednesday. They return in
December, by which time Melbourne will pro-
bably have developed a fuller appreciation of the
fine entertainment that Hazon engineers.
** * *
Wilson Barrett makes his re-appearance
in Australia at Her Majesty’s aforesaid next
Saturday (August 3rd) with the intention of
staging eight or nine plays in eight weeks. The
people W.B. brings with him this time are mostly
the same as before, also several of his former pro-
grammes will be repeated, and the performances,
new or old, will have the polish of experience on
them, anyhow. Season opens with “Man and
his Makers,” a novelty to these regions, and
Wilson Barrett will appear as principal Man in
the play he helped to make.
** * *
At the Princess’s a satisfactory explana-
tion of “ Why Smith Left Home ” is to be offered
to Melbourne for the first time on Saturday next
(3rd). The city has grown thirteen months older
since Charles Arnold and his comedy co. went
away to roam Australasia, but the memory of
“Jones” has been kept green by tales of his
prowess on tour. Moreover a favorable report
upon the Smith who forsook his domicile has been
in the air of the temporary capital for some time.
Consequently the arrival of Arnold and co. and
this alleged joke|is looked forward to with peculiar
satisfaction by the frivolous, who are a great and
growing majority thereabouts. One hears the
typical giddy girl in the tram-car referring to
“Why Smith Left Home” as though she hopes
he was actuated by improper motives.
** * *
The Yic. Operatic Society is keeping the
Princess’s warm for a second week prior to the
Arnold advent. On Saturday last it reverted
to “ Les Cloches,” and did it pretty well con-
sidering the present lack of bone and beauty in
the amateur chorus. As a recruiting-ground for
Williamson and Musgrove the V.O.S. is just now
in a somewhat exhausted state, and the display of
limbs feminihe would call for condemnation all
round if Miss Maud Thornton hadn’t two redeem-
ing circumstances to stand upon. George Leopold,
who is always a sound, substantial miser, in “ Les
Cloches,” was the mainstay of Saturday’s show.
Mias Ray Jones made a neat little Germaine,
but her singing voice of late doesn’t amount to
much more than a clear enunciation of the words.
Miss Ida Osborne, as Serpolette, showed the
effects of influenza, or something, in her warble.
The amateur principals were J. Goudy and
M'Nichol Turner, who have been there before,
and Bonynge Kelly, a tall, slim young man of the
Family Herald type, who had not been there
before, but who sang pleasingly and behaved like
an intelligent Bonynge. It is hard upon an un-
ripe actor to be distinguished by such a fruity
name. Also, it is a terrible thing for James
Wade, an artistic tenor, to be cast for the part of
Grenicheux, who is incidentally referred to as an
Apollo among shrimp-catchers. Wade, in abbre-
viated knickers, only needs a pair of huge panto-
mime feet to make him funnier than the little
man who used to bathe in a small fish-pond at
Rickards’s.
** * m
Dampier is closing his chapter of horrors
at Melb. Bijou this week. On Saturday the bill
was “The Penalty of Crime,” an exceedingly
awful melodrama wherein the principal villain
went around chloroforming old men and maidens.
The brightest spot in the trouble was Master
Dampier, as a dishevelled youth named Jo.
Dampier Junior, at the early age of 15, promises
be an engaging young actor anon. To-night
(Thursday) and to-morrow “ A Convict Martyr ”
will bear his unjust sentence with resignation,
and after that the Bijou will take a rest for three
weeks.
** * *
Bland Holt, who never forgets to move
with, the times, is putting up another drama at
the Royal next Saturday. “ The White Heather,”
which is about the smartest of all the big shows
Bland has ever staged, will appeal to the public
on that occasion.
#* * *
At the Opera House (where clever Marie
Lloyd and the unversatile Hurley will cease to
appear on August 8), a remarkably clever young
man, named Zobedie, is walking with his feet in
air. The same young man was a wondrous feature
of Wirth’s Circus a while ago, and his talent for
reversing the order of things in locomotion is now
more marked than ever. Another triumph of
youthful perseverance is Arnoldi, the juggler,
who performs some of Cinquevalli’s tricks very
neatly, and is doubtless superior to some of the
“ old man’s ” many imitators. But the difference
between Arnoldi and the strong, graceful, agile
juggler of yore is shown in the things that are
done as well as in the things that are left undone
by the newcomer.
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
“ J.M.” (Kalgoorlie) : We said the office would
pay you whatever was due under your policy, it having
taken over the institution which issued the policy. Did
it not do this ? You must remember that you insured in
an institution with a declining business and a heavy
expense-rate, and which, in 1896, wrote off as lost, or set
aside to make provision for possible future losses, about
one-ninth of its total funds. The office which afterwards
took the concern over certainly didn’t undertake to make
good these losses, and you couldn’t expect your policy to
pan out so well as if you had gone into a more fortunate
concern at the first. Also, you don’t state what premium
you paid each year, what table you insured under, what
was the amount of your .policy, or what you got in settle-
ment. You surely don’t expect us to do anything large
and violent about your alleged grievance without any de-
tails of the grievance ?
* * * *
The N.S.W. Auditor-General has just got out
his report for the year, Ist July, 1899, to 30th June, 1900,
and, as usual, he knocks the alleged surplus kite-high.
These are the figures given in Coghlan’s “ Seven
Colonies,” based on the Government's own returns :
Revenue (year to June, 1900).. .. £9,973,736
Expenditure (year to June, 1900) .. 9,811,402
Surplus .. .. .. .. £162,334
But after devoting the best part of a year to an overhaul
of the accounts, and a revelation of the wiles and devices
whereby the surplus is arrived at, the Auditor-General
produces this totally different result: —
Revenue (year to June, 1900).. .. £9,957,453
Expenditure (year to June, 1900) .. 10,191,174
Deficit £233,721
He also figures out that the accumulated deficit (not
reckoning the enormous shortage on the two superannua-
tion funds) increased from £2,757,529 in 1899 to £2,825,912
in 1900. And, as usual, the Government congratulates
the country on the “ highly satisfactory state of the
finances.”
* * * *
Westralia’s revenue for the year to 30th June,
1901, was £3,078,038, an increase of £202,642 as compared
with previous year, but the expenditure (swollen by
S’African contingents and interest on new debt) was
£3,165,244, a stupendous increase of £549,569. So instead
of a surplus of £259,721 as there was at June, 1900, there
is a deficit of £87,206.

The great and sudden improvement in earnings is set
down to better management of the bank’s Queensland
stations, and better prices obtained for cattle. The
bank’s N.S.W. stations have given no return at all ; a
great many of them are out in the West where the bones
of the dead banks lie, and in that graveyard of capital
there is no resurrection while the drought lasts. Even
now, the earnings only represent £ll9s. 6d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds at the beginning of
the half-year, which is so low as to still suggest the neces-
sity of a very heavy writing down of assets to bring them
to their real present value. But, despite all these things,
it is the most promising balance-sheet the A.J.S. has
shown for some time.
During the half-year, the reconstructed deposits
have been further reduced by £69,285, through repur-
chase of “B ” deposits at a discount. The total recon-
structed deposits now stand at £5,232,309. Apparently,
however, the new business of the bank isn’t increasing.
It is only during the last two years that new deposits
have been shown separately, and the amounts were as
follows :
December, 1899. .£1,200,773 I December, 1900. .£1,179,605
June, 1900 .. 1,199,185 | June, 1901 .. 1,076,556
During the same period the reserve of cash and liquid
assets has also shown a steady decrease, the amounts
being :
December, 1899. .£1,556,824 I December, 1900. .£1,068,856
June, 1900 .. 1,150,054 | June, 1901 .. 912,274
Still the reserves are abundant, for the liabilities which
are not strictly tied up under the reconstruction scheme
are only about £1,400,000. But the signs are that the
process of realising the old dead securities is very slow,
and another 3s. 4d. in the £ of the “A” deposits falls
due on Ist January. Apparently it was the repayment of
the last instalment in January, 1900, which so greatly
reduced the cash reserves, and they haven’t grown since,
The A.J.S. Bank shows a great improvement so far as
concerns the increase in the reserves set down in the
balance-sheet, but it has a lot to do yet before it Will look
a solid and promising institution. The least satisfactory
feature of all is this

The A.J.S. Bank has more funds invested and less money
lying idle than it had 18 months ago, yet the gross earn-

saving in ihterest through repayment of deposits have
increased the net receipts, but the assets don’t seem to
be yielding eveh So well as they did in December, ’99.
Yet the gross earnings are nearly £21,000 better thah last
half-year, when they stood at only £122,377. The note
circulation is also the lowest for a very long time—
another circumstance which doesn’t look like expahsioh.

The Ballarat Banking Co. (Ballarat, Victoria)
shows another increase of profits :
Profits, Dividends. Reserves.
Dec., 1895 .. £3,463 5J per cent. = £2,337 .. £53,410
June, 1596 .. 8,250 „ „ 2,338 .. 54,322
Dec., 1896 .. 2,937 „ „ 2,338 .. 54,921
June, 1897 ~ 2,881 „ „ 2,338 .. 55,464
‘Dec., 1897 .. 2,637 „ „ 2,338 .. 55,763
June, 1898 .. 2,785 „ „ 2,338 .. 56,210
Dec., 1898 .. 2,906 „ „ 2,337 .. 56,779
June, 1899 .. 3,022 „ „ 2,337 .. 57,464
Dec., 1899 .. 2,991 „ „ 2,338 .. 58,117
June, 1900 .. 3,154 „ „ 2,338 .. 58,933
Dec., 1900 ~ 3,335 6 per cent. „ 2,550 .. 59,718
f „ „ 2,550 "I
June, 1901 .. 3,541 J Written off Pre- >■ 60,484
( mises . - 225 )
The earnings are equal to £4 17s. lOd. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds, as compared with £4
12s. Bd. per cent, for half-year to 31/12/1900. The Ballarat
Banking Co. has a paid-up capital of £85,000, so its
reserves are very solid by comparison. During the last
half-year deposits have decreased a little, total deposits
for the last six half-years being as follows :
December, 1898 £212,677 June, 1900 .. £230,293
June, 1899 .. 215,877 December, 1900 235,240
December, 1899 218,903 June, 1901 .. 225,644
Cash and Government and municipal securities are
£73,670, being nearly 33 per cent, of all liabilities to the
public. The point where the Ballarat Banking Co. is safe
beyond the average of banks is that the shareholders’
funds (£145,484) are unusually large in proportion to the
liabilities to the public (£225,644) ; the advances would
have to depreciate by 40 per cent, before the securities of
depositors could be endangered. Even in such a mag-
nificent institution as the Bank of N.S.W. shareholders’
funds bear so much smaller a proportion to outside
liabilities that if the advances depreciated about 20 per
cent, it would exhaust the capital and reserves, and leave
the depositors with no margin of security.
The Ballarat Banking Co. is the only surviving
Australian bank which has its headquarters in a pro-
vincial town, and which exists as a protest against the
tendency to centralise all banking business in the
metropolis of each State. There was once in N.S.W. a
Bank of Bathurst with a paid-up capital of £4500, and the
Bank of North Queensland was at first a country institu-
tion with its headquarters at Townsville, but now the
Ballarat Banking Co. is the only bank on the mainland
which has its existence outside the big, all-devouring
capital cities.

In 1895 the bank overdraft had swelled to nearly £63,000,
and in order to pay this off the co. borrowed £lOO,OOO on
debentures at 5 per cent., which was a lamentably high
price to pay for money, especially as £8596 of it went in
flotation expenses, so that the money obtained really costs
£5 9s. sd. per cent. The co. also owes £47,020 on mort-
gage of its hotels, so it is trading on £132,135 of its own
money and £147,000 of other people’s. Also, it would
appear that there is still depreciation to be provided
for, and considering this and the top-heavy mass of
borrowed money the Castlemaine Brewery Co., despite its
greatly increased earnings, and the fact that it again has
a reserve, needs to go very slow in the matter of dividends
for a long while to come.

The policy-holders may not be making anything very
great, but the shareholders are certainly doing well
enough. About a year ago the total policies still in force
amounted to only £156,895, so if the assets are really
worth their face value the co. could pay all its policies at
once, and return the shareholders their capital of £40,000
three or four times over. The co. could do this even if
it received no more premiums, and it is still receiving
£4264 per annum from this source. Meanwhile, the
policy-holders are enjoying 25 per cent, dividends on
their capital of £40,000, and their average for the last
eight years has been 16 per cent. There is certainly
money in the proprietary life assurance business, if
worked as the Victoria has worked it.

At Dec., 1900, it was stated that “Your directors have
applied the surplus”—£774—“ after providing for work-
ing expenses, dividend, &c., to the further reduction of
debts of a doubtful nature,” so this writer naturally in-
cluded that amount in the sums written off, and showed
the reserves remaining as £7665. It now appears, how-
ever, that the £774 wasn’t written off but carried for-
ward, which somewhat alters the position.
The gross income of the Mont de Pi4t6 has
steadily increased during the past three half-years, but
expenses have increased also, and more than swallowed
up the surplus. The increase, however, isn’t in the
direction of salaries, which have rather diminished.

* * * *
Chillagoe Railway and Mines (N.Q.): Shares
12s. 6d., and the debentures are now offered at par. Of
course further favorable developments are reported.
Gen. manager wires “Average sample taken by me
(Harper Group) bottom of winze 6'6 p.c. cu. Pleased
with general appearance of lowest working 125 ; good ore
showing under foot. The lowest level of Zillmanton is
also ‘ indicating ’ a decided improvement. 23 M. lease,
E. drive 160 level improving. Regard general indications
as most favorable. Assay 5’3 p.c. cu.” And these gener-
alities about a group of mines on which something like
£500,000 has already been spent in railway communication,
etc.!! It is too appalling. Adelaide Critic says, “ But the
shares must be worth more than 12s. or else somebody
has been nicely fooled.” Well, as the Critic puffed these
shares when they were three times the present price, it
must admit either that it was nicely fooled or that it has
nicely fooled its readers. Writer has seen a draft pros-
pectus of 10 years ago, when an attempt (that failed) Was
made to float the Chillagoe mines on a basis of £150,000.
Also the original report, in which the whole group were
condemned on the ground that they were too scattered
and individually too small; none had any depth or
appearance of permanence, and the big formations were
far too poor. That seems to be the position to-day, and
it would have taken a big transformation to have
altered it.
* • * #
Chillagoe Railway and Mines : Stewart has
arrived from China, but has been too indisposed to go on
the mine yet. He will be accompanied there by Danvers
Power, who is to make an independent estimate and
assay of the ore bodies. The directors have returned to
Melbourne, and the shares continue to fall, touching 10s.
in London a few days ago. Of course Australia followed
suit. _ If writer had his way he would give the board a
bad time. He has reason to believe that practically all
the unsatisfactory information furnished some two
months ago by Weinberg was given to directors last year.
And, by the way, why hasn’t the full text of Weinberg’s
report been published ? The report must have been re-
ceived nearly two months ago, and meanwhile shares
have fallen by over 50 per cent. Whether any official
took advantage of it or not, the glaring and ghastly fact
remains that there was the opportunity. It is about time
that the remaining scrippers should know the names of
original shareholders who managed to creep out during
the Chillagoe boom. The publication of a list of the pre-
sent registered owners with that of, say, a year ago would
be a more interesting document than the newspaper
encouragement of “ satisfactory developments” which the
public receive with their daily bread. It will take some-
thing more than any report of Mr. Danvers Power to re-
establish confidence in this inflated concern.
• * » *
Chillagoe Blocks Co: Decided to abandon all
but 200 acres ; only 45,000 live shares left in the co. “ It
was considered that a call of Id. per month, or 3d. every
three months, would enable the co. to carry on profit-
ably.” A new way of making profit, by making calls.
* * • *
Mt. Morgan: Div., 3d (£12,500); the smallest
paid for many a year ; and the shares have fallen to 785.,
the lowest they have touched also for many a year. Is it
all on account of shortness of water, or falling off in gold,
or the sale of large numbers of shares in a recently-de-
ceased’s estate, or the simultaneous combination of all
three ?
•* * * *
Woodlark Island: Another start without fail
promised, also a better clean-up. Ore admitted to be
very variable in value, and selection impossible. “ You
will see by the assay-sheets that the ore we have been
working is not high-class, but it has all to come out.”
The assays vary from a few dwts. to 2Joz., and the richest
is in pug from which it is hard to extract. Paid-up shares,
7s. 4d. sellers ; contributing, 3s. : no buyers of either.
* * * •
At the end of Dec. last there were 7010 men
working in and on the Broken Hill mines, besides 2562
working at co.’s smelters, &e., off the mines. By June 30
the number had come down to 5856 in and on the mines,
the smelter-hands, &c.. remaining about the same. Since
June 30 another 500 have been thrown out. This is how
what are known as the “ northern group—that is, the
mines north of the B.H. Proprietary—stand :
Dec. 31 July 26
Block 14 ... 796 .. 440
British .. .. 560 ... 47
Junction .. .. 622 .. 4
Junction North .. 250 .. 6
North .. .. 234 .. 36
The mines south of the B.H. Prop., as well as the B.H.
Prop, itself, have, however, been absorbing some of these,
otherwise the aggregate position would be much worse.
And the unfortunate at Broken Hill who is out of work,
is out of work, and about 300 miles from the chance of
any
* « ♦ *
Block 10 has also dismissed close upon 200 men,
and probably would have sent more adrift but for its con-
tract to supply the Metal Co. with concentrates. The
mill is now shut down a third of its time during the
day shift (8 a.m. to 4 p.m.), so that experimental work
may the better be carried on. Block 10’s position differs
from that of the Central (Sulphide Corporation) and the
South. The two latter have big bodies of average-grade
ore, fairly uniform ; their best chance, therefore, is to de-
crease costs by increasing output. Block 10, on the other
hand, has ore of a much more varied character ; it’s best
chance is to pick out the richer ore. But of course that
makes it more difficult to handle the mine later on.
* • * *
According to what look like good advices lead is
going lower ; a rapid recovery to about £l2 10s. is then
foreshadowed. The same advices state that there is little
likelihood of an improvement for silver.
* * » *
Before, when lead got down below £ll (the
lowest the B.H. Prop, ever sold any for was in ’94, when
one parcel went for £9), the number of workmen got
down to 4290. That was at the end of ’95,- just before the
sulphide mills were started; In most cases they got to
work first on their richer and Softer sulphides ; the great
improvements made since in means of extraction have
barely kept pace with the depreciation in the quality of
the ore available. There is, however, wide scope for
further improvement. Hardly any of the mills take out
half of the silver; most of them only a little more
than one-third. Two-thirds of the lead is the
usual thing ; the other third, like the half of
the silver, goes away with the waste. Of the zinc—the
ores average 18 per cent, zinc—nothing is made ; on the
contrary, the co’s are debited if they leave it in the con-
centrates. The British qre contains, per ton, metal worth,
it is estimated, £6 Is. at present prices. It has been
getting only £2 2s. worth out. The other £3 19s. is there
for the expert ore-dresser to fish for. Moreover, it is in
ore which is not only broken and raised to surface, but is
crushed fine and lying alongside a railway. Plenty of the
stuff—hundreds of thousands of tons of it—is far richer
than that at the British mine. Of course, a problem with
such millions in it is attracting many experimentalists.
Two or three German co’s have experts at work in elabo-
rately-equipped surroundings. Nine-tenths of the effort
is, however, towards magnetic separation.
* * ■* *
B.H. Prop, has issued its half-yearly report—
Dec. 1 to May 31. Profit, £87,579 ; while £120,000 has
heen paid away in divs. And the profit ought not to be
stated at so much as £87,579 either, though that is a
heavy fall, because only £14,926 has been allowed for
depreciation, whereas the average allowance is over
£30,000 —in the half-year to Nov. 1900, it was £40,953.
What with building the iron-flux line to the Iron Knob
mine, and also the coke ovens at Bellambi, construction
expenses have this half-year been very heavy; and
through that and the payment of this £120,000 div., the
excess of liquid assets over liabilities-has been reduced by
£93,056. Certainly the co. doesn’t say it is quite as bad
as that, because the report takes credit for £24,000 which
it still includes in the reserve fund, thought it has been
spent on the flux tramway. To make a profit of £102,505
gross, or £87,579 net (according to the directors’ reckon-
ing), 296,389 tons ore was handled. This compares with
recent half-years like this (in order to show the increase
in the proportion of sulphides treated a column is added) :
Gross Ore , , .
Profit. Raised.
Tons. Tons.
May, 1898 £177,567 204,731 72,685
Nov., 1898 152,876 204,067 77,280
May, 1899 142,180 202,693 108,382
Nov., 1899 144,492 206,193 117,996
May, 1900 129,576 232,487 163,993
Nov., 1900 167,795 293,451 210,808
May, 1901 102,505 296,389 231,869
From £167,795 to £102,505 is an awkward fall; but, seeing
that the co. averaged £3 11s. 6d. less for its lead in the
latter than in the former term, and a small fraction less
for its silver (metallic lead production, 27,869 tons ; ditto
silver, 3,009,1640 z.), it might easily have been a lot worse.
In fact, the co. has dodged about two-thirds of the bump
by getting out rather better ore, by reducing the cost of
quarrying (the open-cuts are about finished, so there
hasn’t been much batter to make in worthless country
rock), and by improvements in both concentrating and
smelting. The effect of the small reduction in freights
(the S.A. Govt, allows Is. and the Silverton Tramway 00.
2d. per ton), of the new iron-flux line, and of the new
coke works (these last will supply only half of the co.’s
requirements) have not yet been felt. At present
special efforts are being made to increase the capacity
of the smelting furnaces, and to reduce the cost of
haulage from the mine faces underground to the mill
(electric traction is being tried underground instead of
horses).
* • • *
B.H. Proprietary doesn’t say what its silver and
lead averaged—only says that they averaged so much
more (or less) than they did some other time, when they
averaged so much more (or less) than they did at some
other time, when they averaged—&c. Highest and
lowest lead quotations for the half year (to May 31) were
£l6 12s. 6d. and £l2 Is. 3d., though the former was for
only a brief space in the opening weeks ; still the average
was probably brought up to somewhere near £l3. And
as the price during the last few weeks has been anything
from £l2 2s. 6d. to £l2 65., this means a considerable
further bump ; in itself quite as big a bump as economies
now in hand can make up for.
* • * *
Some B. H. Prop, figures (those in brackets are
for a six-month period 10 years ago): Ore treated,
296,389 tons (147,473); silver, 3,009,1640 z. (5,028,914);
lead, 27,869 tons (17,466); gross profit, £102,505 (£672,124);
wages and salaries, £314,500 (£199,500); mine timber,
£19,578 (£61,201). Plant and machinery, present valua-
tion, 400,159 ; written off, £565,000. Total cash distributed
to shareholders (divs. and bonuses), £7,976,000. The co.
was formed July 14,1885.
* * * *
It has been persistently rumored that wages at
Broken Hill are to be reduced 10 per cent., and thereafter
raised on a sliding scale. All that has happened so far is
an ostensibly unofficial suggestion made by shift-bosses
that that would be better than having a mine shut down.

* * • *
N.S.W. diamonds : The Elliott Co. seems to be
doing the most profitable work in the State, making an
actual profit of £1338 this year, chiefly on account of the
rise in price. The highest last year was 16s. per carat,
and an offer of 255. 6d. for the 500 carats on hand was
refused t’other day. Stated that many years profitable
work is practically in sight.
• * * *
London and Globe Finance Co. : More start-
ling revelations cabled—the transfer of 200,000 West le
Roi shares at par, ex div., at a time when the West le
Roi Co. had five undeveloped claims costing 38,000d015.,
representing issues of capital equal to £437,000. It then
paid to the British .American Corporation the sum of
£200,000 by giving a half-interest in five claims costing
38,000d015. This has come on top of finding the whole of
the securities pledged for £500,000 borrowed and lost in
the Lake View slump, £425,000 in loans still outstanding,
and the total claims of unsecured creditors exceeding a
million. Yet, when the mail left, the British Austra-
lasian, and other London financial journals, were still
clamoring for the voluntary liquidation of this awful con-
cern—the B.A. urging that Whittaker Wright had made
a lot of money for his friends in other directions, that he
should not be blamed for one big failure, and that, unfor-
tunately, he was a man who was unable to distinguish
between well-meaning friends and incipient blackmailers.
Compulsory liquidation is in England a severe process,
and generally makes it quite clear how the milk got out
of the cocoa-nut.
* * * *
Perseverance: “The additional furnaces will
not arrive on the mine before Oct. or Nov. The founda-
tions, however, are being prepared, and no time will be
lost in the erection of the roasters when they do come to
hand. With the whole plant in full running order, the
Perseverance will probably be in a position to declare a
monthly output of 15,0000 z., and so far as the mine’s ores
are concerned this high yield can be easily maintained.”
That is the picture of the future. The present position of
the mine was revealed at the annual meeting of share-
holders held in London recently—that £llO,OOO had been
borrowed for the sulphide plant, and although Jhat has
been running nearly a year, only half the debt has been
cleared off. No div. was declared, but it was admitted
that the total cost of mining and milling the sulphidejore
was £3 Iss. per ton, including everything. There is also
reason to believe that the bulk value of the sulphide ore
in this mine is under 25dwt. per ton. To put the
average up to ljoz. an undue proportion of the work
must take place on the rich shutes, the effect of which
will be seen when they come back to the poorer ore to
square up the stopes. Already the rich shute of ore on
the Lake View boundary has helped this mine a good
deal. There is an immense amount of ore above the
500 ft. level it is true, but there is a great deal of it under
an oz. That it is variable in value is proved by the bulk
of the ore coming close to the Lake View boundary im-
mediately under the big open cut. That is where the
best oxidised ore came from, and, of course, it is an
established fact that the ore below in the sulphide zone
is correspondingly good. The Perseverance is un-
doubtedly an expensively worked big mine, the shares of
which are too big a price. As there seems no hope of
making any material reduction of working expenses,
divs. must be few and far between.
* * ¥ *
Boulder Main Reef: “A further decline in
these shares is a mystery as well as a disappointment to
Australian holders.” From this, it may be assumed that
the failure to strike pay-ore in the bottom level is a
matter of detail. The decline is no mystery to anyone
who has the slightest knowledge of the business. Unfor-
tunately, few men who write of mines and mining
markets in Australia ever went down a mine or held a
share, and the public have to put up with their ignorance.
* * * *
Oroya: At last this mine has made what looks
like an important development. The diamond drill re-
mained in lode matter for 20ft., and the average of the
full core is given at Boz. “ There is now no shadow of
doubt that the sensationally rich shute of ore worked by
the Associated Northern extends well into the Oroya
ground.” Also said that the ore discloses the same
characteristics as the ore from the other mine.
* * • *
Long Tunnel Extended (Vic.): Resolution to
amalgamate with the Long Tunnel mine negatived, and
the chairman said the question of taking proceedings
against that co. for loss caused by flooding the Extended
works would receive attention. Amalgamation not likely
to take place under these circumstances. The erstwhile
great market value of each mine has vanished almost
utterly, and while the fall of the parent co. has been the
greater, the management of the Extended should kick
themselves, or be kicked, for refusing to sell the mine at
a price that will never be offered again.

South German G.M. (Vic.) sold recently at
425. per share. When The Bulletin, some two or three
years ago, published the truth about the South German
outlook, and the directors came to the rescue with re-
assuring reports, shares were marketable at £7. By the
way, one of the S.G. directors—Gray of Maldon—was sent
into Vic. Leg. Council lately.

Westralian timber exports slumped nearly
£lOO,OOO last year—they fell from £533,198 to £458,77&
So much karri has been palmed-off as jarrah that the
trade has had a knock from which the Conservator of
Forests thinks it can only recover through a Timber
Branding Act.
* * * *
Queensland mining accidents for 1900 were
numerous. Total, 109 casualties ; 18 fatal. The Towers
was responsible for 35 accidents; seven fatal. These
figures do not include coal-mining accidents. Banana-
land’s coal industry employs 1210 miners, and was respon-
sible for the handsome total of 23 casualties, including
nine fatalities, for 1900. Five men were killed at one
time through the old trouble—naked lights, &c.
* * * »
Newcastle’s trade figures for the six months to
June 30 were phenomenal. Though the export of coal
advanced only 185,532 tons (from 1,383,033 to 1,568,565),
its value bounded from £508,274 to £772,147. No wonder
the miner kept on asking, “ Where do I come in I ”
* * * *
How they mine at Bendigo. The New Moon
Co. has crushed 159,677 tons in the 9j years to 30th June,
1901. The gold recovered averaged barely Bdwt. per ton.
Yet it paid £108,600 in dividends (average t'11,432 per
annum) on a total called-up capital of £11,560.
tically 100 per cent, per annum out of Bdwt. rock. Co. also'
spent out of earnings, £19,107 on machinery'. There have
been no calls for 11 years. Last half-year the average
got down below 6dwt. per ton, and there was also a slight
falling oft in the output of stone as compared with 1900,
yet the dividends were £66oo—at the rate of £13,200 per
annum or 114 per cent. If the New Moon was English-
owned its whole output would hardly pay the expenses of
the London office.
* * * *
Dredging in itself is a good enough game in
suitable places—always provided that the man who pays
the calls stands by and cleans up his own gold. That is
the fly in the ointment. It is not necessarily the wash
that declares the dividend —it is the manager, whose
power is, in this matter, absolute.

tons last half-year.

The Austral Otis Engineering Company have
now delivered one of the water-ends in connection with
their contract with the Melbourne and Metropolitan
Board of Works. The second water-end will be delivered
within a week or two. These water-ends are a very
massive piece of work and reflect credit on the makers.
They are made of a special mixture of iron, which was
subjected to severe tests before being put into the work
and gave highly satisfactory results. Each water-end
weighs about 70 tons.*

* * * *
If further proof is needed that Luhrig Yanners
are the best and most economical machines for dressing
fines and slimes, it will be found in the fact that the Tas-
mania gold mine, Beaconsfield, Tasmania, has decided to
increase the number of these machines in the extension
of the present mill and reduction works. When com-
pleted it will have over 100 head of stampers at work, and
60 Luhrig Yanners, including the additional 24 of the
large type. Before finally adopting plans the Tasmania
board sent its manager to inspect the chief reduction
works in other States. Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, are the Sole Agents for the Luhrig appli-
ances.*

For the year to 30th June, 1901, the exports of
the United States exceeded its imports by £138,400,000.
This huge balance went mostly to pay off old foreign
debts or to buy up foreign securities. The Yankee's
mortgage on the earth will soon be a very real affair, and
he is jostling J. Bull very hard for the billet of the
world’s great money-lender, pawnbroker, and mortgagee.

LEROY
LIQUEUR
BRANDY
The Waxcellent Madden seems to have
tripped over his own moustache in his eagerness
to bite Royalty’s ear for that baronetcy. Madden,
the Chief Justice, who is expected to be a sound
lawyer, appeals, on a point of constitutional law,
to Judas Chamberlain, and is duly notified by
cable: “ Adoption of seizure by His Majesty’s
Govt, as an Act of State” (proposed by Madden)
“ would not help, if no local authority to seize.
Defence of Act of State not available against British
subject.” Regarded altogether, and especially the
last sentence, Chamberlain’s message seems to
say, “What the devil is all the fuss about if, as
you say, the paper has no influence?”
* * » *
Queensland Government, taking a leaf
from the N.S.W. book, is sending round a
memo, to the post-and-telegraph officers in the
Northern State, demanding subscriptions for the
weary memorial to Queen Victoria (who is think-
ing of Queen Victoria now ?) Of course the cir-
cular doesn’t say one must give, but it is signed
by the permanent head of the P.O. Dept., and
his official title is under his name in big print. A
communication of this sort from tiie Deputy-
P.M.G. to his underlings is not a request ; it is
similar to a Royal invitation to sing or play at
Windsor Castle —no compulsion, but Will
any of the recipients of these stand-and-
deliver circulars have the backbone to send
them back to 'the D.P.M.G. by return
post ?
SHOPPING WITH THE
MISSUS,
“ John, if you had a spark of manhood you’d
carry the child!”
Shortly after Queen Victoria’s death
Hobart Mercury started a memorial fund with
£lO raised in the office; but despite frantic
appeals to the loyalty of the Tassies —which the
Mercury boasts is of a higher order than that of
any other State —the fund is still less than £lOO.
Either the Raspberrylanders are not as loyal as
they’re cracked up to be, or they think that pro-
prietor Davies having been made a C.M.G. the
object of the fund is achieved. George tried
hard to get his memorial fund amalgamated with
the Tasmanian Soldiers’ Memorial Fund which
amounted to some hundreds, but the subscribers
to the latter firmly declined to allow that sort
of Tambaroora, and the burly Mayor almost
weeps as he looks at his fund slowly crawling up
to a century in this fashion :
Shillings.
Already acknowledged .. .. 1981
Snooks .. .. .. .. 1
In vain lie reminds the public that subscriptions
“need not now be limited to a shilling.”
* * * *
The expected has happened. Slush
Hennessey, with an assortment of distended fol-
lowers, has asked N S.W. Premier See to pay
£335, the deficit resulting from the operations of
the gorgeous Sydney citizens’ ducal celebration
committee. And the Premier (no doubt afraid of
being called a “ pro-Boer ” if he refused), instead
of bluntly telling S.H. and co. to go and pay then
own Crawl-bill, says he thinks the Treasury ought
to stump up the amount, seeing that the event
was “ the greatest in the history of the country ! ”

* * * *
According to Melb. Age, Queen Alexandra
has set a fashion in babies by driving abroad with
one of her grandchildren on her knee. The result
is most surprising. Babies are now almost as
fashionable as dogs, and ladies of ton are actually
borrowing them for decorative purposes when
driving in the park. The baby-fancier looms in
the distance.
* * * *
The stale imported Chinese egg is now
very largely consumed in N.S.W., and to a
less degree in some other States. It is
said that the importers could profitably
sell these eggs in Sydney at 4d. per
dozen. One merchant states that he
got a letter t’other day from Hong-
kong offering eggs at that port at
Id. and 2d. per dozen. The egg
industry is worth about £500,000
per annum in N.S.W. If no
steps are taken, it will soon be
practically monopolised by the
Chow with his cheap chilled
egg laid by an overworked
heathen hen.

got a pair. Also dozens of policemen got auto-

ife that he wasn’t disguised in a bogus “order.”

• * » *
Victorian tax-payers will get a shock
when they are presented with the bill for fitting
up the Exhibition-building as a State Parliament
House. There is no doubt that the money spent
on decorating the dome and main hall for the
reception of York, and the fittings for the State
Parliament House, will run into a sum which
would have been sufficient to complete the
Parliament House in Spring-street. Members
have pressed the Govt, to put them up most
sumptuously. There are rooms not only for
every half-dozen members who choose to call them-
selves a party, but there are rooms for nearly
every one of the members of both chambers.
The corridors are elaborately fitted with costly
chairs and lounges, and carpeted with beautiful
scarlet material. The estimate given for the
work originally was £30,000 but when all accounts
are footed the sum will not be far short of £70,000.
What is to be done with the fittings when the
Federal Parliament leaves Melbourne no one
can say, least of all the Peacock Government.
» * * *
At present Victoria is paying the interest on
the cost of, and the upkeep of, three gubernatorial
houses. The agreement between the Govt, and
the Federal Govt, re the upkeep of Government
House, where Hopetoun reigns, has not been
signed. A few weeks ago the Vic. Govt, rushed
into a heavy expenditure in the purchase of an
enormous place at Malvern for the State Gover-
nor’s residence. Except for a few rats and an old
butler the Malvern palace is empty. Then there
is the Mt. Macedon house, which costs a good
sum annually to keep in order. By the way, the
Peacock Govt, has decided that the Macedon
house is not to be maintained in future for State
Governors. So far it has not been decided what
is to be done with it. It looks, at present, as if it
is to become a white elephant. There is just a
chance of the State Governor, when appointed,
renting the Macedon house, but seeing the trouble
there is over that official’s pay, the chance seems
small. By the way, great pressure is being:
brought to bear on Vic. Govt, to increase the
Governor’s Wages by allowing him a private sec.
and an A.D.C.

Srobably accounts for the high artistic merit of

Auckland made an even greater mess of its
Grand Hotel fire than Sydney did of its conflagra-
tion. Poor Clegg jumped 120 ft. from the top of
Hordern’s building because the ladders could not
reach higher than 90. In Auckland, Dora
Wallace was only 30 ft. from the ground, and yet
she was driven to jump for her life and sustained
fatal injuries. Had they no life-saving ladders
in Auckland, nor nets or tarpaulins or blankets in
which the unfortunate woman could have bet n
caught ?

From Sydney D.IV, 25/7/’Ol :
Almost daily the Mayor of the city receives insulting
letters regarding the increased city rate, and some of his
correspondents have gone the length of threatening him
with personal violence.
A terrible Victorian person proposed to
the Decimal Coinage Committee that this country
should have a sovereign worth £1 Os. 10d., divided
into 100 pennies, each worth 2gd., the penny
being worth ten farthings. '1 his is the dullest
idea up to date. By adopting the dollar Australia
would get a decimal coinage, and also secure uni-
formity with Canada, the U.S., and many other
places. This Victorian idea would give us a deci-
mal coinage that nobody outside Australia could
comprehend. The world wants badly to gel a
uniform decimal coinage—not to add to the exist-
ing confusion by having people invent new coins.

HOME CIGARETTES


Launceston (Tasmania) is decorated just
now with a large mare’s nest about a lost noble-
man. Ten years ago (in June, 1891) a certain
Samuel Cox died in the local Benevolent Asylum,
his age being incredibly stated at 105, also at 117.
Probably he was 85 or 90 or thereabouts. The
legend is that his real name was Jervis, and that
he was the son of a large English landowner at
Lichfield. He went voyaging in a vessel called
the Regent Fox, with a naval uncle who cast him
ashore in Tasmania long before there was
any white settlement, consequently he lived
with the aborigines for 40 years. The naval
uncle thus became heir to the estates, won the
great sea battle of Cape St. Vincent, became Earl
St. Vincent, and lived rich and respected for
many years on the property he had “shook ” from
boy Samuel. Samuel, in the unexplainable
fashion of such wanderers, never claimed the pro-
perty, though it seems to be alleged that he told
the tale of his aristocratic descent occasionally
in public-houses and other places. The toughest
discrepancy lies in the fact that John Jervis won
the battle Which made him Earl St. Vincent in
1797, 94 years before Samuel died, and had been
in the West Indies for at least three years
before 1797, and as it was previous to this
that he marooned Samuel in Tasmania, that per-
son’s Australian career must have spread over
about 100 years. Apparently, by his own account,
he could have been only about five years old when
he was in the navy, and an unfeeling relative cast
him ashore. All the same, the managemenf of
Launceston Benevolent Asylum takes the matter
very seriously indeed, and proposes to recover the
cost of Samuel’s maintenance out of the. alleged
“ estate ” which should have come to him if it
hadn’t been for the sins of the uncle aforemen-
tioned in the century before last.
* ■* * *
Dr. Koch’s opinion, expressed at the
London Tuberculosis Conference, that infection to
human beings through eating diseased meat and
drinking diseased milk was extremely rare, is
being vigorously controverted, and will lead to a
long and interesting discussion. Here, indeed, is
a field wherein some young earnest Australian
doctor may win fame. If consumption and cancer
aren’t spread here by diseased-meat food it isn’t
because of the absence of diseased meat. A coun-
try medico the other day wrote to a Sydney paper
stating that he had cut cancer from corned beef
at his own table, and that he had found a big bag
of tuberculous matter in a roast leg of mutton
served up to him at dinner.
There was something very sad in the an-
nouncement of Mrs. Goe’s age. Sixty-two ! The
Bishop of Melbourne’s wife belonged to the order
of good women who seem to think that their first
duty in life is to look old, and who in their well-
meaning way tend to make the worldly ones more
worldly. Mrs. Goe was only 48, it seems, when
she first came to Melbourne. A photograph of
the new arrival at that period . shows her to
have been quite agedly disposed, from the crown
of her bonnet downwards. Why do the wives of
the clergy make a point of looking as though they
never were young? It offers no encouragement
for other women to get born again, and it certainly
doesn’t tempt male sinners to. marry into the
Christian Endeavour set. What religion wants is
a jaunty, peroxided sisterhood in prettily-built
frocks who will advocate the bringing of inward
spiritual grace to the surface, as much as possible.

At a very fashionable Northern wedding
last week (the bride was 34 and the groom 60) the
organist played, as a voluntary, “When we are
married, what will you do, dear?” from “The
Belle of New York.” And next day some incensed
clerics of same denomination protested hotly
against a set piece “Rock of Ages,” being ex-
hibited in a draper’s window. Said it was
sacrilege.
* * * *
Many Sydney landlords, being called u pon
to pay about 3d. extra rates on a small house at
10s. a week, have cheerfully put up the rent to
15s. or so, solemnly assuring the tenant that the
whole ss. was wanted to cover the extortionate
new charges of the City Council. Even good
pious landlords are doing this sort of thing.
Apart from any moral disadvantages about being
such a degraded variety of liar, how about the
criminal aspect of the matter ? The Bulletin
would like to see a test case tried to find out if
this isn’t getting money under false pretences.
Of course a landlord can demand ss. a week more
rent if he likes; that isn’t criminal. But de-
manding ss. is one thing and persuading the
tenant to pay it by false representations is
another.
* * * »
Oxford-street, Sydney, will see a thril-
ling accident one of these days. Between 5
and 6 p.m. the steam trams for Waverley and
Paddington are simply rushed by a crowd of
passengers eager to get home, and they crowd the
footboards much as was the custom at Common-
wealth time.' Then , drivers were careful and
went slow, but these trams in the evening (some
are throughs) travel at top speed and one night
the wheel of a ’bus will get close enough and
scoop the clustered footboards of three swiftly-
moving cars. It has nearly happened more than
once already.
* * • *
The Royal Commission on sweating has
taken some Valuable evidence back to Melbourne
re sweating in the Sydney tailoring trade. One
of the witnesses deposed as to the rates paid by
his firm, and then, with humility in his heart,
went to a mass-meeting of the sweated women-
workers, and told them he was shocked to say his
evidence, he had since found out, was “incor-
rect ” ; his bosses’ books had disclosed that a still
lower rate was paid than that to which he swore.
“It was the last straw to break the camel’s back,
and he had resigned.” Melbourne Commission take
note.

* » * *
They must have curious ideas of comets
in Sydney D.T. office. Thus their correspondent:
OROOKWELL, Tuesday.—A very remarkable comet
was observed here this morning. It was forked-shaped,
and made its appearance at about 4 o’clock on the NAV.
horizon. It appeared to be very close to the earth, and
was travellling at a tremendous pace towards the sun.
That scientific touch of “travelling towards the
sun” deceived the night-editor. 4-s the comet
appeared in the West and the sun in the East the
former must certainly have been going at a terrific
bat. Had the object really been a comet, its rate
of speed, if worked out, would have surprised the
correspondent. Of course the apparition was a
common meteor.
QUID PRO QUO.
HUB.: “My dear, you know you cost a lot.”
WIFEY: “ Well, don’t I look as if I did!”
The fearsome Vic. J.P. doesn’t quite
monopolise the absurdity of that State’s magis-
tracy. A couple of constables, in a Port Melb.
Sunday-trading case, being flatly contradicted by
the licensee and another witness as to the possi-
bility of seeing into the bar from the place where
they were standing, Keogh, P.M., went to the
hotel and had a look for himself. He found the
licensee’s version correct. Back in court
he remarked he could not understand the
constables, who had nothing to gain, conspiring
to commit perjury, and fined the publican £5.
Great is the power of the Uniform ! .

The Yarra Sculptors’ Society, which
Douglas Richardson started in Melbourne tWo
years ago, is still a-blowing and a-growing. At
the recent annual meeting Richardson, as presi-
dent, congratulated the members present on the
fact that they were more numerous than last year,
and had twice as big a cash balance to carry for-
ward. The official report of the meeting didn’t
mention the size of last year’s balance, but, after
all, what does it matter if cash be somewhat short.
Art is long, and there are absolutely no limits to
enthusiasm. V. . ..
Dear Bulletin.— -The attention paid by
the Peacock Govt, to the morals of the Vic. old-
age pensioner is truly marvellous. Not far from
Omeo, a bedridden woman, aged 86, and her male
partner, a year or two younger —though quite
destitute —were refused a pension, on the ground
that they were occupying the same house without
having been married. However, the difficulty
has been got over by the humanity of a clergyman
and a lawyer, who rode 80 miles to marry the old
couple. Such hypocritical quibbling as this stirs
up the gall in anybody who remembers the shame-
ful waste of coin that lias been going on in Mel-
bourne for the past 12 months, while worthy
people are starving.—J.P.C.
* * * *
British newspaper phrases: “The dull
continuous aching pain caused by the war.” . * i
“The dreary drip of daily casualties.”
A Melbourne suburb is in spasms, all over
a harmless, necessary constable. One night lately
the local senior officer failed to do his “round,
and law’norder very nearly broke down in conse-
quence. For hours the constables on beat searched
high and low, and were finally thinking of run-
ning in the whole population on a charge of
“ concealing a senior officer in the execution of his
jooty,” when it occurred to some, one to search
the watch-house. Arriving there they heard a
great riot proceeding from a cell, where a casual
drunk was protesting against being disturbed in
his sleep by the outcries of the lost senior and the
watch-house keeper. They had been escorting
the over-loaded one to his couch when the door
slammed; and for four long cold hours two
large policemen had been raising Cain in
an endeavor to attract the attention of Constable
Decimal Ten, who was sleeping some 20ft. away.
Policeman Decimal Ten said he heard the row,
but took it to be an unsober prisoner with an
attack of fantods, so he simply turned over and
snored some more. For this alleged neglect of
duty he was transferred to the city. And now the
residents are pleading to have their policeman
back again. They say he has a beautiful snore
and his dreamy countenance is as soothing as a
brick dropped on a worm, and they won’t be
happy till they get him.

Despite war and hysteria, Maoriland does
pot lose sight of progressive and useful legislation.
A., important measure, for instance, is the Tes-
tators’ Family Maintenance Act of last
session. Prior to the passing of-that Act,
a will could not be upset unless undue
influence or duress could be proved.} Now,
, however, should a testator omit—no
\ matter from wliat cause—to make
\ proper provision out of his estate
\ for those whom, if he were alive,
' he would be bound to support,
the aggrieved party or parties
may apply to the Supreme
Court for an order to have
reasonable provision made.
Since the Act became law
one case has been determined
under its provisions. A man
died, leaving only £2OO for
his widow out- of an estate of
£l5OO, and in spite of the op-
position of distant relatives
who would benefit by the will
as it stood, the court ordered
that she should receive £1 per
week for her lifetime. About
the justice and common-sense of the
Act there can be no doubt what-
ever. The l&w compels a man to
support his wife out of his estate
while he is alive, quite regardless
of the fact that he may want the
estate himself for drink or other pur-
poses. Why, then, should she not have
an equal claim on the same estate when
he is dead and doesn’t want, the estate
himself, either for drink or.other objects—
or, at least, when he can’t- use. it, how-
ever much he wants it for drink ? Also,
why should a corpse have powers that
the live man doesn’t possess? If any
man has a grievance against his wife
which is good enough to entitle him
to refuse her further maintenance,
he should demonstrate the fact in
the law-courts while he is yet alive.
When he takes a mean posthumous re-
venge by leaving the woman unprovided
for, there is always the risk that she
will become a burden on the State, and the
State has enough burdens without paying
for any dead man’s grudge.

If the R.C. conscience had a reasonable
sense of humour it could enjoy the Melb. Age
report of an Orange procession at Carlton (Melb.)
t’other Sunday. Carlton is not the suburb where
the riots come from, but the local L.O.L.’ers do
the thing properly all the same ; 150 members in
full regalia proceshed to a Presbyterian taber-
nacle. The leaders bore the Bible and two un-
sheathed swords, and took up prominent positions
under the pulpit, which had a Union-Jack in
froiit of it. On the walls of the House of God
were placards reminding the worshippers of
Boyne and other improving battles. And the
choir received valuable assistance from Mr. and
Miss Moy Ling, as though the function weren’t
yellow enough and funny enough without intro-
ducing a Chinese element.
TWO IN A BOAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(he speaks)
Just so ! sit so; and take the rudder lines—
The left and right, and pull with either hand.
Let us go moving down the road that shines
To fairyland.
(she speaks)
A dream, a dream, the silver Harbor seems.
Row soft! roiv slow! lest you should roughly break
This calmness beautiful where something dreams
’Twere sin to wake.
(he thinks)
How soft the moon is shining on her face!
Her soul how white, how far withdrawn from love!
I feel like one spell-bound in holy place,
Afraid to move.
(she thinks)
O woman art.' O art that shapes desire,
That makes the hunted, huntress in her turnl
Be near me now, and flood my eyes with fire
To make him burn.
(he thinks)
Her eyes, how dark they are and how divine!
With dim, delicious, half-hid things aflood.
They beckon me as meat, sweet meat and wine,
One mad for food.
(she thinks)
I caught him glancing downward at my shoe.
By trifles, such and such, the man is led;
I’ll give my lips another bite or two
To make them red.
(he thinks)
*Tis Eden still where such an instep trips,
And where it falls the earth must gladder be,
But, hush! by that compression of her lips,
She fathoms me!
(she thinks)
Yon island has a fane like Daphne’s grove,
Where lost to all the world young lovers sit;
Suggestion is the finger-post to Love-
Til steer for it.
(he speaks)
Yon island is the place whereto they go
On Summer nights when moons above a/re fair.
(she speaks)
Indeed, and pray what is it that they do
11 'hen they go there t
(he speaks)
They do not sit three feet apart like us,
l Vith converse cold, beneath the cold white moon f
Bat—oh, be not afraid '.—they nestle thus,
And thus they swoon.
(he thinks)
A pearl! a pearl of most exceeding worth,
Nay more.'—so white, so innocently whitel
Methinks I am the happiest man on earth
This blessed night.
(she thinks)
Now let me handle skilfully the noose '.
With cunning give and take, make artful play,
Lest, school-girl-like, l snap the gear and lose
My latest prey!
N.S.W.

A paragraph confirmatory of opinions fre-
quently expressed by this paper makes official
appearance :
The Government health officer’s report concerning an
outbreak of enteric fever at Logan Downs Station in
Queensland, attributes the disease to abominably defec-
tive and inadequate sanitary. conveniences in, connection
with the shearers’ hut: The quarters-of station hands
and shearers are much the same in both Queensland and
New South Wales, and, in the interests of workers, sadly
need more attention at the hands of the Government.
