A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 29, 1901.
My Dear Arini,—
Opening of Parliament is ever and always
described as “ a brilliant function.” It may be
very red-and-gold and instrumentally brazen in
lands where kings and things open the corrob-
boree. But in Australia there is no superior
ceremony, save perhaps
inMelb., where President Zeal’s tea puts—or used
to put a glittering tail to the Parliamentary kite.
The first N.S.W. Parliament since the inaugur-
ation of the Australian Commonwealth, opened
on Wednesday last week by Acting-Gover-
nor Darley, was about as brilliant as a fog-bank.
And it was all over while you’d be making
two pancakes. There was nothing to see—even if
the dressing had been worth viewing the Council
Chamber was too dark for any but cats’ eyes.
C.C. was crammed with women all wearing warm
clothes and a de profundi* demeanor. They re-
minded me of that party in the parlor, all silent
and all—(bad word). Enter the stately “ Looten-
nant-Guvner ” (as American residents call him) at-
tended by his son, Capt. Darley, and another young
A.D.C., Captain Watt, with thin, ’possumy legs
left undraped by his Highland skirt. Soon as
members of the Lower House were yarded (the
space in which they were wedged is insufficient by
law for any travelling stock), the A.G. read The
Speech, with its good old familiar wheezes about
noxious weeds, rabbit-pest, and female suffrage.
These things I think have been in every Aus-
tralian Governor’s speech for years, and you may
know by the alteration in the reader’s voice if he
be personally in favor of the last. Gov. Dailey
hurried over the clause in a way that suggested
it wasn’t a chocolate-cream on his tongue.
Rosehill Races on Saturday brought out very
few women, but shoals of men. The day was
“princely,” as Olive Schreiner has it —windless
and clear. And as the males gathered in solid
crowds in the betting-paddock between each
event, little serpents of blue smoke hung above
each man. Not enough breeze to blend the spirals
into a cloud. No smoke without fire —in the
centre of the pack was a woman with a flame-
colored toque. She fitted somewhat into the
proverb-picture, especially as she was making
things warm for Mr. o’i.Vlelcliisedeck over an
alleged “little bit ” on Chippy Norton.
Concerning fire: the sward on the straight
nearly opposite the grand-stand was on fire
through a lighted match, and you’d scarcely
believe what a good blaze that short crop of
winter grass made. Violet dressing was still in
abundance, but the flowers themselves were not
there, save on Miss Palotta. She wore a
large bunch, and her big feathered hat,
and the very newest things in neck-gear—the
pelerine. Ruffles have gone out, Paris fashion-
arbiters say. Grace’s pelerine was of white satin,
with far-apart black spots, and edged with swans-
down. Her muff was huge and of the same
materials. There was a plain skirt with a, long
plain coat of fine white serge containing a woman
who wasn’t plain. The nightgown effect was
lessened by her toque of blue with grey bird’s
plumage. Another frock was of pale blue with
narrow gold braid. And there was a hat that
looked at a distance like a gilded flower-pot;
closer, you saw it was a shape covered with wide
gold braid. A tree branch, or another woman’s
umbrella, or something mischanceful, had ripped
the braid loose from the top, and the gear took on
a very out-all-night effect. Many of the women
were heavily furred about the necks. What
warmth your neck requires in the day-time if
you’ve an expensive fur, and what freezing you
can endure at night if you’ve a single-stone dia-
mond necklace !
“ The King’s son, and the King’s son’s wife,” as
divers verse-writers in M.L. weeklies prefer to
call the Yorks, have left our coasts and left a big
vacuum in all the cities they’ve visited, but the
Ophir is much deeper in the water thereby. The
))resents the Yorks have garnered in from the cities
c mse the vacuumand thesubmergingof the Ophir’s
Plimsoll mark. W.A. school children, whipped-
up by the obsequious education authorities, sent
silver dingoes, emus, and kangaroos for the Royal
grass-orphans. Amongst the tons of stuff taken
from M.L. were a pair of paroquets and an albino
wood-hen. A carved Maori house furnished,
not with mats and scalps and greenstone
axes, but with addresses from various city
councils, was brought along by a boss chief and
presented on behalf of the councils. How care-
fully the Duke will lose the key of that house !
Of course the Councillors would add the anomaly
of a key to guard their precious, staggering syntax.
Then, there’s the doll which a little girl named
Smith (of South Yarra) sent to the Duchess for
little Princess Victoria. Now, the family of
Smiths to which she belongs will in future be
known from other Smiths as the Smiths that gave
a doll to the King’s granddaughter, and got in
return a letter from the lady-in-waiting thanking
“the little girl Marguerite Smith.” A corres-
pondent writing from Perth tells me that there
was nothing wrong with the arches of welcome to
the Yorks save the inscriptions on them. “Glad
are we that your Highnesses have come,” and
“ Our true intent is all for your delight,” prove
that the text-makers didn’t “tower” in originality.
From M.L. comes the story of a Mayor, who,
after formally welcoming the Yorks in the name
of his Council, rushed the Mayoress and his
daughter up to them instead of waiting for the
Mayoral reception later. At this reception the
Pair were compelled to sit for several hours
listening to the “local talent,” and when they
could sit it no and said they would like to
leave, his Mayorship put the mud roof on his
loyalty by detaining them whilst his daughter
favored them with a vocal selection. Then he
forgot to see Them to their carriage, and They
forgot to give him photographs.
Calling upon a friend at a city hotel recently I
chanced upon a remarkable wedding. The bride,
who is well over 40, wore the conventional white
satin and veil. The little “ Buttons ”of the hotel
was bearing her train, and seemed greatly amused
at the office. Bridegroom is said to be 24 and
poor. Bride is known to be wealthy, with station
property wayback. Some weeks ago she was due
at the altar with a different bridegroom. But
although he. was there she was not, and now the
reason’s plain.
Talking of marriages—one that is to be. Some-
time ago the daughter of one of our Somebodies
went to Europe to study art. After some more
time she returned much against her will, and,
after spending a further time here, insisted upon
being allowed to go back from the Artless, empty
life of this city to her beloved art and fuller life in
the old world. And whilst she was packing her
palettes and tailor-mades, the emptiness of life
here was suddenly filled by a man who more than
compensates for art and the old world. A man
whom she will presently marry. Which reminds
me that Miss Mabel Watson is engaged to Mr.
Stewart Osborne.
Friends are congratulating Knight Harris and
his ex-Mayoress on the birth of their first grand-
child—Mrs. Carty Salmon’s. George Lambert
also hails his first-born in Paris. You recollect he
married one of the artistic and literary group of
Abseil Sisters. Now shall we see the “ Head of a
Child” by G.L. in this or that exhibition. Joy
for the births, grief for the deaths —the death
of Mrs. Stoddart, a sweet little woman, and an
exquisite miniature artist, who died in London
after an operation.
Miss Pearl M‘Crossin’s and Miss Fanny Walker’s
concert last week was remarkable for its good
audience (good in point of numbers, but most un-
reasonable in its demand for encores) and the
quantity of flowers given to the principals. Miss
M‘Crossin has a pretty mezzo-soprano voice. I
liked her best in “ Still as the Night ” and a tiny
song “Violets.” Miss Walker gave Handel’s
“Laseia ch’io Pianga.” Audience made a great
fuss over the contralto, who deserved it, and re-
sponded with “ Caro mio ben.” Herr Veil mar,
Miss M‘Cardell, and Mr. Grimm filled their spots
in the programme satisfactorily.
In future, Mr. Percy Bates’s Saturday-night
Pops will be held at the Queen’s Hall. Poppers
don’t like being disturbed in their pop-pastures,
and the Town Hall has a bad habit of getting
engaged for balls, political meetings, and things.
At the last pop, Miss Winifred Davis was
starred. She is from the Guildhall School of
Music, London, and proved to be a tall, graceful,
very attenuated, dark girl, dressed in yellow,
nervous, and blessed with a nice, above the
average, mezzo-soprano. Another girl was one
with the Duchess-novelette name of Crystal
Rienits. She submitted a soprano voice to the
poppers in “Dear Heart,” and they liked it
well enough to fetch her back for “Two’s
Company.” Yet a third newcomer was
Mr. Charles Rosenthal, from Melbourne Uni-
versity. He’s good enough to leave a large
note dumb at the Melb. Uni., for his baritone is
a really fine one and you could hear all he had
to say in the “Demon King” and “Mighty
Deep.” Mr. W. A. Walsh gave some humorous
reci tations, but he was mocked by an exasperat-
ing echo.
It is really cannibalistic the way that the
majority of our singers and players pounce upon
applause and gobble it up under the delusion that
it’s an encore. Now at the benefit concert to
Father Rouillipj, ,af leaky-boat fame, on Monday
night, the programme was cruelly prolonged
because so many of the performers, after sing-
ing their songs, ran on again directly after
they had run off. Yet the quality and quantity
of the applause didn’t warrant their re-appear-
ance. There’s no mistaking the desire of an
audience to bring the same singer back as distin-
guished from its desire to merely acknowledge the
favor she or he has just conferred. And it’s very
disheartening to the performers appearing far
down on the programme to see the audience
slipping into its wraps and streaking for its trams
when it should be waiting and watching ever,
longing and lingering yet, in its seat until the
Nashnulanthem flung it on to the footpath.
Miss Esther Kahn, who lends tier comely self
to portraiture this week, is a Londoner by birth,
and made her first public appearance as a pianiste
at the age of five. A year later she won a scholar-
ship, which took her to the Guildhall School of
Music, but her studies there were interrupted by
her parents removing to Sydney. Maestro Josef
Kretsehmann then “ discovered ” her, and whilst
under his tuition she made some successful
public appearances. Miss Kahn promises to
be a composer of merit. She has written
several pieces for, piano, organ, and voice. Four
of her pianoforte items were played by her in the
Town Hall, where M. Wiegand also introduced
three of her organ pieces. Paling’s have recently
published a charming song by Miss Kahn, “My
Lady Plucks a Red, Red Rose,” which got a
splendid reception when tenor Henry Weir sang
it the other night.
A tuneful waltz, “The Echo,” comes to me
from Paling’s, also a descriptive song, “ Two
Pictures,” words and music by Edward Finn.
Mr. Robert Richardson supplies the pretty words
to Miss Esther Kahn’s “ My Lady Plucks a Red,
Red Rose.”
Certain insurance managers are wearing black
;n their hearts for the late Mr. “Tin” Kellv.
f J
His death will take some scores of thousands out
of the funds of their societies. He made his
large fortune more as a buyer of metal (principally
tin) than as an owner of mining shares. He had
a great fancy for life-assurance, and only a short
time before his death (and he was over 70) the
M.L. of New York declined to take his life for a
proposed £5OOO. Mr. Kelly leaves a widow, three
sons and a daughter. The last has done a lot of
foreign travel and sings like a bird.
There’s an epidemic of winter sales and
“ wonderful bargains.” All the shops are making
space for their August display. Burnt - out
Hordern’s business, which is located pro. tem. at
Ex. Buildings, would give you some amusement
if you didn’t want the same piece of salvage as
another buyer. The struggle for brands plucked
from the burning shows woman to be a very
panther when her prey (scorched silk at 2d. a
yard) is in dispute. Two ladies seized the same
piece, each asserting a prior claim. Then one bit
the other’s arm to make her drop the spoil. “ How
near to fair is what is good,” writes the poet.
How nigh to God is—woman at a salvage sale.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
Her Ex. did the Vic. Artists’ Galleries last
Thursday afternoon. And she had a joke over
the going. The nice concert to accompany the
Countess’ visit was billed for 3 o’clock. So the
Lady thereupon decided to outdo the gapers, and
have her visit over before the mob arrived.
She came a little after 2 o’clock, the bored Capt.
Corbett acting as escort. President Eorde Pat-
terson met the large, cold ladyship and piloted her
around the show. In a heliotrope rig-out, and
large hat of the same bright tone, the Countess
looked even better than usual. She was in a most
active mood and scampered over the artistic
course at a bargain-sale gallop. She pointed an
irreverentdigitatoneaf ter another of the canvases,
accompanying the poke with such disjointed com-
mentary as ‘that’s nice,’ ‘and that,’ ‘O!’
‘Ol’ ‘O ! ’ in a sort of ‘ I see ’ tone,
as her mentor supplied a running fire of
information about the easel workers. She
must have had some prior knowledge of ‘Eern-
shawe,’ the hugest of the pilot’s own hangings,
for she assumed an expression of respect, and
murmured gently (as all praise should be), ‘ Yes,
it’s very fine; I like it.’ The agitated pilot
bowed his crested head. Before Geva Riddell-
Stanley’s portrait, too, the Countess paused for a
second and declared it ‘very nice,’ but in the
presence of the canvas labelled ‘My Mother’ the
frigid lady shivered. It even moved her to a
groan. And yet ‘My Mother ’ is considered by
artists a very clever picture indeed.
“Capt. Corbett, holding his hat in his hand,
far out, as a cook does a saucepan of boiling onions,
followed in Her Ex’s train, not even affecting an
inteiest in the beastly daubs, don’t you know.
When Hugh Patterson, to lighten duty for him,
pointed out some ‘good things’ among the
women’s work (over which he is enthusiastic),
tlie gallant captain let his eyes follow in the
direction indicated, and nodded his head in thanks
or approval—hard to say which. But he broke
into brightness when the Commanding Eye re-
called him to active service, and cleared the way
through a gathering shoal to put her out to sea.
“ It was sad to see the disappointed faces of the
suburban crowd and fashion flutterers who waited
in vain all the afternoon at the gallery for the
arrival of the Countess. They made no pretence
of ignoring the pictures, but sat with their backs
to them, listening to a concert, and watching the
door for the illuminating presence of Lady ’Ope-
toun. There was no one to tell them she had
come and departed, so they waited on and on, and
even when free tea came round and Miss Rose
Mason thrilled them with ‘ Beloved, it is morn,’
and the artists were there themselves in motley
garbs to be discussed, the heart of the watchers
did not rejoice, and the crowd flung itself out of
the gallery doors, with * Thwarted' writ large on
its inquisitive face.
“Even Miss Ruby Madden had enough sense
of humour to bring a camera to the opening of the
Royal Melb. Golf Links, at Sandringham, and
take her dad at the supreme moment of driving a
ball off a specially-prepared ‘tee’! She, radiant
with health and complacency, had the eye of the
camera turned on the Waxellency, with her finger
on the spring. Behind her, a dozen snap-shot
Johnnies had the eyes of their machines on the
figures of both. The Wax. raised the driver,
swung his taut body round, and whew ! missed
the shot. The disrespectful daughter doubled up
and laughed so genuinely that everyone round
about wondered what would happen after such
unusual boisterousness. The Waxellency went for
it again, but missed a second auspicious blow.
“At that opening on the swell Sandringham
links, The Wax. found an excuse for rattling off
some shrapnel jests. He beamed at the opportunity,
threw his chest out, leaned a hip on his gamp, and
wooed the winds and trees and numbed ears of the
passive golfers. ‘ These links,’ he ended with a
perorating smile, ‘ will doubtless be the means of
forming many other interesting links’—and he
shed an illuminating beam on the mixed doubles
that hedged him in. The Chiffon King and Mrs.
Sargood hostedit and let crowds enjoy their over-
flowing buffets. Senators were strewn among the
fashionable throng, and various Reps, of the
House. The Club residence is a palatial place
rearing up among the ti-tree of Sandringham.
‘ ‘ That Clarke-Cotton wedding owed its dainti-
ness, not only to fine feathers, but also to the slim
youthfulness of principals and chorus. It seems
no time since bridegroom Frank Clarke came of
age; his bride, the former Miss Cotton, though
but a young thing, is perhaps the elder of the
pair. Demure little Miss Lesley Madden (who
unconsciously made mows at the officiating par-
son’s queer intonation), shy debutante Vera
Clarke, school-girl Dorothy Cotton, and three
childish relations of the happy couple made up a
group of genuinely-girlish bridesmaids, squired
by unimpressive best-man Russell Clarke and
slim groomsman Cyril Cotton.
“ The bride wore the usual white satin, all lace
and tucked chiffon. The maids, in pink crepe,
varied their skirts with tucks and lace-insertion,
their bodices with big sailor-collars and vests of
white tucked chiffon; their straw hats ran to pink
roses and forget-me-nots, while lace and roses
trailed over their white chiffon muffs. The cere-
mony was performed by that big, handsome
athlete, the bridegroom’s cousin, Rev. Ernie
Hughes, who, by the way, is popularly credited
with forswearing matrimony till he meets ‘ a
woman whose temper is broken by fasting and
her knees calloused with prayer.’
Among the wedding guests, Miss Ruby
Madden affected light furs, pink bodice-spray,
and hat in tones of blue, green, and mauve, as
antidotes to the black gown left over from her
royal-mourning stock. Her sister, Mrs. Vallange,
appeared in pale blue, with pink roses in a Frenchy
hat. Turquoise earrings, violet velvet muffs,
ivory cloth gowns, and Venetian beads were much
m evidence in the dressy throng. S’Rupert’s
dame wore a domestic, tamed-down air. She
came in a strawberry turn-out and many pearl
dependencies, with the Cumming girls—a small
daughter and a substantial young sister being in
tow. The best dressed of all the rustling party was
Mrs. Sep. Miller, who wore a great gown of black
and white (reminiscent of the York visit), a smart
cape to match, and a pale violet hat. Her pearl
necklace (the finest in Melbourne), And strings
upon strings of the big, nobby Venetian chain
(the latest thing in jewels) helped to sheet home
her distinction.
“Another marriage with money in it was put
through very quietly on July 9th, when Miss
Sadie Green, second daughter of the well-known
dancing family, was joined in the bonds to Mr.
Jim Purves, eldest son of the K.C. The bride-
groom, only son of Mr. Purves’s wealthy first wife
{nee Grice) will, it is understood, ultimately suc-
ceed to his late mother’s valuable possessions, in-
cluding the Purves mansion in East Melb., and an
estate at Frankston. It appears that for six years
Papa Purves, who looked high for his boy, staved
off the match, coaxing the would-be bridegroom
on one plea and another to prolong his European
wanderings till some months ago the exile re-
turned, supposedly cured. Now, all of a heap,
came marriage 'by special license at the Registrar’s
Office ; family rage and reconciliation, and depar-
ture of the forgiven honeymooners for Europe, per
the Damascus (July 23rd).
“ Young Mr. Purves plighted his troth to Miss
Sadie six years ago. Despite sea separations and
plots and counter-plots, he stuck, to his, purpose
and never lost sight of his fiancee for longer than
he could help. A month ago, so runs the story,
the couple (whom the Fates were dragging
asunder) met by accident at the races. ‘What’s
going to win ? ’ asked the man, talking inanities, as
men do in time of emotional pressure. ‘ Not Man
of Honor,’ replied the lady with a good play of
wit On a favourite of the race. The meeting shortly
dispersed, but a fortnight later the pair met by
arrangement for lunch. ‘ Before we eat I want you
to marry me! ’ said the constant one. ‘ When, how,
why, what!’ gasped the Charmer. ‘Not a moment
to think ! We’ve waited for six years, we shan’t
wait any longer.’ ‘ But I haven’t time to think it
out, ’ entreated the bride-elect. ‘ Time enough to
think and tell everyone after,’ was the reply. So
a cab was procured, and the ring a little later still,
and Jim and Sadie are going to live happy ever
after. The pair left for England on Thursday.
“ Mornirigton (where the bridegroom’s mother—
Mrs. Grice—lives) rejoiced in a sort of impromptu
way when the pair went down to honeymoon
there, and a highly-decorated railway car was the
final send-off of some merry-making friends in that
summer suburb.
“There is much talk re the misfortunes of a
young lady whom a young gentleman is going to,
or was going to, marry. A young person whom
he is not going to marry heard of the proposed
wedding, and, instead of taking it out of the man,
as commonsense would have it, she put a little
whip under her cape, and went on the warpath
after the intended bride —which is ‘so like a
woman.’ The Watched-for opportunity came, and
the young lady and the young person clashed on
the Block, and fireworks ensued. A very brief
whipping terminated in mutual hysterics, and
now everybody who is anybody wants to know
how the marriage stands—on or off?
“ Boor Mrs. Goe suffered so much (she died of
cancer) that death came as a relief, though her loss
is a sad blow to the frail old Bishop—a childless
Darby bereaved of his Joan. The mourner him-
self seemed the delicate one of the pair, for Mrs.
Goe had always been robust till she developed her
fatal malady. The Bishop and his kindly wife
have never put on frill, looking upon themselves
just as an energetic, supervising couple. Mrs.
Goe was most sympathetic to country-parsons’
wives, inviting their boys for holidays to'“ Bishop’s
Court ’ from town boarding-schools, &c.
“ John Deegan, wine-and-spirit merchant,
who secured the E. Melb. seat in the State
Parliament, has a sardonic smirk, a taste
for fancy vests, an intimacy with Shake-
speare, a turn for playing Shylock, an interest in
the Beef-steak Club, a love of talk, and high
esteem for the first personal pronoun. He is a
good fighter, has notions of progression, and is
extremely satisfied at annexing the seat. His
daughter Lucia warbles very prettily, and was
heard at the Town Hall with John Fuller’s ‘ Pano-
gramorama.’
“Very naive circulars are about suggesting to
bachelors who are neglected (?) in the way of
mending or darning, to put themselves in the
hands of a certain Mending and Repairing Club,
which performs such little services at a trilling re-
muneration,; but present-day Pickwicks are wary,
and Barkises are not as willing as they used to
be. Still, many women are availing themselves
of the benefits of the estimable institution. No
woman minds making a blouse, but nine out of
ten will demur at putting a stitch in a reefed
petticoat.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The successor of Jawbone Neild in N.S. W.
State Parliament is Jawbone George.
State Premiers See and Peacock are still
full of hope. They expect to catch baubles when
they gad to the coronation next year.
Should the High Commissionership go to
B. R. Wise, “ Tom ” Rose, of Argyle, may get the
N.S.W. State Attorney-Generalship.
Barnum-Powell has been presented with
“ a beautiful ‘ make-up ’ box ” by Wilson Barrett
and other mummers. Theatrical and appro-
priate !
President Lackey’s ill-health will, his
friends fear, compel him to resign the chairman-
ship of N.S.W. Leg. Council. F. B. Suttor is
looked upon as his successor.
Dan O’Connor may be a “ Soapsuds Jove,”
but he is still the most picturesque person in
N.S.W. Assembly according to the nightly re-
ferendum of the ladies’ gallery.
Mr. G. H. Cox is the father of N.S.W.
Leg. Council, having been appointed in 1863.
Mr. Henry Clarke is father of the Leg. Assembly,
having been first elected 25 years ago.
Two or three baldheads asked what the
world was coming to when Socialist S. A. Rosa
moved “Jack” Want into the chair at the Hotel
Australia anti-Commonwealth meeting of Thurs-
day week.
Worth noting that Ashton, Edden, J. C.
L. Fitzpatrick, Haynes, Holman, Arthur Griffith
and Neilson, who all consistently opposed the
S’African war, were triumphantly returned to
N.S.W. Assembly.
Three brothers Hogan joined Maoriland
contingents. Two were killed in S’Africa, the
third was wounded in seven places, but got away
with his life, only to succumb to pneumonia on
the return voyage.
Bruce Smith, M.H.R., for a long time
boasted the biggest leg in Australia. But t’other
day he met his match in a Scotch Victorian, who
beat him by a good half-inch. “Must be de-
formed,” whispered B.S. to the ladies.
Let Peacock think of it and writhe—the
policeman who slept on the Duke’s door-mat at
Adelaide got an autographed photo, too ! Pre-
sumably, therefore, Peacock and the policeman
have been put away in the same cell of the royal
brain.

In the high fever of the N.S.W. State
elections Works Minister O’Sullivan put up a
record. One night, between 8 and 9, he addressed
two well-separated metropolitan meetings and
managed to catch the mail-train to his country
constituency.
Mac. Paterson still hangs on to the North
Brisbane State seat, although he has been an
M.H.R. for months. It isn’t money matters that
worry him, anyhow. He is Tyson’s executor, and
the per cent, allowed him means the trifle of
about £BO,OOO.
Apropos the suggestion that the leader
of the Federal Opposition should be paid a salary—-
say £lOOO a year. Printed in the dailies : “ Owing

Reid will not be able to attend the House of
Representatives this week.” ’
At Gowra (N.S.W.), the other day, an old-
age pension was granted to John Tom, who per-
sists in a claim —officially disallowed —to have
been the first finder of gold in Australia. Mr.
Tom, at all events, possesses the first nugget
found ,on the Lewis Ponds field.
An Adelaide weekly took the damper for
loyal photographs during the Royal visitation.
Two represented the Duchess under slightly
different conditions, and were labelled respec-
tively—“ Her Highness going in to Lunch’’and
“ Her Highness after Lunch.”
Miss Mary Dunmore Lang, daughter of
the venerated Dr. Lang, the mention of whose
name quickens the blood in every patriotic Aus-
tralian, refused to kiss the Bible as witness in a
police-court case in Sydney last week. She was
allowed to swear in old Covenanter style.
Griffith’s translation to the Federal
Bench means a vacanoy on Queensland Supreme
Court roster that should go to Paul or Noel,
capable minor-court Judges. But as both are sus-
pected of Brains and Democracy S’Onah, in esse,
will most likely be the usual style of thing.
The old lady in the country who, during
the Royal visit to Sydney, presented the Duchess
with a handsomely-worked nightdress, and got a
Testament in return, has since been burnt to death
in her bed. “The men of the village made
strenuous efforts to save the Testament, but with-
out success.”
Sydney E. News refers to “ the much-
quoted philosophy of W. Abdul Sulieman ”
(strong flavor of Solomon !), “the S’African Josh
Billings,” and actually prints, as gems thereof, a
garbled version of one of Charles Lamb’s best-
known quips, and a number of Edward Dyson’s
Bulletin apothegms.
The latest of the pioneers to “go over ” is
Stephen Norman, one of the first employees of the
late Edwd. Henty, at Portland (Vic. ) over 70 years
ago. He had allegedly reached his 107th year.
Though not a rolling stone, having always resided
in the same district since the day of his landing,
he amassed no money.
The Bulletin hears that a lady who was
formerly a prophetess among “ Christian Scien-
tists,” and author of one of their principal books,
is now a confirmed invalid in London. She has a
spinal trouble, which the best Christian Science
healers have been unable to cure, and she has now
condescended to take treatment at Continental
spas and be attended by first-class medicos.
The Commonwealth Senate should stand
well for average height among the Legislatures of
the world. There is not a little man in it. Zeal
and the sturdy Sargood, of Vic.,, are the two
shortest, and they are each over sft. 7in., while
no less than 13 out of 36 members range from
sft. llin. to 6ft. and upwards—considerably up-
wards. These are O’Connor, Neild, Higgs,
Drake, Dawson, Symon, Playford, Baker, Keat-
ing, Clemons, Harney, Pearce and Smith.
None of these are Victorians, Best, with
sft. Bin., being the tallest of the Southern-
ers. It is a commentary on the eternal
allegation that hot climates beget degen-
eracy that nearly all these big men are
Australian born, and that the majority of
them hail from its hottest parts. The Min-
istry, by the way, has nothing to be
ashamed of in physique, and every one of
its active members is native to the soil.
The nation that starts with cheap meat,
a superabundance of territory and sea air
for its capital cities ought to breed men
and women. But—alas !—a Parliament of
Australian-born girls would not be a very
noble chested gathering—unless they ail
came from Tasmania.

“There is one other terrible disease,”
said King Edward, speaking to the delegates of
the Tuberculosis Conference. “I refer to cancer.
God grant that before long you will be able to
find a cure, or check its course ! ” One touch of
Nature! The King’s brother Alfred lately died
of cancer, and now his sister, the Dowager Ger-
man Empress, is tortured by that merciless
malady.

King O’Malley’s blatherish remark in the
House of Reps, that his great ambition was “ that
the Commonwealth should found a mighty city
between the equator and the rising sun,” recalled
the historic 4th of July orator Our Mighty
Republic is bounded on the North by the Aurora
Borealis, on the South by the Procession of the
Equinoxes, on the East by the Rising Sun, and on
the West by the Day of Judgment.”
Symon’s accession to Federal politics must
have already cost him a lot in loss of legal prac-
tice. Bike Downer and Glynn, Symon travels to
and from Melbourne every week to do legal work,
but the distance is too great. Glynn recently
caught a severe cold in the train returning to
Melbourne from defending in a criminal libel-
case, and was in bed for six weeks. The absence
of big guns has already greatly benefitted the
juniors of the pious city.
“P.S.’ (“a Boer correspondent”), who
is allowed by the Morning Post (London’s most
powerful snob organ) to suggest in its columns
that all Boers found in arms after a certain near
date shall be hanged, is given out to be Philip
Schreiner, brother of the gifted and chivalrous
Olive Schreiner, Well, sometimes it is expedient
to murder the man you are robbing if he fights
too hard. But that a woman like Olive Schreiner
and a cold-blooded fiend like “P.S.” could have
been given to the world by the same mother is
surely impossible.
“ The latest story about the King,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “is so
‘ rich ’ that it matters little as to its truth.
Edward VII., desiring to make a present, called
upon the selective taste and services of the
Countess of to buy a diamond necklace not
to exceed £lOOO in cost. The Countess thrilled
and with worldly wisdom saw that the gift must
be for herself. The £lOOO necklaces in Bond-
street were fine, but one at £2OOO pleased her aris-
tocratic eye more, so she personally paid the 100
per cent, difference and submitted the gemmed
circlet for His Majesty’s approval. H.M. ap-
proved so much that he gave it to Madame G., one
of his German connections. Now the C. of
wants to know how to get her £lOOO back without
insulting the Royal generosity.”
Died at Sydney, aged 71, that popular
citizen, the kind and cheery Thomas Hussey
Kelly, familiarly known aforetime as “Tin”
Kelly, because of the tin-smelting business in
which he laid a substantial foundation for his
fortune. Mr. Kelly came to N.S.W. from the old
country as a young man and moneyless, and he
long occupied a comparatively humble position in
the establishment of a great Sydney shipping
firm. Then he struck out for himself most success-
fully, acquiring interests in many valuable pro-
perties, including Great Cobar copper-mine, the
last of his shares in which he lately sold for over
£50,000. He no doubt died extremely wealthy,
aud his life is said to have been the most heavily-
assured (£00,000) in Australasia. Unlike many
rich men lie leaves a host of friends among the
poor, to whom he was unostentatiously generous.
The trouble over the Findley business in
Vic. Parliament recalls the row raised by the Fat
class to whom the word “ loyalty ” is synonymous
with all their stolen privileges, when A. T. Clark,
prc rietor of Williamstown Advertiser, was
held guilty of having spoken disrespectfully of
Queen Victoria’s personal appearance. This was
in the days when Berry was a force in Vio.
politics. Clark was in Berry’s Cabinet
and the Fat Stock seized upon his “disloyal
utterance,” and howled it by night and day, with
the object of bouncing Clark out of politics. A
chinless crank travelled all the way from Adelaide
with the object of horsewhipping the Minister in
the street, and did try, but Clark was a powerful
six-footer, and put his hoof down on his assailant
“with a firm hand.” For long Clark was made
the object of numberless petty persecutions, and
never heard the last of “the scurrilous abuse” till
the day of his death, although he personally had
nothing to do with the disrespectful remark in
question.
A Sydney E. News correspondent' remi-
nisces concerning Olive Schreiner %
I little thought, when I first met Olive Schreiner, just
after the publication of her first book, “"The Story of a
South African Farm,” that she would develop into the
woman who seems to set everything at defiance. She was
everything that was lovable to men and women. “ Olive,”
as she was universally called by all her friends, was always
fond of seclusion when thinking out a book, and some-
times went into a convent near London for weeks at a
time ; but, eccentric as she is, she Would not seek the
seclusion offered by a wire-fence prison—even for “ copy.”
When Olive Schreiner went out of public life to devote
herself to ostrich farming, little did she think that Within
a few years fate would have given her a taste of life within
wire fences, too. She was a beautiful girl when about 19,
and wore the daintiest and most original of dresses, which
I believe she designed herself. At the time I speak of
everyone quoted her, everyone sought her ; in fact, she
was the “ lioness ” of the hour.
No one having met her could ever forget her —the
dark, deep eyes, whose sombre depths were most
often seen; but at times, when she was gay, they
flashed and glowed like fire. Her face was dark
almost to brownness, but a rare, lovely color lit it
up at any excitement, and, with her dark mass of hair,
and firm, rounded features, she was most original and
attractive. Her mouth was a feature that certainly gave
promise of much self-will —straight, full-lipped, and deter
mined; but I have seen it exquisitely tender, too.
“ Olive’s” voice was deep, and curiously vibrant, so that
whoever was talking in the room you could hear her
voice quite clearly, although she never spoke loud ; and
things she said seemed to linger in your ears for years.
My memory of “ Olive Schreiner,” as I used to see her at
our house in St. John’s Wood, is one of a sweet,
lovable, artistic, spirited girl, who bore her honors
very meekly, and seemed to have no desire except
to pour her soul into her books. One winter’s
day I walked across London with her, and it seems only
yesterday. The white world all about us, above and
below, the flakes falling fast, the crackling snow under-
foot, and “ Olive,” in her dark, rich furs, walking briskly
and talking of her country and its glory, her eyes spark-
ling as she spoke of riding over the wide veldt in the sun-
rise, the majestic mountains, the splendid men and
women who worked always, and never complained, the
gorgeous scenery and bracing air. I asked if she was
glad her book was such a success, and she replied,
dreamily, “ 1 had to write it.” I shall never forget how
radiant a picture she made that day, with the red sunset
behind the white park trees, her furs white-flecked, and
her face glowing with life and enthusiasm. I know there
was no one in trouble who would not tell “ Oii.ve,” no
child who did not love her, no animal that did not know
ithadfoundafriend.no man or woman who wanted a
helping hand who was refused, no one who was not
better for her company.
She was subject to deep melancholy at times, but
she generally kept to herself at such times, and
many used to think that she was even more attrac-
tive like this, when her face became sombre - and
stern and tragic, more like she is now. People have
FORGOT THAT
SHE WAS DEAD.
BUT CORRECTED
HIMSELF.

spoken of her as an hysterical woman, but I never saw
the least signs of it when I knew her, and I saw her
very often. I have heard from personal friends that
“ Waldo ” and “ Lyndall ” in the “ African Farm ” repre-
sent herself to a great extent; knowing her, I should say
this was so- Many a time have I seen her the heart and
life of a large assembly of some of the most noted people
in literature and art in London, ev’eryOne loving and ad-
miring her, she herself as unconscious as a child of having
done or been anything worthy of notice. She was sedate
and dignified, in spite of her lack of inches and her
plumpness, and no picture I have ever seen has given any
idea of her quaint, picturesque figure and mobile faee.
This is simply a reminiscence of “ Olive Schreiner ” as I
knew her when she first appeared in London, and wrote
her first remarkable book. I remember how astonished
everyone was when she married and settled down to a
quiet life, but it did not Inst long, you see. I knew a
woman with those eyes, and that 'mouth, could not
devote herself for ever to ostriches and a husband. Not
having seen or heard of her personally for years, I can
say nothing of her later life, but as a beauty, a girl of
character, a Charming companion, a marked! v clever girl,
a girl of bewitching gowns, a girl original and versatile’
I idolised her.
MRS. KRUGER.
Not wrapped with purple, crowned with pride,
But plain and broken, marked with toil,
The mother of a nation died,
Who was a daughter of the soil.
Now with the simple men and grand
Who died for Freedom, young and old,
She clasps that share of their dear land
The greedy foe could not withhold.

No sooner had Royalty left South Maori-
land than down came the snow Tt was the
land. than down came the snow. It was the
heaviest fall for 40 years, but in one way it was a
God-send to the farmers, by enabling them to
cope with the imported small-bird nuisance.
Digging shallow trendies in the snow, they put
down long lines of sorrel-seed, and, when the
, • , u ,°, ,i j I
birds settled in thousands to the food, they were
blunderbussed wholesale from one end. At
Oamaru one shot killed thus 120 birds—the morn-
ing’s work totalling M 0 head for one gun.

“ Hawer : The aboriginal monarch—even
the beery kind that pervades the town in a
wreoked top-hat—appears to maintain some kind
of jurisdiction amonV his subjects. I recently
saw one not only stop a furious disagreement
between two subordinate aboriginals, but fine
them threepence for insurrection—it seemed to be
all the money they had about them—and drink
the indemnity before their very eyes.

Writes Gippsland MERCURY :
Near Foster on Good Friday a sermon was preached in
a large gum tree to a congregation of 25 people, seating
accommodation being provided for 50. The heart of the
tree had been burnt out, and 20ft. from the ground the
stem had been cut through and a root erected. The tree
has a diameter of 22ft-

“A.P.R.”: Chanced on a gory battle-
field t’other day. Several dead ants lay about, vari-
ously maimed, and five live ones were still at it.
Two of these, dusty and crippled, struggled on
feebly, while a third, completely disabled, gripped
a blade of grass with malicious intent. The other
two fought on until one succeeded in sawing his
enemy in halves. He then cursorily inspected
the dead, and wandered away in a lop-sided, un-
certain manner. These ants wore of a dark-green
hue, and about long.

has anything in it, how is it that one ant is always

Re Narrabri (N.S.W.) selectors’ residence
dispute (8., 29/G/’01). Near Donald (Vic.) two
selections (forming the mile), have but one home-
stead between them. The original occupants
(married) pegged the blocks 30 years ago, and,
confronted with the residence-clause, built the
matrimonial nest square on the division-line.
Forfeiture quashed.
* * * *
The struggle for a crust is razor-like even
on the ocean-floor. “Jack” Hamilton, M.L.A.
for Cook, N.Q., tells this of Thursday Island
pearling grounds :
“ There are two classes of divers—those with the dress
going down 30 fathoms or more, the air being pumped
into the dress ; and the naked diver who works down to a
limit of six fathoms only. Some times the naked wretch
strikes a patch of shell, and this being bruited around,
along comes the “ dress ” man to annex, whereupon the
naked diver stirs up mud and sand to obscure the locality.
In revenge the men with the pump throw fat meat into
the water to attract the dreaded white shark, and thus
get the original prospector “ scoffed.” But sometimes
the shark tackles the dress-diver, who has to cut the air-
line at his wrist, the rush of water and air scaring the fish,
and sometimes finishing the Jap.

A cow in line condition, slaughtered
at Ashburton (M.L.) lately, yielded a handsome
lot of carpenter’s requisites—2o 2Jin. nails, 17
2in. nails, 18 nails, 15 tacks, 3 screws, 1
washer, 6 pieces wire and 5 staples. The animal
was apparently not inconvenienced by the nails
that had worked into the stomach-coating.

“ C.G.E.” : Have watched the gradual
change in the plumage of a sparrow that lives in
my garden. In less than three months the bird
has altered from the ordinary brown to pure
white. Well known that the North American
ptarmigan changes color on the approach of
winter, but in the ordinary house-sparrow the
change is certainly irregular.

‘ ‘ Orni ” : In some parts of Victoria great
ravages have been made on the lambs by hawks
this season. On June 6, near Seymour, a well-
known pigeon-shot brought down with a rifle at
200yds. a hawk which measured over 7ft. from tip
to tip of its wings. As big as the largest known
eagle. [The “eagle-hawk,” which is probably
here meant, is a true eagle.]

“ Wat T.” : Extract from a sermon de-
livered recently by a N.S.W. country-town
preacher:
I went to my daughter’s and I saw a fine, fat, rosy little
bov. I went again and I saw the same child, but he was
pale and thin, and I said, “ Susan, Susan,” says I, “ that
child has worms.” Then, in a voice of thunder, pointing
to the congregation, “ You have worms, my brethren !
spiritual worms 1 You’re fed-up on the Word ; and what
good does it do you ? No more than it does to feed-up a
’ide-bound ’orse on corn and chaff! ”
A Maoriland naturalist has written
to the London Spectator about a remark-
able tish—found pretty well all over the
North Island—which is called by trie
Maoris “ kakawai,” and is found buried a
foot or so in the earth. When exhumed it
is apparently dead, and, if immersed in
water, should the season be summer,
speedily does die; though if dug up in
May or J une, when the autumnal rains
keep the soil moist, and then placed in
water, it, within a few days, casts its
skin and becomes very lively. It is 2, to
3in. long, of silvery sheen and tapering
shape.
, » * *
“ S.F.” : Nothing new under the
sun. To “milk” a venomous snake’s
poison-glands, the “beastie” is “ pronged
close up to the head, and made to strike a
small disc of stretched parchment, on the
opposite side of which is a sterilised glass
receptacle. The “ little tambourine is
perforated by the striking fang and the
poison, at the same time ejected, is caught
by the waiting watch-glass. Now, from
immemorial times, Indian snake-charmers
have been using practically the same pro-
cess, the reptile being made to strike
through a leaf.

A man from Roma (Q.) district writes to
say that ha has lately met the champion mean
man. Said man lent one of his employees a gun
with which to shoot some ducks, a slow horse to
carry him to the lagoon where the ducks usually
hung out, and one rusty spur—he hadn’t a pair—
to make the horse go. And later that young man
found these three deductions from his cheque:
“ Loan of gun, Is.; loan of horse, 13.; loan of
spur, 3d.”

i “ Meera ” : One Saturday night, a few
years ago, sundry Kanakas, returning home from
a pub on the Lower Clarence, had the father of a
row on the road. Next morning I saw Billy Lifu
searching for something at the scene of the shindy,
and asked what he was seeking. “My finger,”
answered Billy, holding up a hand wrapped in a
blood-stained rag. Billy explained that in the
misunderstanding of the previous night Tommy
Tanna had got his finger in his mouth and bitten
it off. “Perhaps Tommy swallowed it,” I sug-
gested. “ Oh, no,” replied Billy, “ I ask him, and
e say he spit it out.” The finger was found,
whereat Billy appeared much pleased. I inquired
if this unfortunate episode would lead to ill-feeling
between him and Tommy. Billy said that as
Tommy spat the finger out honorably there
would be no break in their friendship, but he
gave me to understand that if Tommy had swal-
lowed the finger the rift in the lute would have
been something considerable.
* * * *
Interesting squabble on a Northern lugger
—off Thursday Island. A Jap. seaman com-
plained that the South Sea cook didn’t cut up the
turtle small enough for hi 3 fancy. Hereupon
Billy Mallicollo retorted by calling the brown
man a Chinaman, and the Jap. retaliated by sum-
marising Billy as a “Binghi Kanaka,” which, it
seems, is a curse of great tonnage. The Poly-
nesian knife here slipped into the Jap. stomach,
and also described a queer little eccentric round
the back of a second Jap. who interfered, and all
was rage and blood and heathen imprecation.
Practically no crew remained when the row was
over.

“ K.W.” : While shearing at a station on
the banks of the Rangitata (M.L.) I often, in the
THE LATE MRS. MARY STODDARD, ARTIST.

evening, would go bobbing for eels down the
river. One night I caught one about 2ft. in

doubled up, and within a few inches of the length
of the one that had swallowed it. Would like to
know if an eel can swallow another eel larger than
himself. I fancy he could, with a slight strain.

“ Riverina ” : Be that par. (B. 25/5/T)
about the drunk receiving the gun to shoot him-
self. That sort of thing is fairly common in the
bush. I saw one man struggling with his mates
to get free to drown himself (so lie said). After a
great deal of pulling and shoving, one of the
mates, sick of it all, told the others to let the
would-be suicide “drown his blessed self. 1 ’ The
others let go. The released one stood foolishly
for a while, then, sullenly muttering “Blanky
fine mates to let a bloke drown himself,” he went
to the hut and “turned in.” But the drunk of
your par. was not such a resourceful gentleman as
one I heard of. This beery person, at his own
urgent request, got the loan of a gun to shoot
himself with, went round the back, sneaked to the
only other pub. in the township and pawned the
gun for 53. Then the spree went on.
“Nick”: A queer fish was killed at
Eden (Twofold Bay, N.S.W.) t’other day. It
was about 10ft. long, and much like a shark, but
had no teeth. The whalers pronounced it to be a
“ Bone Shark, ” so-called from having in its gills
layers of a whalebone-like substance. In its in-
terior was found a quantity of “ whale-feed.” Can
any Bulletin reader enlighten ?

“ Xylo ”: Re persistence of vitality. In
Victoria recently a spaniel fell down a shaft 84ft.
deep, and after lying there for 20 days he had
vitality enough left to attract attention by whines.
He was rescued. Also, about the same time, at
Warrnambool, two sheep remained for 18 days on
a narrow ledge of cliff overhanging the sea, un-
able to go up or down; and exposed to very severe
weather. They were also rescued in surprisingly
good condition.

“ A.P.R.” : Queer that some svvaggies
don’t take to peddling. The Asiatics, who
monopolise this trade, are mostly cheerful fellows
eternally .getting “bushed.” When re-directed
they smilingly waddle off along the track which
they were expressly told not to follow. The
colored hawker is an appreciative butt of the
rouseabout’s idiotic jokes, and to avoid mistakes
he grins incessantly, even at one’s most serious
remarks. Still, they prosper, and despite their
capacity for getting lost, they don’t seem to die
on the track nearly so often as mij, bt be expected.
* * * *
“ Will J.” : Water-carting methods in the
Vic. mallee are curious at any time, but one I
struck recently was something extra. The vessel
used was a barrel, closed at both ends and filled
through the bunghole. A kind of axle projected
at each end ; the thing was mounted on two old
buggy wheels; traces were attached to these
again, and the owner was his own horse. He had
no stock, he said—everything, including his cart,
gone to the dogs. But he seemed delighted with
the success of his apparatus, and asked me what 1
thought of it. “I shall ’ave to be careful of this
drop o’ green stuff, though,” he added; “no
washing or anything o’ that sort. ”

“ Scutum ” : A one-time Glasgow miner
was induced to take up a selection on a branch
of the Wilson River (N.S.W.). The writer,
passing the place some time after,, saw the
selector, armed with a large stick, chasing a horse
up and dow n a little paddock near the road. The
grass in the paddock was what is generally known
as “poverty grass.” “What’s up, mate?” he
asked. “ Horse got the colic or a lazy fit ? ” “I
can’t make out what’s the matter with him, ’’ said
the whilom miner; “he won’t eat this beautiful
grass, and I’m trying to make him hungry.” Said
he had never owned a horse before, and had
always been accustomed to working underground.
The selection is now deserted. >

“ Frey ” : Can
any Bulletin reader
tell me where the
hawksbill turtle (that
supplies the “tor-
toiseshell” of com-
merce) lays its eggs ?
Although fairly numer-
ous on this (Port Darwin)
coast, I have never caught one laying,
though I have seen the green turtle and the
thin-shelled turtle depositing their eggs on the
sandy beaches.
At a N.S.W. coastal station a supersti-
tious Scotchman, with more than a passing
belief in mermaids, had charge. One day
a friend drew his attention to one of these
rare animals in the water some distance out.
Mac. saw it distinctly through his glass, and
ran like a madman down to the boathouse.
With two men he got the boat out, and set
forth to make a capture. As they rounded
a rocky point they came in full sight of
something white topped by a female face
draped with dripping locks of hair. The
floating animal evidently expeoted the boat
to pass; but when it turned right towards
her, while its skipper’s finger was pointed
straig. !• in her direction, she made towards
the rocks at a great pace. The two boatmen
pulled stolidly according to orders, and a
desperate race ensued between them and
the specimen ; but the specimen got there
first, and fled on two dstinctly human legs
along the shore. She was the daughter of an
adjacent land-owner, and the pursuit had
headed her off and forced her to land fully
200 yards from her boots and other pro-
perties. As Mac. hadn’t sense enough
explain that he saw a" large shark not far
off, and had rushed out to save an undressed
fellow-creature, there was a vacancy there-
abouts just as soon as an enraged and influ-
ential father had time to wire particu 1
of the outrage to somebody in authority.
* * * *
D car Bulletin.— Your correspondent,
“Bright” (8., 18/5/’01) is, I think, barking
up the wrong Lee, or the tree is barking up
the wrong correspondent, when he states that
ring-barking is the “ real cause of the dying
away of boree and myall trees.” I have
noticed many places where these trees died
off, long before ring-barking was in general
vogue as at present. Also in stating that
trees draw rain he is taking the cause for the
effect, as rain most certainly draws up or pro-
duces trees. Witness the dense forests in
countries where there is a considerable rain-
fall, for instance on the West Coast of Tas-
mania, Gippsland, and the Far North of Aus-
tralia. But in the arid interior of our con-
tinent there is hardly any respectable timber
except on the watercourses; and rain, when
it deigns to fall, does not seem to favor the
timbered country any more than the plains.
In my opinion it would be an immense benefit to
N.S.W. to utterly denude it of its useless timber
and scrub, and the sooner the better.
* * ♦ *
Peter Birr, the English horticulturist,
gives the daffodil a rest in S. Aus., and warns
that baked province that still greater bakedness
will be its portion if, instead of cutting down the
few trees it does possess, it doesn’t go in more
vigorously for planting other trees. Forests in
Australia, he reckons, would recoup their cost in
50 years, as against 70 years in Germany, where
wholesale afforestation is part of the settled policy
of the country.

A GOOD CHANCE.
URCHIN : “Hi, Sisf There's a
charnst of a pair of gloves.
Yer might get er dozen—e’s awful sound!"
Sir John Colomb proposes to move the
headquarters of the British army bodily to
S’Afrioa. Troops that are mostly stationed and
trained about the big cities of Britain, in a crowded
country of streets, roads and houses, are more or
less lost when they are turned out to fight in
empty lands of immense distances, where there
are few roads and no mile-posts, and the lost
soldier can’t refer to a policeman or a directory,
or inquire at a pub. The idea seems somewhat
foolish. The great Scotch county of Sutherland
MISS ESTHER KAHN, a Talented Australian
Pianist and Composer.
has only 10 persons to the mile—less than Viotoria;
and there are other empty wastes in the old
country for the British soldier to get lost in
without going 6,000 miles away from the place
where he may some day be the worst-wanted man
in creation.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

STANDING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ No man so deaf as he who will not hear.”
In thoughtful mood the drunk soliloquises,
But as he muses this thought ever rises,
“ No full man but could stand another beer.”
And then with pleading voice he turns to me—
“ I could stand one if you would stand,” says he.

What Is A Cold ?
It is now generally conceded that the distress-
ing complaint known as a cold is due to the
presence of a specific micro-organism. These
organisms are always in the air wherever there is
a concourse of human beings. In the purer air,
on mountains, and at sea, they are either absent
or rare. None of Nansen’s people suffered from a
cold during their prolonged- stay in the arctic
regions. There is an island off the coast of
Scotland which is but rarely visited, and its
people never have colds except when a ship is in
port, and then they are highly subject t i iem.
Inhabitants of more thickly-populated areas
probably become more or less immune from attack,
but exposure to cold and damp lowers vitality
and gives the microbes their opportunity.
The first symption of a cold is a feverish
condition. The pores become contracted and the
skin cannot do its work of removing waste pro-
ducts of the body by perspiration. In consequence
the kidneys are compelled to come to the assis-
tance of the skin and do a double, share of work.
This is demonstrated by the visible presence of
earthy salts in the urine just as soon as a cold is
contracted. The burden thus thrown upon the
kidneys is often more than they can sustain. This
is a source of great danger; in fact, nothing is
more dangerous. There is a medicine known as
Warner’s Safe Cure which possesses a remarkably
specific action upon the kidneys, stimulating them
and enabling them to bear the increased strain
thus placed upon them. Personssufferingfromcolds
cannot do better than take a course of Warner’s
Safe Cure, as any break-down of the kidneys or
their incapacity to remove the waste products of
the body will result in rheumatism, gout, indiges-
tion, blood disorders, gravel, debility, or, perhaps,
Bright’s disease. Even when any of these
disorders are contracted, so potent is the action
of Warner’s Safe Cure that it will effect a speedy
and permanent cure.*

E.P.T- : Please don’t quote Parkes as a model

money from a man and then appoint him to the Leg.

to name the men and the amounts, even now. But even
then Parkes may not have been corrupt, for he only
borrowed money from men of the self-righteous, purse-

Tommy Tanna’s sins away.

-Even in the matter of vermin rivalry exists
between Ballarat East and Ballarat West. |
The latter municipality, believing that there ’
is now no danger of bubonic plague, have sus-
pended the payment of bonuses for the des-
traction of rats. Ballarat East magnates are '
urged to follow suit, on the ground that the
ratepayers in that historic quarter will suffer
"another injustice” in having to kill all .
rodents foolish enough to cross the Yarrowee 5
from the city. In short, it is regarded as de-
cidedly "unfair” that Ballarat East should
have to slaughter Ballarat West’ rats free of
1 charge. The wise men in the East and the
autocratio men in the West are at last yearn-
ing for the long-talked-of amalgamation of
these two municipalities, in the hope of wiping
out for ever the pettifogging jealousies that
for many years characterised the dealings of
# the two districts.
A petition was presented (fo the House of
Representatives yesterday by Mr. Piesso
(Tasmania), from, the Women’s Christian
’Temperance Union of Australasia, praying
“on behalf of the mothers of the Common-
wealth, and in the interests of their sons, who
have so freely been given to protect Aus-
tralia.’* that the Federat Defence Bill should
be so amended as prohibit the sale of in-
toxicating drinks at any of the military
canteens throughout Australia.

Measles are very prevalent amongst the
constables stationed at Russell-street. Alto-
gether, about 14 or 15 constables are laid up
Suffering from measles. Yesterday four con-
stables who had been attacked were admitted
to the police hospital, St. Kilda-road, within
an hour.

RAILWAY STATION,
SYDNEY.


The Pinnacles, the mine of varied experiences a
few miles out from Broken Hill —it owned a smelter
before B.H. Prop, had one—is now being worked by
the Queensland Smelting Co., and is more prosperous than
ever it was when the last co. (a Sydney venture) had it.
Spite of depressed markets a profit is being made, and
there is 12 months’ supply of newly-found ore in sight.
» * * *
Broken Hill’s exports for the January-June
half-year totalled £1,038,817 ; corresponding half of ’9O, i
£1,103,572. The lead slump was responsible for the
decline. Altogether 197,094 tons were sent over the line
to port; of this 38,834 tons were crude sulphide ore and
136,838 tons concentrates, the latter representing about
850,000 tons of ore. Total output of ore from the field
may therefore be set down at 900,000 tons for the six
months. A couple of by-results of the lead slump are
worth noting. The export of carbonate of lead (the very
best of lead flux, of which only small quantities remain)
has been trebled in the effort to keep the divs, from sink-
ing to zero; while the export of “ zinc concentrates ”
(the poorest of the metal products, of which vast quanti-
ties remain) has absolutely ceased. But in the aggregate
there is still a big margin between cost of production
and value of output at Broken Hill. While the exports
were valued at £1,038,817, the value of the imports (and,
except as to a few sheep and cattle, everything that is
consumed in Broken Hill has to be imported) was only
£593,020. Of these imports, £46,849 worth was mining
machinery—mostly improved concentrating plant.
• • • •
Broken Hill mines have been finding that the
prodigious efforts made within the last two or three
years to do without timber —the reaction from the
'Murkan plan of transplanting a forest of softwood in the
worked-out places—doesn’t pay. And so, from £40,965,
the value of the imports in the first half of ’9O, the figures
have gone to £68,349 in the first half of ’9l.
_*♦ * - »
There is much vague talk just now«f new sul-
phide-treating processes, chiefly intended.to turn the at
present worse than valueless zinc to account. Block 10,
not seeing much fun in maintaining a huge output of its
best ore while lead is so cheap, proposes to use part of its
plant for re-treating the tailings heaviest in metal; but
details of the process are not yet given. More important
probably is the outcome of a long series of experiments
carried out by the experts of the German Metal Go.
Hitherto the co. has treated B.H. ore and tailings only by
a rather wasteful and exceedingly unhealthy dry (mag-
netic) process; now a wet process has been evolved A
French invention is also just above the horizon. But the
best of all talk —results on a commercial scale—has not
yet begun.
» * * »
Tragedy in figures. First half of 11)00, wool
Worth £46,309 passed out of far west N.S.W. thror h
Broken Hill, ditto first half of ’Ol, £15,982. Average
rainfall for the six months to June 30 over the far west
was no more than 2|in. ; and, to make matters worse for
the weak stock, the winter’s cold has been intense,
frequently falling below freezing point. So the figures
for ’O2 wiil probably be worse again.
# * * *
The British has closed its concentrating; mill
and discharged about 450 men —all, in fact, but a few
picked men, who will continue badly-needed development
work. That, as frequently stated here, is the weakness
in this mine’s position. Its neighbor, Block 14, though
but very little of its ore is rich, has a good assortment of
it, including fairly large reserves of easily-smelted car-
bonates; so it will probably hang on, even with lead at
£l2 or a fraction lower. The British would have been
shut down some weeks ago had it not been for the
promptings of the London board ; th.e Adelaide directors
have for some two or three months recognised that the
ore was being picked without hope of profit.
Captain Warren, than whom there are few
better miners in Australia, has been re-appointed general
manager of Block 10, which position he lately resigned
after 13 years’ service. By the way, Mr. Warren is the
only B.H. manager left who is “ captained.” The modern
school-trained man won’t stand it.
* * * *
“ Northerner ” thus savagely curses Chillagoe :
“Re mining in Chillagoe. A man may spend £lO to £2O
coming here, be offered a contract at a price which has
forced some other party of men to give it up through
being unable to make wages, and if the new min refuses,
the chances are that he won’t get another show. If a
contract be unfinished the co. retains 25 per cent, of the
value of the work done, with the right to deduct a fur-
ther 10 per cent, if payment is demanded before monthly
pay-day. The co. can also—according to terms of agree-
ment—cut the price, sack the men, or stop the contract,
without giving reasons. All the shafts are wet and hot
and unventilated. Men are half-time idle ; contracts are
not let for more than 50ft., and a cut in the price is almost
certain on a further contract. Also, you pay 15s. for
501 b. of flour, 11s. for a pair of working boots, and 7s. 6d.
for dungaree trousers (too hot for moles). No vegetables
to be had, and the water-supply (coming from shafts
which are being worked) is highly impregnated with lead
and copper—to say nothing of perspiration. And with
all this joy, the co. offers contracts at which, by hard
bullocking, you may make 10s. per shift. There’s hardly
a man in the place who has been here 12 months, and
miners in search of work should steer clear of Chillagoe,
with its Kanaka-tucker, cruel heat, fevers and agues, star-
vation wages and complicated sliding-scale contracts.”
The liquidators of the London and Globe
Finance Corporation report that they find it impossible
to at present issue a reconstruction scheme, but they
hope that such will ultimately become possible. The
report also states that the liquidators found practically
the whole of the securities pledged to secure the £600,000
advanced, and there were loans still outstanding amount-
ing to £425,000, while the claims of unsecured creditors
exceed a million sterling. No doubt the bulk of this
money was used in rigging Lake Views.
* y« * •
London and Globe: At the collapse Whittaker
Wright said “ he had hoped to retire to be free from these
great responsibilities, but he pledged his health, his
strength, his life and his private fortune to carry this
enterprise to a successful issue.” Now the liquidators of
the concern say that Whittaker Wright has no official
connection with the corporation, consequently has taken
no part in connection with the liquidation.
* * * *
Recent coal-finds near Port Clinton, Broad-
sound, Q., begin to attract attention. If said discoveries
are nearly as valuable as represented and as close to good
navigable water as alleged, the Oallide Syndicators at
Gladstone, Q., will have some nasty sand in their eyes.
Oallide people have about 70 miles of railway to build,
over fairly rough country, a job that is not unlikely to
cost £300,000. After interest, salaries, wages, wear and
tear are provided for, the output will have to be some-
thing extraordinary to pay “ divs.”
* * * *
S. Australia’s latest new goldfield is three or
four miles out from Olary, on Broken Hill line. The
field was first exploitd 16 years ago, was favorably re-
ported on, served as a peg for several syndicates, and
was then abandoned. The present workings are, how-
ever, new ; and as Broken Hill men are taking a good
deal of interest, the place may be explored a bit more
methodically than most S.A. rushes have been. Remark-
ably fine specimens have been found ; but there is
nothing so far in the shape of defined lode or reef. The
gold, coarse and rough, occurs in the cavities of a con-
glomerate, which also contains some very beautiful cry-
stals.
* * • «
Bendigo has a world-wide reputation for good,
plucky and economical mining. Its latest achievement
is this : The Thomas United co. at Fosterville is crushing
divs. out of stone that yields only 26grs. per load. It
breaks, hauls and crushes for a fraction over 3s. 2d. a
load, working ordinary hours and paying the usual un«m
rates of wages. At the beginning of last half-year it had
a credit-balance of £436. Then it crushed 9350 loads for
4700z5., dealt out £6OO in divs. and started the current
term with £39 in hand. Whilst this stone is all coming
from above water-level the co. is prospecting for some-
, thing better below.
* * * *
I Cuprite Tin, Watsonville, Q. : “Face all in
' tin-country, small blobs of good stone, soakage increased,
ground much harder, both good indications. North
drive—no increase of tin face; no more quartzite, a
favorable indication.” It would be equally interesting to
know what would be regarded as an unfavorable indica-
tion.
Wore Them Out Herself.
There was a time when Mrs. Hayes considered
herself to be what she calls “a gone woman.”
She actually divided her clothes and other
personal effects among her children. Thank
goodness—but here is her story, told in her
own way; by all odds the best way.
“ Three years ago,” she says, “I had dreadful
pains across the left side of my stomach and under
the shoulder-blades. My left side swelled up
fearfully. I was laid up weeks at a time, work
being out of the question. While these fits were
on I could neither walk, sit, nor stand with
comfort.
“ 1 was really ashamed to let the neighbours
see me crawling about; so I spent most of my
time lying down or leaning against something to
ease the dreadful pains.
“ I had been a hard-working woman all my life,
but now I lost my strength and dreaded to eat
anything, knowing the woeful suffering I was
sure to experience afterwards; as if eating were
somehow a crime against the laws of nature.
And at night I rolled and tossed about instead of
sleeping.
“The doctor said it was indigestion, and-no
doubt he was right, but he was not able to relieve
me.
“I considered myself a ‘gone woman’ and told
my husband I was sure I could not last much
longer. Indeed, I was so fully persuaded of this,
that I actually divided my clothes and personal
effects among my children.
“ Thank goodness and Mother Seigel’s Syrup
I have since worn out most of them myself.
“After a lot of coaxing and argument (for I
was tired of trying things, and hope had about
died away in my heart) I consented to take
Seigel’s Syrup, although the doctor had advised
me not to touch it.
“ I was not quite sure of the effect of the fir
bottle, but my husband insisted on my going on
with it. So 1 did go on with it, and after I had
got through half the second bottle there was no
doubt of the result. I was much better; I felt it,
and others could See it.
“It was hardly short of a .miracle, the way
Seigel’s Syrup brought me round. From a poor,
weak, and wretched woman, unable to work or
scarcely to raise my hand to do the smallest
piece of work, it gave me back health and strength,
restored me to my husband and family, enabled
jne to go on with my work once more, and, in
short, made me as well aS ever I was in my life.
“ I am now upwards of 60, and ha ve reared a
large family. I have lived in the district about
37 years, and am well known here.
“My husband and sons, as well as our grand-
children, work in connection with the coal mines,
for which the district is noted, I have told all
the neighbours, what. Seigel’s Syrup did for me,
and am perfectly willing that my case should be
published if you think it may be useful. ” ( Mrs.)
Julia Hayes, Mount Keira, Paradise, near
Wollongong, N.S.W. October 14th, 1899.
Mr. John Hickey, blacksmith, at the same
place, Writes that he has known Mrs. Hayes all
his life, and (in common with many others) knows
her statement to be true. He adds that she is
respected by everyone.*
