POLITICAL
POINTS
“Is that honorable warfare 1” “ Yes, it
Is.” “ Then what is dishonorable warfare ? ” “It
is just the same.”— Victor Hugo.
It is plainer every day that C. A. Lee is
the stop-gap Leader of N.S.W. Opposition.
Sydney B.T. made him, and is now dexterously
unmaking him.
Newly-elected President Stirling is suc-
ceeded as Tory leader in S.A. Leg. Council by
wealthy squatter Buncan, an even more hardened
Conservative than his predecessor. Buncan has a
record of over 30 years of S. A. political life.
If N.S.AV. Independents, already 31 and
with alluring prospects, only had a leader with
craft and capacity, their strength might dominate
the situation. But the question is—Where is the
leader? The other parties haven’t got him, any-
how.
S.A. is only one constituency for Federal
elections as regards both Houses. It occurs to
the Jenkins Ministry, however, that a bye-election
for a solitary member will be rather costly if it
has to be taken over 903,000 sq miles, so it is
proposed to cut the State into seven electorates.
N.S.W. Premier See’s first exercise of his
power to nominate to N.S.W. Leg. Council was
on behalf of ex-Justice Minister R. G. B. Fitz-
gerald, whom the electors of Robertson have just
kicked out of his Leg. Assembly seat. Mr. Fitz-
gerald’s constituents would not give him a three
years’ seat, and See now gives him a life one.
John Norton was against Reid’s Govt., and
against I -ne’s. He journeyed down to Quean-
beyan on purpose to assist O’Sullivan, in the
recent election, on the hustings. When Parlia-
ment met John walked into the meeting called by
■the “ Independent ” or unattached members, and
no doubt influenced the opinions of that body.
Yet, when the debate on Lee’s censure-motion
was started, he rushed in to declare his “ uncom-
promising hostility ” to all past, present, and
future actions of the See Ministry, of which the
O’Sullivan is the most prominent item.
THE TOAD AND ITS PREY.
TRENWITH : Great Heaven! how the beast grows! Say, Alick, how about our own safety if we feed
him like this? ”
Baby Watt dropped about £l6O through
standing down to improve the chances of Deegan'
against Findley at E. Melbourne. Bay after
'Slection Bard Fitzgibbon started a fund with £1
and invited other Jingoes to roll in their
sovereigns to recoup the loyal infant. They rolled
to such an extent that, after a few City Council-
lors and others had made up a tenner and the Vic.
Cabinet another tenner, the matter was dropped
with a loud thud. Boes the Jingo ever pay for his
aihout ?
“ The Boers have called all boys who have
reached the age of 14 into the fighting line.”
There must be brighter fortune far
For that brave land with trials wrung
Where none too old for heroes are,
And none too young!
In Fiji, the infuriated British autocrat
who runs the show has shut down on Seddon’s
annexation scheme by issuing his large, off-hand
uka.se that anybody who says anything to any
native calculated to make said native dissatisfied
with the present form of Government shall be
gaoled for not more than six months with hard
labour. The blessings of Freedom !
“ Mr. Arthur Griffith to move : ‘ That no
greater sum than £5OOO per annum ought to be
provided by the people of New South Wales for
the payment of the salary and expenses of the
State.Governor.’” Good! Why should the Aus-
tralian States continue to be the stamping-ground
either of out-at-elbows aristocrats or of British
noblemen who wish to “ try it on the dog?”
Mr. Randal Carey, President of Sydney
8.T., in resigning from the Rocks Resumption
Board, has simply come in out of the wet. The
appointment was made subject to the ratification
of Parliament —which fact, under recently-existing
circumstances, foreshadowed much acrimonious
Parliamentary discussion. No billet, no discus-
sion—so the Major proceeded to get under the
umbrella of private life.
According to London Times, there were
over 24,000 “refugees” in the 12 camps estab-
lished by the British in Orange River Colony at
the end of May. Present cables put the numbers
at 35,000 ; and the death-rate at these camps varies
from 70 per 1000 per annum at Norval’s Point to
383 per 1000 per annum at Bloemfontein—average
of all camps over 116. Kitchener is “pacifying”
the Boers all right. More deadly than lyddite is
the camp-system.
Whafc on earth were the big Freetrade
dailies doing when they failed to suppress last
week’s cable that Britain objected to China in-
creasing her Customs duties to pay the war in-
demnity, because such a policy would virtually
saddle the biggest traders—England and America
—with the whole of the indemnity ? Said dailies
have for years been assuring their readers that the
buyer (China)—not the seller (Britain)—invariably
pays the Customs duties.
Vic. Speaker Mason refused to allow
Labor-member Prendergast, a few nights ago, to
quote from the Leader (Bavid Syme’s weekly) a
satirical estimate of Waxellency "Madden. The
paper said that evidently the way to show the
complete incapacity of a lawyer is to make him a
Judge. Mason would neither have the journal
quoted nor laid on the table. Clearly, things are
coming to a fine pace when lese Maddensty is added
to the list of offences.
Snow’s Tramway Grab Bill has been intro-
duced into S.A. Leg. Council, and is sure to go
through that Tory-bossed Chamber with ease.
It’s sponsor is Vardon, elected last year mainly
by the votes of the workers. Most of the unspeak-
able features of the original measure are still
retained, and the other great question is as to how
many politicians have been retained also. Last
year Price, M,L. A., complained that he had never
seen so much barefaced lobbying during his poli-
tical career.
THE DOG UNDER THE WAGGON.
“ The cable message which appeared in the Argus of yesterday stating that
it was rumored in London that the Salisbury Ministry proposed to give repre-
sentation in the House of Commons to the British colonies was the subject of
much conversation amongst, members of the Federal Parliament yesterday.”
John Bull (to Australian representative): “Of course, we are
profoundly grateful for the energy and patriotism you show
in hauling the Imperial State coach along! ” And yet how
delighted the Fatman type of Australian would he to he
a small portion of the dog!
It is explained by the authorities in Eng-
land that, owing to Victoria having cut down the
salary and allowances of its State Governor to
£5OOO a year, there is great difficulty in finding a
sufficiently gorgeous personage to take up
the job. The Canadian province of Ontario,
more than twice as large as Victoria, and
with nearly twice the population, pays its
Governor £2OOO a year and never has any difficulty
in filling the billet. But Canada merely imports
that gorgeous creature, the Governor-General,
from England. The State Governors are local
men, appointed by the Governor-General. The
misguided Commonwealth wants to import not
only its Gov.-General but its six State Governors
from England, and the travelling expenses of the
glittering object and his family and dependents
must be paid ; also, he needs to be compensated
for loss through leaving his affairs in Britain to a
deputy, and often for the sacrifice of a profitable
connection as a guinea-pig.
Sydney Telegraph opines that
N.S.W.’s financial statement can’t be
presented before November, because
State-Treasurer Waddell must wait till
the Federal tariff is ready, so that he may
know how much extra customs revenue he
is going to have. Otherwise he might
under-estimate the customs revenue and
have a surplus. By way of a really
shocking proposal—how would it be if
State Treasurer Waddell assumed that
the Federal customs tariff wouldn’t yield
any more than the N.S.W. State tariff
does, and provided enough money to pay
his way on that basis, and then, if there
was a surplus, he could pay off some of
the accumulated deficits with it ? It
would be contrary to all Cornstalk tra-
ditions, no doubt, but perhaps the country
could stand it for once.
Friction has arisen already between
some of the old N.S.W. roads-and-bridges
members who succeeded in entering the
Federal Parliament and their successors
in the State electorates. The old mem-
ber, who owed much of his earlier lease
of political life to the assiduity with which
he fetched and carried for his consti-
tuents, still receives applications and de-
mands from his old supporters, and in
many cases he has tried to carry them
out as heretofore. The new comer, who,
in his turn, hopes to establish a mortgage
on his seat by the line of conduct which
his predecessor found so successful,
naturally objects to this poaching. As
the State minister has no object in con-
ciliating the Federal M.H.R. and bends
a willing ear to the request of the local
M.L.A. it is easy to see that the latter
holds the right end of the stick. What
the Federal member fears is that when
he appeals for re election on broad
National lines, the parochial elector will
only remember that he was of no use in
securing a culvert over Dry Dog River,
or a grant of blankets for"the town, and
will vote accordingly.
Poor old One-Man-One- /ote again
bobs up in the Philp program ne for pre-
sent Q. session. It has been on the menu
since ’B7, and the Philp crowd will pro-
bably stump the country on it next year.
Sydney Telegraph has only two attitudes
about the Federal Government. The Govern-
ment gave way on various points re certain early
measures, and the I). T. asked Australia indig-
nantly how long this spineless mockery of a
Ministry was to be endured. Then it didn’t give
way on the Inter-State Customs Bill and one or
two other matters, and the D.T. wanted to know
how long Australia would submit to the Minis-
try’s overbearing dictation and be crushed beneath
the iron heel of despotism.
N.S.W. Labor-party
has a tough question ahead
of it—whether Ferguson
and Sleath, two of its best-
known members hitherto
(the only two who fought
for the Commonwealth Bill),
and another Barrier mem-
ber, Williams, shall be ad-
mitted to the party. All
three ran on the Labor
platform; but all of them,
disagreeing with the local
Labor bosses, were opposed
by “selected” candidates.
On the one hand is the
necessity to preserve dis-
cipline and prevent the
splitting of the Labor vote ;
and that can’t be done by
lightly forgiving anybody
who runs in opposition to
the candidate selected by
the local Labor body. On
the other hand is the doubt
whether the Parliamentary
Laborites should accept
without inquiry the decision
of every little local clique.
In the case of Ferguson and
Sleath they have been
marked men ever since the
last Federal referendum.
The party gave its mem-
bers a free hand then ; these
two interpreted that to
mean that they could figlit
for the Bill if they wanted
to, and they did it. Any-
how, the fact that these
three come from all-Labor
constituencies, and that
their election really amounts
to a popular protest against the tricks and in
trigues of the local Labor-in-politics bosses, ought
to gain for them at least an unprejudiced hearing
from the party.
Sydney Daily Telegraph seizes on the
dispute re passage of Tattersall’s-svveeps corre-
spondence as a means to fan some sort of a flame
against the doings of the Federal Parliament. It
demands to know if such an infringement of little
Tasmania’s State rights is to be permitted. Quite
so. But at the Federal Referendum the Tele-
graph was most sarcastic about the rights of the
same little Tasmania. “ Six Senators indeed ! ”
“ Let Tasmania and Victoria amalgamate and the
same representation do for both.” The paper’s
latter-day solicitude is curious reading.
What a lot of Boers (vide cables) are being
killed, and how very few are merely wounded
now ! Apropos , a private of the W.A. Sixth
Cohentingent, whose full name is given in Cool-
gardie Miner, writing from S’Africa to a Cool-
gardie friend, says :
There are about 30 columns on the march, trying to
catch General Botha, but he manages to give them the
slip somehow, or they are not trying to catch him. The'
Australians said that they won’t take prisoners. They
have made up their minds about that lot. The bayonets:
are the best for them they reckon.
Noble warriors!
How the “Patriotic Bias ” warps minds !'
George Meredith, in his “ lines on the Voyage of-
the Ophir,” in the May Pall-Mall Mag., has.
set his admirers a tougher problem than ever the
Philistines found in the “Egoist.” Banal and
Jingo commonplace ! “ Victoria’s holy name ”is
invoked as by any penny-a-liner, and Kipling at
his worst never plastered England with stickier
praise than this—“ The Power that trusts to
Love,” “Imperial means beneficence and strength
to service bowed.” What of the Boer women and
children who are dying like flies in British
laagers that Boer men may be forced to acquiesce
in the theft of their country ! Surely it is some/
impostor masquerading ? Or dotage ?
A pioneer’s grievance :
Dear Bulmtin.—About Old-Age Pensions: There’s a.
clause in the N.S.W. Act which promises to work harshly
in many cases. No one not British-born can claim a pen-
sion unless he has been naturalised ten years. There are
dozens of aged persons, 50 years and more resident in the
State, who, either from ignorance or apathy, have neg-
lected to take out papers. I know of one man, 89-
years of age, who came out in ’53. He has reared-
an Australian family, some of them now in good posi-
tions ; but he, like many others, is carefully left alone by
his children and grand-children. He is ineligible, for the
single reason that he did not worry about the naturalisa-
tion. Having no other hope but to die in the land of his
adoption, he is classed as a foreigner. Surely his case
would be met by his taking the oath now. On his merits-
few are more entitled to the blessings of the O.A.P. Act
than this old alien.—B.
It is estimated on no worse authority than,
usual that the Westralian transcontinental line
(1100 miles still to construct) would cost £220,000
a year to work ; interest on the inevitable loan for
building purposes at 3i per cent, about £175,000,
making £395,000 ; earnings, £330,000 ; loss, includ-
ing interest, £05,000 a year ; profit, if the line is
built out of revenue, and there is no interest to
pay, £llO,OOO a year. Even if it was only £50,000
a year the transcontinental would be a highly pro-
fitable line —provided it was built out of taxation,
and there was no interest to meet. It would cer-
tainly pay enormously better than jubilee drunks,.
Federal and ducal celebrations, S’African wars,
relief works, dead-houses, museums, and the like.
The calculations are based on the assumption that:
passengers are carried from Sydney to Perth ins
4| days for £l6 ss. first-class, or £lO 6s. second.

The Jenkins Government’s scheme of Parlia-
mentary reform has been coldly received by S.A.
politicians, one daily paper remarking that
many consider the proposals “ill-timed” and
think Jenkins should have “left it alone.”
The proposal to turn 26 politicians out
into the cold world is always “ill-timed.”
When Holder last year proposed a popularly -
elected Convention to re model the Constitution
Parliament said it would deal directly with the
matter “next year” (whichis now), asFederation
would then have been accomplished. Now, it
being invited to act, it has an idea that it would
be better to leave the matter to be settled by the
new Parliament which will be elected in
1902, as it will be fresh from the country
and have a “mandate from the people.”
Of course the 78 members of that body, having
spent money and perspiration in getting in, will
hardly favour abolishing themselves, and will say
that the new election which a re-arrangement of
the constituencies would involve would be a sinful
expense wh&n the country had just been at the
cost of an election already. So the J ENKINS idea
will continue “ill-timed.”
W rites a Man from Kalgoorlie :
Re the recent Westralian railway strike. Facts are:
The men affected have been asking an increase of a
shilling a day (the men on goldfields were getting Bs.
and on coast 7s. per day) for the last 15 months, first from
Forrest, then Throsse’ll, and lastly Leake. Failing to
get a definite answer (the present departmental heads
have .never been known to give anybody a definite answer)
they finally demanded a reply within 36 hours. Then the
excitement commenced. The Government said it recog-
nised Che fairness of the men’s demands and the need for
increased pay, but wanted a board to arbitrate on the
matter. Also, it wanted a month in which to classify the
men—which simply meant shelving the question for
another month. As the men didn’t want classifying,
and the need for increased pay was conceded, they
refused arbitration until the Rev. Geo. Wheatley
(after a week’s strike) joined his prayers to those of
the Government and induced them to accept. Then
a board was formed, the matter considered, and the in-
crease granted, all in a oouple of hours. The Government
nominees on the board evidently had their instructions to
grant everything with as little delay as possible—a pro-
ceeding tending to bring the principle of arbitration into
contempt in Westralia. Apropos, when the establishment
of bonded stores on the goldfields was asked for some
time back, Customs Collector Mason’s chief argument
against the proposal was that a large percentage of goods
were lost — i.e. , stolen—in transit from the codst and it
was necessary to collect the duty at Perth before the
stealing happened, or much revenue would be lost.
Well, the railwaymen’s wages were not (and are not)
nearly sufficient to enable them to live honestly. Eight
shillings a day is scarcely eriofigh to pay the butcher’s
bill in Westralia, if a family is to be properly fed.
- /, • ♦
Despite the boast of Melb. Age, Peacock
made a wretched speech in favor of the Yic. Con-
vention for the reform of the hide-bound constitu-
tion of that State (writes “Gallery-hand ” to The
Bulletin). Either Peacock’s heart was not in
the task, or he neglected to prepare himself. His
speech was a thing to delight any Opposition
leader —even the dull and dismal Irvine. There
has been strong evidence lately that Peacock is
not the fighter for liberal measures that he once
was. Something seems to have happened to
him. Whether it is the influences of his
Tory father, the Dookoyork’s photo., the Windsor
uniform, or a rapidly-increasing credit-balance
that has made this sudden alteration in the once
fighting Democrat, it is hard to say. Unless he
shows more fight than he did in moving the second
reading, he will never get the Bill through the
Assembly, to say nothing of the Leg. Council.
In his desperate anxiety to get in the
habit of sending away troops as a regular thing,
Premier Seddon of Maoriland aotually appealed
to the British Government for permanent authority
t o seize British vessels as troopships, so that con-
tingents might be despatched in all directions at
the shortest notice. The British Government,
which has had a good deal of experience at
despatching troops, replied coldly tlrat- it found
such violent devices quite unnecessary, and
seemed almost to imply that Seddon was a
bounding ass,
“ Worker ” writes :
Joseph Oook, M.H.R., was in a tight corner during the
recent N.S.W. State election. Ten years ago the workers
of Lithgow, which practically meant the Miners’ Union,
took Joe from the pit’s mouth and put him into Parlia-
ment as a Labor-member. They also paid His election
expenses, because he couldn’t pay them himself. Three
years later, seeing the chance of a portfolio, Oook severed
his connection with the Labor-party, but despite that and
a good deal of other suspicious wobbling, his old work-
mates still stuck to him. At last election, Oook being
out of State politics, his old supporters selected another
miner, Pillans, as their candidate, and expected Cook to
help his return. Sam Smith, M.P., went-to Lithgow to
speak for him, but Oook wouldn’t even take the chair
when asked. There was also a candidate from the
Liberal Association, under the wing of which Joe has
been nestling for the last few years. He wasn’t game to
take sides, so he simply stood by and watched his own
party, who had done so much for him, go down, refusing
to put in one word when they asked him to help them.
The late general secretary of the Lithgow Miners’ Union
has since written to the press thusly :—“ He (Oook) has
branded himself as a spineless ingrate, and, so far as his
sympathy for labor is concerned, has at last shown him-
self in his true colors, and in a light to be condemned by
every worker in the State.”
READ WHAT . . .
VSTADATIO
. . . IS DOING.
HYDATIDS
KaJgoorlie, W.A., August 23,1899.
Mr. S. A. PALMER,
Agent-General for Australasia for Webber’s Vitadatio.
Dear Sir, —With pleasure I write to inform you of the
good I have derived from Webber’s Vitadatio. For many
years I suffered from Hydatids, and a little over three
years ago I underwent two successive operations at a
hospital in Victoria, the doctors taking away five (5)
quarts of hydatids. Shortly after I became ill again with
a pain in my side, and after coming to Westralia the doc-
tors informed me I had another hydatid cyst, and wished
to operate on me. It was then that I saw Vitadatio
advertised, and thought I would give it a trial, which I
did, with the result that I had altogether three bad turns,
getting relieved of the hydatids by vomiting. lam now
thankful to say that the lump has entirely gone from my
side, and that I feel a new man, being better in health
and strength than I have been for many years. I shall
only be too pleased to answer any enquiries made about
my case. Yours faithfully,
tSigned) WM. J. TEMPLETON.
Witness : M. F. Smith, Kalgoorlie.
For further particulars,
S. A. JPALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

Brunswick (Vic.) ratepayers kick on find-
ing that, although the widow and the afflicted are
chivvied by law and forced to pay their rent, two
local councillors owe about £4OO for rates and inte-
rest between them, and have never been harried in
the matter. Things are getting so that it’s hardly
worth a man’s while to be an alderman.
“ Castro’s Last Sacrament and Other
Stories,” by Albert Dorrington (“ Alba Dorion ”)
is the best book of short stories yet produced in
Australia. The S. Daily Telegraph recognises
the author’s “absolute genius.” The S.M. Herald
says the volume “ contains dramatic pictures
which the best of our artists in words might well
envy.” A handsome cloth-bound volume, 350 pp.,
post-free from Bulletin office for 4s. 6d.
Recently an Adelaide paper published an
alleged libel against John Cosgrove, late of Nance
O’Neil Co. A writ was served upon the publisher
but the matter was eventually settled. Canter-

B ? n , fc oub Chippy Norton for the Nursery and

Twlr e u° u !fh to make the opposition look like hacks.

though the public came at 14. Granville Stakes went to

* * * .
Nowadays Mo. shuts up, and warns brother
Ikeys, directly he lays a hundred or two against anything
with a chance. This makes it almost impossible to work
a. big commission unless extra cunning of a unique brand
is brought to bear as that by which Sequence money for
Epsom Handicap was obtained last week. The engineer
allotcea several assistants special positions in Tatts 9
rooms, while others were told off to do the “ shoos ” from
a given point and at signal by telephone. Not a hitch
occurred, and Mo. was carried completely off his feet for
about £5OOO at hundreds to four—something* unusual
these times. 9
* * ♦ ♦
Blot ’: Toe-rag Mo.’s at Sydney ooursing
meets are killing the game. Sydney Club’s recent Cup
didn t draw 300 spectators in two days, owing to absolute
impossibility to bet with a fair chance of winning.
Clubs tolerate all (with or without bags) who pay
the required five “bob” which is often all their
capital. The penniless and bagless Moseses won’t take
any risks and always battle to lay the outsier in
each course, asking 6 and 7 to 1 about anything with a
chance, and offering as little as “ evens ” or 6 to 4 against
\ ( , ® ne ' Suggested that only books recognised by
A.J.C., V.R.C., or Kensington, should be countenanced,
When punters would have something more than Buckley’s
show. Like horse-racing, coursing without betting would
speedily shut up.
• * .. * *
Victorian legal authorities are not, as is the case
with those of N.S.W., hampered by narrow limits as to the
definition of “what is a place?” insofar as betting is
concerned. T other day bookmaker Gallagher, operating
at Flemington, was fined £25 for betting while wearing
“ tall boots, about Ift. high and sin. broad.” Some
people have fixed on an umbrella as “ a place within the
meaning, &c.,” hence Victorian Mo must bet under the
rays of a scorching sun or in a heavy downpour or leave
business entirely alone for the time being, which breaks
his heart. Truly his brother of N.S.W. is in a paradise
comparatively.
* * * *
There was a curious happening at the recent
“Tath’s” meeting in Adelaide. The instant each race
starts a gong is sounded, the tote windows being at once
dosed, and no more money accepted. On this occasion
R. King had two horses in the Hurdle Race, The Shirk
being well backed, and Dignity, an ex-Vic. chestnut,
hardly at all. At the last moment a commission was
worked for Dignity, making him first favorite, but,
whether intentionally or otherwise, it was done so late
that the gong sounded in the middle of the operation;
yet someone issued tickets for £230 through a door.
As it happened, the old black Barton just beat Dignity,
paying an unexpectedly good div. out of the clever
people’s money ; but a searching inquiry is promised.
Only fair to the machine to say that such a thing has
never been known to occur before.
* « * »
Those gentle shepherds who oppose the Tote
might be more profitably engaged in forming a Church-
and-Turf Guild. The Bulletin has heard of a St. Kilda
(Melb.) lady devoting £7 which she won in a Tatt. sweep
to the—er —service of the Lord. Anyway, the lucky
Christian matron straightway paid a year’s rent in ad-
vance for her pew-sitting at All Saints Church. What
more than that can the parsons want ?
• * * *
Rack3traw and Mystery are this scribe’s present
fancies for the Hurdle Race at Caulfield. Both horses
can stay the distance (3J miles), and all going well mean-
while, they will be two of the very fittest starters for the
event in which they are undoubtedly well handicapped.
Rackstraw’s 10.3 is only 101 b. more than he nearly won
the Flemington Grand Nat. with last year ; and Mystery
has exactly the same weight (10.4) that he carried into
second place behind. Valdis in 1899. They are likely to
hold their own against any of the heavier-weighted horses.
But inasmuch as 39 entries for the Caulfield Grand Na-
tional were handicapped at less than IQst., there is; per-
haps another Shoddy in the crowd. The trouble is to
find out which of the lightweights may be expected to
beat such respectable performers as Rackstraw and
Mystery at a difference of a stone or so.
• •
Mr. Martin Loughlin, whose Sir Peter gathered
in first G. N. Hurdles, also owned the favorite (Twilight)
for the first G. N. ’Chase. Seven started and odds of sto
4 were laid against Twilight, Mr. Goyder’s crack Sussex
carrying 13.1 and beating Twilight by three lengths.
“ Tom ” ■ Corrigan piloted two of Martin Loughlin’s
(Wymlet and Game respectively) home in ’B5 and ’B6. The
first time (’B9) of the Hurdles and Steeple being run on
one day saw Mr. S. Miller get the second leg-in of his
Redleap double through Eaglet, favorite and winner from
end to end, with Martin-Bourke in the saddle. In ’9O the
first money was increased by £5OO, making £l5OO ih all,
and reps, from M.L., S.A., Tas., etc., competed. Fearless
11. got home with S. Edge up. Two years later Redleap
(humping 13.3) went out favorite, and scored with a heap
in hand, creating a new record. Eleven of 19 starters
came down. Daimio annexed the ’94 and ’95 events.

J. J. Miller’s Sporting Pamphlet for 1901 is to
hand. The pamphlet, as usual, is an easy dictionary of
“ form” of candidates for Caulfield and Melb. Cups, with
a mass of general sporting statistics included in the
•volume.
• • « *
A bit too suggestive. A Melbourne cross-coun-
try jock is named Coffin, and not long since Sydney had
.a lepper rider named Death.
* * * *
Bill of Portland is turning out a gold-mine for
ihis ’Ome owner. His list for this season filled, and, at
latest, close upon enough for next season had also been
received. Over 20 were secured for 1903. Full financial
•significance will be understood when ’tis known that
nominations cost lOOgs. each.
M.L. cross-country rider, A. Ellingham, has
been among the lepping events with a vengeance. All
the principle ’chases throughout the country have at
different times come his way. First, Hawke’s Bay
(500 sovs.), and Napier Park Steeples (’88), as well as
Grand National (a year later) fell to him through Chemist.
He was up on Denbigh when that prad secured the
Wellington ’Chase in ’B6, and steered Moifa over the
jumps and to the front in Wanganui and Auckland big
“leps” this year. Ellingham also won Hawke’s Bay
Steeple with Ruby.
■“ * « *
Next M.L. Trotting Conference will consider a
proposal compelling importers of trotters to at once
notify the Trotting Association of their arrival, their
description, and also to forward certificate of identity,
&c. Registration-fee, a guinea ; failure to cofinply with
the rule, disqualification or £2 fine. But this won’t stop
the unscrupulous “ ringer,” who imports Australian per-
formers and runs them as maidens bought out of the
pound, or from untraceable Maoris.
* * * *
Turner-Billy M‘Kell fight, angled for months
past by several clubs, came off Saturday night at Sydney
Alhambra A.C. Most tipsters picked M'Kell as having
more dash and cunning. After three rounds it was all
Turner, who thumped freely with a telling left and had
M'Kell anyhow at times, though Mac “ scratched” full of
grit and scored well occasionally. After round 10 M'Kell
gave in. Turner now wants to meet Victorian “ feather ”
“Micko” Walsh in Sydney for a good pile. Hitherto,
cognoscenti declined to accept Turner as a first-flighter,
but now there’ll be some money wagered if Walsh connects.
* * » *
Late Otto Cribb was only 23, and started in his
father’s wake as a tobacconist and barber. Then he tried
chemistry and, ultimately, the bruising business, for
which he had a natural bent. . His first engagement was
at 20; afterwards he had three others with the ‘ ‘ raw un’s ”
in M.L. Oribb’s first and last encounters in Sydney were
with Mick Dunn ; he was tried out by that individual a
day or two after arrival. Cribb would have fought twice
a week had opportunity allowed ; as it was, he scored 15
successes in a little over 12 months. It took all this to
convince some people that Cribb could fight. Absolutely
untaught and cruder than his old-time namesake, he was
one of the greatest natural hands at the game Australia
has owned.
• » * *
All evidence indicates that Otto Cribb
(really A. O. Simpson) was anything but fit for violent
exertion. Post-mortem revealed no very severe contu-
sion. No signs of contusion of the scalp-tissue were
found, the skull was not fractured. Hinder portion of
brain was somewhat lacerated, and from this, bleeding
had occurred. Vessels at the base of the brain evidenced
early degeneration. Both lungs were deeply engorged
with blood (from the lacerated tissue referred to) and
serous fluid. Numerous patches of inflammatory change
of some standing were visible over the surface of the
right lung, which adhered to the chest wall throughout.
Vessels of the heart showed early signs of degenerative
change. Appearances that pointed to the presence of a
certain disease were described. Testifying witness (Dr.
Jamieson) further said hemorrhage on the surface of the
brain brought about by concussion accounted for death.
A blow on the face might have been the cause as follows :
“ The force was transmitted through the semi-solid sub-
stance as through water, and the wave striking against
the opposing surface there created the damage.” Con-
ditions, apparently, predisposed to hemorrhage through
slighter violence than if the system were healthy. The
degeneration of the blood vessels and other aspects indi-
cated the aforementioned disease. No medical man
could have well ascertained these facts during life, and
laymen would have been unable to judge whether
deceased was fit to undergo an ordinary boxing contest
or not.”_ Friends of Simpson aver that after his initial
success in ’Frisco a prominent local doctor warned him
not to fight again for 12 months, otherwise his life would
be jeopardised.
• * * *
T’other day, at Dublin, Irishman P. O’Connor
set the world’s broad jumpers a fresh task by clearing
24ft. 9in. Previous “ bests ” were American, 24ft. 7£in.,
by Myer Prinstein, at Philadelphia, on April 28,1900;
24ft. 4iin., by A. C. Kraenzlein, at New York, on May 26,
1899; and British, 24ft. OJin., by W. J. M. Newburn, at
Dublin, on July 16, 1898. O'Connor subsequently cleared
sft. 9£in. in the high jump.
•** * ■
Q.-N.S.W. matches over, senior grade Rugby
resumed on Saturday. Glebe walloped Eastern Suburbs
by 10 to 6, after a disappointing .game which evidenced a
had reversal of form. Souths were lucky to beat Norths
by 6 to 3, and, though they top the list, are only 11
points ahead. Newtown and Western Suburbs made
up 12 each ; and ’Varsity absolutely blotted Balmain
out, compiling 42 to 7. South Sydney (17) is now ahead
of all others, with ’Varsity and Glebe (16 each) next, and
Eastern Suburbs (14) filling fourth position. Eastern
Suburbs are in front in the second and Balmain in the
third grades.
* • * • .
S.A. and Victoria meet at football this Saturday
(3rd) on Adelaide Oval. With her best 18 available the
match has always been a sure thing for Vic., but the game
is progressing by leaps and bounds in Adelaide, and any-
thing short of a very strong team from the gold State
will go down. In the first game in Melb., on June 15,
S.A. put up 4 goals 16 behinds to 8 goals 7 behinds, and
would have won had ridiculously easy chances not been
missed.

Maoriland Rugby Union has affirmed that when
any club, in order to obtain the services of a player, pays
his subscription due to another club, it commits a breach
of the rules of professionalism.

* * * *
A. E. Stoddart’s explanation in London Sports-
man regarding the conditions upon which the amateurs
of his two teams visited Australia is refreshing to a
degree." He says : “ Both went as the guests of the Mel-
bourne 0.0. and the trustees of the Sydney ground.
jThere was no question of making terms, but it was under-
stood by me that this meant that the two clubs would
;pay all travelling expenses and hotel bills, exclusive of
wines, cigars, tips, &c. I was, however, left a discretion
in the matter of ordering champagne for the team at the
expense of the promoters when I considered it necessary
—and, wijbh the weather we experienced there, it was a
•necessity. The discretion was exercised by me, freely
but wisely, in the best interests of our health and cricket.
... No payments or allowances of any kind, direct or
indirect, were made to any members of the teams except
jto professionals. . . . Each amateur member had to pay
a considerable sum out of his own pocket, varying, no
doubt, with the means and tastes of the individual but
lin ho case, I believe, less than £100.”

! “ Stumper” : Sydney Mail (13/7/’01) has a pic-
ture of Dundee (New England) C.C., winners of premier-
ship in Northern Border Association last season. Second
man from right, rear rank, is Harry Kiehne, who enjoys
the unique distinction of being the only wooden-legged
cricketer in- Australia—if not in the world. Present
writer has often seen his stubborn defence bring victory
to his side. Of course, while batting he has a man to run
for him. He fields at point, and is safe to stop anything
ivithin reach. Also, he is a “ white” man, and is always
welcome either as a comrade or opponent on the crickef
fields of the Northern border.
Fifth inter-State lacrosse match, N.S.W. v.
Victoria, resulted in latter’s fifth triumph by 16 goals to
3, a repetition of scores in Sydney match three years ago.
Cornstalks shaped a heap better than ever before in
catching, passing and checking. They lacked combina-
tion and knowledge of ways and means of getting goals.
Stiffening of recruits from S.A. players helped N.S.W. a
deal this time. On Saturday N.S.W. went under to S.A.
at Adelaide for 2 goals to 9 —a distinctly good perform-
ance, all things weighed.
• * * *
Biggest four-bowl event (Association vice-presi-
dents’ pairs) of N.S.W. was decided on City Green
(Sydney), Saturday. Huenerbein and M'Pherson (St.
George B.O.) won—l 7 to 14. Close of first two heads saw
the game 5-love in favor of opposing couple—Law and
Carrie (Petersham 8.C.). Twelfth head totals gave St.
George the lead for the first time—lo-9. Points were level
.(10 all) after the 13th head. St. George scored in each of
the next five and added 7, where they stayed while Peter-
sham went up four, thus finishing three behind. Play
was exceptionally good. Fine drawing shots (the best
feature of the game) were credited to both sides and all
four players in turn. In the semi-final a week before,
Huenerbein and M'Pherson easily beat the crack City
men (Butler and Harris), after showing them the way
throughout. Up to then latter couple had performed
particularly well; two of their heats in successive rounds
were won with a total of 70 to the other pair’s 11.
* * * *
Roberts-Memmott billiard match at Sydney
(former conceding 2500 in 7000) ended Saturday. Roberts
did as he liked and won by 2095. This wipes out his
recent defeat by 1754 in Melbourne by Memmott. The
Britisher finished latest match with a big flourish of 436
in the afternoon, and 408 in the evening, embracing all
forms of play. He has only done better on this continent
twice ;in Sydney with 500, and Brisbane with 559. Best
breaks of the game were, Roberts : 65, 79, 352, 218, 71,
169, 108, 81, 157, 54, 74, 97, 113, 107, 68, 131, 161, 64, 207,
78, 198, 77, 222, 54, 57, 63, 58, 93, 63, 380, 80, 282, 97, 436,
85, 80, 60, 408. Memmott: 63, 53, 84, 90, 86, 59, 58, 112,
82, 47, 44, 54, 66, 40, 60, 46, 49.

* * * 9
World’s champion professional swimmer “Joey”
Nuttall, still in the middle twenties, challenges all comers
for £5OO a-side. Nuttall, a marvel a few years ago, has
since run a pub ! His best performance was against
’Murka’s premier M'Cusker, five years back, over the mile
on Hollingworth Lake under 27mins. On this achieve-
ment Sydney champion Read should easily put Nuttall
down. Read covered the mile under 25 mins, last season
and, being only a youngster still, should improve muchly.
That Read, fit and well, can beat Nuttall there is little
doubt; but that Read will be given the opportunity is
quite another thing.
• • • •
Twenty-four-year-old ’Ome wheelman Gascoigne
is coming to Sydney after seven years of English success.
“ Down Under ” he had few amateur peers, and as a pro. is
better still. He rides unpaced and has never lost a pursuit
race. Gascoigne’s best are the mile, unpaced, at Crystal
Palace (Eng.) last year in 2.5 2-5. Accompanying rider—
Jenkins —is 27, and pro. premier of England at a quarter,
one, and five miles. The two own the European tandem
supremacy.

At Poverty Point.
Melba again : She “ has definitely made up her mind to
pay a visit to Australia next year. She dreads the sea
trip, as she is a very bad sailor, but she really does intend
to return to her native land on this occasion.” Next year !
Sad to relate our old friend George Leitch, “ The In-
sect,” became severely paralysed in London last year. He
has now recovered the free use of his limbs, save that he
writes with his last hand ; but from the latest medical
consultations he fears that he will never be able to act
again, as he cannot command his speech.
Titheradge got some very candid notices for his per-
formance as a passionate lover in the gloomy Spanish
“ Mariana.” Max Beerbohm begs Mrs. Pat. Campbell not
to cast him as the Borneo to her projected Juliet.
“ Never again let him be lured from the line he excels in
—the line of quiet, modern, drab, Anglo-Saxon emotion—
into the line of lurid young Latin lovership.”
Wilson Barrett opens his Mell). season with ‘ Sign of
the Cross,” and his little list does not contain much that
has not already been tried on the dingo. Wilson’s por-
traits now scattered about Melbourne represent him as a
slim youth of 30.
Bland Holt’s army in “ A Million of Money” sports the
smart uniforms of the late Rupertswood Battery, which
were handed over by S’Rupert Clarke or “ Janet Lady ’’
to some charitable institution to dispose of. Holt being
invited to purchase the second-hand goods, made a
generous offer, and the charity committee jumped at it.
Does Mrs. Brown-Potter regret the sacrifice of a richly-
gilded billet as James Brown P.’s wife for the anxious
excitement of a mummer’s career? The other week her
daughter, Ann Urquhart Potter, was married in New
York to James Stillman, son of the Standard Oil Co. boss,
and one of the best “ catches” on the ’Miirkan marriage
market. The event at which Mrs. Potter might have
figured as an enviable momma in a peerless Paris gown
and a beautiful state of domestic preservation, only served
to advertise the fact that the worn wanderer is getting
rather old for her stage characters.
There were several dramatic items in the engagement
of the present Italian Opera Co. by Hazon and George
Allen. For instance, when the “ selectors of the team ”
had decided upon youthful Edvidge Vaccari as a most
desirable light soprano, they found that her acceptance
of the Australian offer depended on their being able to
get her bed-ridden mother’s consent to the long parting.
The girl was the sunshine of the home as well as a finan-
cial pillar of the establishment. The gentlemen must go
and see the invalid in her bedroom. Contract was signed
at the end of a second interview, when the tearful
mother solemnly commended the best girl in all Italy to
the care of Signori Hazon and Allen, and sent them
away with a little lump in their respective throats.
Said that Dalia Bassich, who, by the way, is not an
Italian but a Dalmatian, had never played Mimi in “La
Boheme” until she made her brilliant success at Her
Majesty’s, Melh. She was engaged for Williamson’s co.
on the strength of a “Fedora” performance, and will
play the name-part in Giordano’s opera when it is intro-
duced to Australia.
At Kalgoorlie (W.A.) lately, an amateur collection (the
consumptive hero had clay-stained paws 20in.xl8in.)
was playing. a sloppy melodrama to some three-
score accidental patrons, whose approval was expressed
chiefly in ghastly yawns till' the “Juvenile lead” came
along, and was asked in sepulchral tones by a “ god ”
seated in solitary majesty to “ Choose your death 1 ” The
applause that followed that was genuine.
“S.S.” : The most realistic thing seen in Melbourne
for years is Hamlet’s performance of Alfred Dampier.
An Australian writes to us from the land of the timber
ham : —“ American theatrical taste is largely for variety
shows, here called vaudeville. Continuous vaudeville
goes on daily at half-a-dozen New York houses. Show
starts about midday and runs till 11.30. The programme
repeats once or twice, so that the artists have to show
two or three times daily. Audience can sit all day, and
take its meals with it if it wants to. Vaudeville is the
same old variety, with a larger dash of coon-songs and
cake-walks than Australians are accustomed to. There
is a complete absence of the English comic-song ; the
humor is more satiric —the difference between Judge and
Ally Sloper. Writer tried a one-act piece that had
proved mirthful in Australia and England, but the Amurri-
kan manager said ‘there wasn’t a la-af in it.’ There is
no distinction between vaudeville and the legitimate
here ; a man may star one season and do sketches the
next. Our old friend Daniel Bandmann has been present-
ing ‘ Merchant of Venice ’ as a twenty-minutes’ sketch,
also 1 David Garrick ’ was similarly slaughtered. As a
set-off to vaudeville, the travelling and stock co’s. have
enormous repertoires, and snatch up every success.
Even they do variety business between the acts, for the
American, as a rule, does not go out to see a man about a
dog. The American dog is dead ; I don’t know what
killed him.”
Irene Vanbrugh, the bride elect of Dion Boucicault,
was the original Sophy Fullgarney in “ Gay Lord Quex.”
Jennie Lee, when last heard of, was about to play some
more Jo at a London suburban theatre. The little woman
is as clever as they make them, and her talent might have
been fitted with half-a-dozen star characters in better
plays than the one she has kept alive for the last 30 years.
But no authors came to her rescue. “Jo” is the only
part in which she can get a hearing in her own country.
Jennie is tied to her broom, and doomed to perish always
of a consumptive cough, with the same old limelight on
her matronly form.
“ Trilby ” isn’t played out everywhere even yet. With
young Majeroni as Svengali, Erskine Scott had an abso-
lutely record booking at Broken Hill. And then —at
Broken Hill, of all places in the world! —wet weather
came and marred the season. The Broken Hillite, who
can stand 112° without turning a hair, positively squirms
under a raindrop.
Truly the Queen is dead—very, very dead; long live
the King. In the “Second in Command” at Sydney
Royal the soldier starting for the Transvaal at the beginn-
ingof the wartalked about wearing the King’s uniform, and
almost exactly the same thing happened in “ Serving the
King” at Sydney Lyceum. Whac of the gushful pro-
mises of a few months ago that Victoria, the Great, would
Never be forgotten ? Her birthday is abolished as a holi-
day and passes without a mention of her in the papers.
Her name appears in the columns of the daily press about
as often as that of Hardicanute or Harold Harefoot. And
the dramatist crowds her out, and ignores her, and puts
the crown .on Edward VII. even in her lifetime. Long
Live the King!
Housework.”
Housework seems easy to a man, but there is a
great deal of lifting and reaching to do, a great
many trips up and down stairs to make in the
course of a day’s housework. It’s hard "when a
woman is well—for a woman suffering from some
form of disease it is daily
torment. There are thou-
sands of such women
struggling along, day by
day, in increasing misery.
There are other thousands
who have found a complete
cure of their ailments in the
use of Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills. Mrs. Jew, pro-
prietress of the Theatre
Royal Hotel, Brisbane,
states clearly what she
means in a few words. She
writes: —“ For some years I
have suffered from a sluggish
liver, accompanied at times
by severe pains in my chest
and shoulders. These pains
not only caused me intense
suffering, but they often
kept me from doing my
household work, and con-
fined me to my bed. No
medicine gave me permanent
relief, and many failures
made me despondent, so
when I tried Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills I had little
hope that they would give
me any great relief; but I
can assure you that one
bottle made me feel a differ-
ent woman. I find they are
a very good pill, acting mildly and searphingly,
benefiting the whole system; I can recommend them
to anybody needing such a medicine.” Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills are a perfect blood purifier and a
positive cure for biliousness, indigestion, con-
stipation, headaches, sallow complexion, liver
and kidney troubles, piles, pimples, boils,
blotches, and female ailments. Sold by chemists
and storekeepers, Is ljd per bottle, or six bottles
6s. 6d. Sole proprietors, The W. H. Comstock
Co., Ltd. (Australasian Depot), 58 Pitt-street,
Sydney. Packed in amber bottles, and the full
name blown thereon.*

“At Marshall Hall’s orchestral concert, attended
by an admiring crowd, it was a lesson in hero-
worship to see women raise their hands and sort
of aim applause, not at the orchestra, but at the
conductor, who, for all his seeming indifference,
no doubt inhales such incense with secret satisfac-
tion. The Hymner, thanks to button-hole posy
and neat, though not spick-and-span, attire, looked
less rough-hewn than in Tom Roberts’ portrait,
which suffers from over-accentuation of that black
coster curl, plastered down towards bridge of
sitter’s nose. Artist might have done worse than
accidentally singe off the forelock, at the risk of a
storm, of course, for the ex-Prof. is understood to
pride himself on his low forehead—so Greek, you
know.
“ The concert music was all masterly, especially
that tender, soothing ‘ Siegfried Idyll.’ Wagner
did well to make it ‘ soothing ’ considering that it
was first performed at dawn, outside the bedroom
door of Wagner junior (aged one year) and his
ma. Travaglini, of the Opera Co., aired his dress-
suit by daylight and gave a strong, calm rendering
of an air from ‘ the Magic Flute.’
“The evergreen Napoleon Boffard was turned
on at the third musical at-home of the season (at
Mrs. Albert Miller’s) last Friday to make an
announcement. It was the most amusing that
happened—funny things being out of the way of
these affairs. ‘ I haf to make the apologies of
Signorina Rebottaro, who is ill by the influenza, ’
declared the stout tenor, with many sweet smiles
and nods. The frilly gathering giggled for an
hour over the statement, and Nap. tripped off
the platform with the triumphant air of a pre-
cocious pirouetter. At that gathering, Mrs. H.
M. Cox was singled out for special attention.
The Daylesford heiress that was has developed
into a very quiet, retiring dame. But the taste
for hunting-pink still goes strong. It appeared in
her vest (with a purple costume) and under her
hat-brim.
“First night of ‘Rigoletto’ drew a big, live
crowd. Sapio, as Gilda, the court jester’s daughter,
looked sweet, girlish and innocent, in fair plait
and white gown trimmed with pale-blue. She
would be an ideal Marguerite with those sad eyes
of hers ; her appearance steers between the
usual versions, which serve up Mephisto’s mash
either as a doll-faced simpleton, or a gullible
dowager.
“The Sapio voice was in excellent trim, flexible
as a bird’s, clear as a silver bell. Bozzoli, as the
hump-backed buffoon, was powerful and pathetic.
People in the cloak-room, afterwards, were raving
about Dani, but all my sympathy was enlisted by
Rigoletto (Bozzoli). Dani’s ‘ Donnae mobile’was
to me a shade tinny and disappointing ; pathetic
songs suit him better than frivolous ones.
“At ‘Rigoletto,’ Mrs. Dalzell was the most
attractive frocker. Beside the Scotch charmer,
local women, no matter how crimped, cos-
tumed, and complexioned, dwindled in dis-
tinction. Even Mdlle Duret, with her chiffon
sentinels arresting attention in her hair
and shoulders, missed the ’bus. Mrs. Bob
Power and a host of swagger dames are rigging
up showy powder and flower sets for the District
Nursing Ball, next Wednesday (7th). It was the
intention of the fair engineers to raise a canopy of
flowery stalls and decked crooks for the Gov.-
General and the haughty Generaless to walk under
on entering the hall, but as it is pretty certain the
dual Excellency can’t break through the mourning
hedges, the canopy will not be. Mrs. Power will
secure their presence if anyone can, for the tactful
wife of sporting “Bob” is in strong favor at
Govmentouse.
“ Lady Hopetoun, distributing the Humane
Society’s awards at the Town Hall (July 22nd),
wore seal cloak with sable collar and hat covered
with lilac. She and her chum sat in a row with
Mrs. Barton, Janet Lady Clarke, &c., quite ignor-
ing her feminine neighbours.
“ Not one of the heroes that got the Humane
Society’s medals was handsome, courtly, or even
very big and strong ; and they nearly all blushed
and blundered in the public gaze. First re-
cipient, just a boy, was so nervous he turned his
back on the Countess, but when someone was
drowning he toed the scratch all right. Next one,
an elderly man (Irish, I guess), all _ bows and
salutes, had got badly knocked about in stopping
a bolting van. Number three was a white-
bearded patriarch ; another, a railway-porter, had
jumped into a deep well, all broken bottles at the
bottom, and saved a child. Policemen, a clerk,
a woman, a little girl, all had proved themselves
willing to lay down their lives for strangers. A
thin little chap made the spectators laugh, be-
cause, in his confusion, he ran to hand-shake the>
Countess prematurely. This poor boy had rushed,
into a burning house in search of his little sister,
groped about till he was badly burnt himself, and'
failed in his quest, after all. A blind, lame
miner, led by his wife to get his medal, had
climbed up ladders and crawled along shafts
to get help for his mate, bleeding, blind, dazed,
and maimed though he was.
“ The deed of that blind miner, Madden, was a
splendid one, unsurpassed in its heroism, as any-
one who read the details will admit. What a
pity that instead of the brave man’s poor reward
being handed him by so cool and uninspiring a
personage as the Countess, he could not have re-
ceived it at the hands of some magnetic woman of
high estate whose enthusiasm would be con-
tagious ! Ah, what electricity would have run
through the audience had Madden’s medal been
handed him after a dramatic little speech by such
a woman as the late Madame Lucien Henri!
Then the public might have done something sub-
stantial to help the brave miner. Now, his reward
is to be a miserable 10s. weekly pension. And that
is all!
“ A little Melb. weekly remarks: ‘ Lady Some-
body has a wonderful memory for names and faces,
to which gift may be accounted much of her
popularity.’ The ‘ popularity ’of most people—
in fact, all people whose path in life is paved
with newspaper pars—depends upon their cheap
capacity for remembering names and faces. It
isn’t in the least degree wonderful. The dullest
mortals can cultivate it. A good many politicians
live upon it —this ignoble art of always identifying
the man or woman whom one has noted as a per-
son worth identification. Not to have this
‘wonderful’ gift is, in a person of fair brain-
power, to be broad-minded, studious, and—un-
popular. The higher intellect revolts from the
thought of remembering the names and faces of
thousands of commonplace nonentities.
“The most restless member of the well-assorted
House of Reps, is starch-merchant Robt. Harper.
He passes incessantly from one member to
another, doffs and dons his silk hat a dozen times,
bows and laughs to his relatives in the gallery,
disappears from the debating boards, reappears
again almost immediately, and gives the observer
the impression of being ever before the camera.
He is as restless as a child in a new frock. On
the other hand, Malcolm M'Eacharn is abashed,
never stirs, never speaks, but just watches the
antics of the old political Punchinellos with the
blinking, observant eyes of a tabby basking in the
sun.
“Be tints. The snowy hill-country round Mel-
bourne is worth seeing just now, even at the price
of chilblains. T’other day I explored a mountain
road in a snow-storm. The scene was spared the
sadness lent by leafless branches and skeleton
trunks, though masses of dripping greenery, frost-
powdered foliage, etc., supplied the shiversome
element. Sprays of ripe hips, showy as big, red
plums, blazed here and there against fern-fronds,
glittering with crisp, powdery snow. Baby gum-
trees, tender little saplings, seemed to like the
cold, for their young leaves shone satin and their
tiny stems glowed red as coral.
“ Castlemaine’s Rep.—J. M'Cay—is one of the
most persistent interjectors in the House. This
is the young man who married an Irish girl, had
three R.C. priests to tie the knot (his own dad, a
Presbyterian divine, looking on), and for best man
an Akgus scribe who made such a memorable
speech deriding holy matrimony and all the sacred
paraphernalia thereof. An avowed unbeliever, he
took this opportunity of paying M'Cay out for
making him assist in a ceremony he scoffed at.
But as the speech happened after several fizzing
toasts, the holy fathers put anger out of their
hearts, and forgave him, thinking he knew not
what he did".
“ Marie Lloyd spins along the Block—some-
times in perky rig-out with a saucy nose in the
air, an independent wag of her skirts, a pair
of clicking heels, and the loveliest long chain of
coral pieced together with diamonds. Quite the
most novel and expensive chain one sees round
any swan neck.
“ The push at the Clarke-Cotton wedding at
Melb. St. Peter’s suggested an idea. Janet Lady
Clarke is an indefatigable collector for charities,
But if this wedding had been staged at the Prin-
‘ cess’s, and fancy prioes charged for admission, a
sum might have been raised that would have
saved much wear and tear on the baronial plate.
As it was the average theatre audience, mainly
women, rushed the show, saw it badly, and
nothing was saved but the risk of being fined £2
for overcrowding.

dings on 24th. Both took jdace church, and
the sparrows have been since.
Burnley Hill, who led Mrs.
altar, is pretty well known in all the
cousin of Lord Lamington, and in one way or other con-
nected with about half the peerage. The affair is quite
an Indian-summer romance, neither bride nor groom
being in their first youth, but it is said that the latter
was loved as a girl by her present husband. An hour or
so later Miss Mary Tully (whose family is well known in

announced to Miss Queenie Levien, daughter of the

PERISHED IN THE SNOW.
A WATCHMAKER FROZEN TO DEATH.
At Corryong, Vic., a watchmaker, Charles Butler, has
been found frozen to death in the snow.
Poor fellow ! it was the very last kind of death he
could have expected to meet in this country.
N.S.W. State Treasurer Waddell, at New-
castle, on 27th July:
There was one thing in particular he wished to allude
to, and that was that N.S.W. was the only one of the
Australian States whose railway system was paying not
only working expenses but full interest on the cost of
construction. (Applause). All the other States were
behind in this respect.
This is the sort of foolish, ignorant boasting
for which N.S.W. politicians are especially
noted. According to Coghlan’s “ Seven Colonies ”
for 1900, Westralia’s railways have shown a
net profit for six years out of the last ten,
the six profitable years being 1895 to 1900 in-
clusive. South Australia’s lines showed a profit
for three years (1891, 1892 and 1900) out of the
last ten. N.S.W.’s lines showed a profit for one
year only (1899) out of the last ten (though
the Auditor-General disagrees with Coghlan and
declares there was a loss in that year also). The
other three States made a loss every year. During
the last 15 years there was, according to Coghlan,
one (1893) when N.S.W. had the railway system
which showed the largest net returns on the
money invested (of course, the railway with the
largest returns may still show a loss ; it all depends
on the rate at which the money was borrowed);
Victoria had the best-paying system in two years
(1886-1887); South Australia in six years (1888,
1889, 1890, 1891, 1892, and 1894) ; and in the other
six years (1895 to 1900) Westralia was far ahead of
everything. In 1900 N.S.W. only held third
place, and its accumulated railway deficit is close
on £8,000,000. When these facts are available in
the N.S.W. Government’s own publications what
is gained through an inflated parochial character
going round telling the thing that isn’t so, merely
to create a diseased parochial vanity ?
A flourish of trumpets was made over the
landing of a gun captured from the Boers by the
Vic. cohentingent which returned last week. A
special officer was detailed to superintend the
landing, and Govt, went to the expense of hiring
four powerful draught horses to drag the trophy
in triumph through the streets. A special squad
of artillerymen were paid to act as escort, and the
gun was so well advertised that several thousand
more people than usual turned out to inspect it on
the march. But, when the procession came along,
no gun was visible, and the public, which had been
waiting for three hours after the advertised time of
maroh, said “Blanky !” and made remarks deroga-
tory to the Empiah and the cohentingent, a,nd
were even dissatisfied with the official explanation
that the gun was found to be one which could not
be mounted on wheels. The inference was that it
was a gun of position, capable of belching huge
masses of iron at the enemy. Later on, however,
it transpired that it had been removed to the
ordnance stores without any horses or escort. An
artilleryman had simply put it over his shoulder
and walked to the end of the pier, where he took
it on a tramcar to the barracks. This implement
of devastation turns out to be a tiny machine-gun,
weighing a few lbs. It is now asserted by some
rascally pro-Boer that it was captured out of the
pocket of a Cape rebel as he was in the crush
outside the box-office of Capetown Theatre.
New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency Co.,
Ltd., report for week ended July 27th a brisk demand
for merino wools. Result: Fair biz. at late rates. Lon-
don reports merinos hardening, and coarse crossbreds
slightly better. Improved London prices for oats
and wheat have had hardening effect, _ but sup-
plies offering are limited, holders being inclined to
hold. Prices : Chickwheat, 2s. Cfd. ; prime, 2s. Bd.; and
f.a.q., 2s. 7pi. to 2s. Bd. per bushel. There has also been
a good demand for marsupial skins, hair, and horns,
sheepskins, and hides, but shank bones can only be dis-
posed of at reduced rates, and tallow has slumped another
68. per ton.
“H.S.R.”: Bulletin is wrong about
Wellington never having lost a gun. He lost two
in the Pass of Maya, in the Pyrenees (Vide Max-
well’s life of D. of W.) “Smiler” Hales is also
wrong when he states that nothing can stop a
charge of British cavalry. Such a charge was
stopped all right at Poplai Grove by the
Johannesburg police who were Paul Krugers
bodyguard during the combat. Otherwise, Paul
would have been captured and the war would
most probably have fizzled out. Also, those
terrific bayonet-charges we read about never
occurred. Col. Moore, of the Connaught
Rangers, asserts that they were for the most part
figments of the imaginations of the war corres-
pondents, and that on no occasion did the Rangers
get near enough to the Boers to use the bayonet,
although the regiment saw most of the fighting -
some of the companies losing (50 to 70 per cent, at
Colenso and Pieter’s Hill. Shouldn’t wonder if,
after all, when we get the real truth, the cam-
paign will resolve itself into a farm-burning and
stock-stealing raid, with about as much glory as
attached to the elephant’s attack on a nest of field
mice “because they had squealed at him.
When the French submarine boat Narval
got back to port after a 12 hours’ cruise under
water, most of the crew made a bee-line for
hospital. The first six hours went comparatively
well, after which the breathing of artificial or
compressed air became difficult and distasteful,
and a terrible irritation and depression seized
upon the nerve-centres. Headache and vomiting
became general, and it was found that the
generating of electricity under water let loose
salts of lead and sulphur among the crew, that
brought on grave intestinal troubles.
“E.D.”: lie Kipling and his critics: An
idea is like a machine. The man who invents
improvements on the latter is allowed by law to
derive such advantages, moral and material, as he
can from his work. So the man who embellishes
an old idea with the decorative qualities of his
genius may be justified in his use of it not other-
wise. But in this case the man’s idea is the mother
by which the late-comer fathers a little quiver of
new ideas. This after all is the process of the
generation of ideas. The English language as
we use it to-day is composed largely of other
fellows’ ideas; we may use the old building-
material, but only to produce new edifices, and
Kipling sometimes tears up a church to build a
chapel. To steal another man’s spire, or window,
or gargoyle, is in an artist a confession of defeat,
and it is a true art instinct that inspires the
builders with a passion for the label: “ Our own
make.” Your artist, when he is most an artist,
wants no other man fathering his brain-bairns.

out by says the S.M. Herald, in the
middle of a post-mortem eulogium. Pizness !
Pizness ! ”
Mr. Dobbin, P.M. of Melbourne, has de-
cided that a publican is not liable for having a
drunken person on the premises if the said drunk
be a boarder. The police are appealing from the
decision.
Sydney Telegraph's new artist, J. 0.
Gasking, seems to have solved for that paper, as
far as line-production is concerned, the difficult
problem of drawing for the rotary printing-press.
Some of his recent sketches give admirable results.
A Woman Suffragist writes :
“The goody parsons who have backed up the Mel-
bourne telephone-girls in their ill-advised refusal to take
their share of Sunday work with the men, urge that it’s
disgraceful to ask a woman to work on Sunday. How
about the women checking-clerks of the M. T. and O. Co.,
who spend every third Sunday afternoon at the Bourke-
street office ? Or to come nearer home, what of domestic
servants? Do all of these have their Sundays to them-
selves? Do these reverend gentleman brush their own
boots or help to get breakfast on Sunday ? ”
From Mr. W. J. Spruson
Dear Bulletin.— Please let me say, in reply to your
par (20/7/’01), that I did not use the words there attributed
to me. You happen to have in your quotation a rather
mild version of Gipps’ misrepresentation. Some of it was
much better than the average during the last campaign.
The inventive talent displayed by electioneering agents
in that quarter would bring' certain wealth if exercised in
other channels. Also, Labor did not defeat me. It was
Labor plus other weighty influences, chief of which was
“ Rocks Resumptions.” In regard to the latter I was held
solely to blame ; but although I am responsible for the
scheme, I am not, of course, responsible fot the bungling
and mismanagement of Ministers and officials. —W. J.
Spruson, 20th July, 1901.

Premier Jenkins’s measure for the reform
of the S.A. Constitution is energetic and business-
like. Its leading features are :
The abolition of one Minister.
Assembly to be reduced from 54 to 36 members, repre-
senting single electorates, suffrage as at present.
Council to be reduced from 24 to 16 members, elected
by Household Suffrage", with the whole State as one con-
stituency. Half the Council to go for re-election every
three years.
Premier Jenkins proposes to make S.A. Council a
copy of the Federal Senate, only with a less
liberal suffrage, and the S.A. Counci], which cor-
dially approved of the Federal Constitution, can’t
logically object. All the same, it will object—
iliogically ; and then everything depends on Jen-
kins’s sincerity and his capacity for maintaining
the fig’ll it to the last man and the last ditch. The
two qualities, unhappily, don’t of necessity go
together. William Shiels, for instance, could
draft Bills whose cleverness and goodness of
intention brought tears to your eyes, and then he
fought for them with all the fui'y of not much wet
blotting-paper ; while John Forrest can defend a
hoary abuse with the courage of the last man
holding a fortress single-handed.

The following resolutions were carried unanimously :
“ That the Report and Statements of Account be received and adopted, and ordered to be circulated among
the Proprietors.”
“That the Honorable H. E. Kater, M.L.C., and E. W. Knox, Esq., be elected Directors of the Bank.”
“That E. S. Cape, Esq., and the Honorable F. T. Humphery, M.LC., be appointed Auditors for the ensuing
year.”
“ That the thanks of the Shareholders be presented to the Directors, General Manager and Officers of the
Bank for their very satisfactory management of the affairs of the institution.”
T. A. DIBBS, General Manager.
In moving the adoption of the Report, the Chairman, Mr. George J. Cohen, said
I am glad to be able to say that, as compared with our Balance Sheet of twelve months ago, the statement of our
position now presented to you shows an increase in our total figures of £487,000.
On the debit side there is a shrinkage of £40,000 in Bills in Circulation, but our Notes in Circulation and Deposits
and other Liabilities have respectively increased £71,000 and £422,000.
On the other side our liquid Assets are less by £275,000. Coin and Cash at Bankers are practically the same, a
reduction of about £14,000.
The item Government Securities shows an increase of £96, ;00, the Board having taken advantage of the favour-
able opportunity to add to our hjlding. Our Bills Discounted and Advances generally show the very satisfactory
increase of £725,000.
During the half year we have varied our Government Securities, and now hold £308,000 in British Government
Bonds.

\V e have examined tlie foregoing Agg regato Balance Sheet, made up to 30th June, 1901, and report that we find
the several items therein stated to conform to the Balances in the Oeimral Ledger at Head Office,.and to the Balance
Sheets furnished by the Branches ; these latter bein.or certified to by the.respective Managers and Accountants.

of electricity, and only a fringe of imagination of the un-
told stores of strength and wealth wrapt up in this
phenomenal power. .
Therefore it requires no argument to convince the
most pronounced of optimists that the field in any one of
the great and honored professions of the day is far too
vast for the thorough comprehension of the most per-
fectly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
men. Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult of
all professions-that of prescribing for the ills and
diseases of mankind—we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, off-handedly and without careful, minute, and
searching examination and diagnosis— without any know-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippantly dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
Thanks to the march of civilisation, culture, hnd edu-
cation, people are getting to know that modern medical
men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessness of
mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
ing that “ gullibles ” are not so plentiful as they used to
be, the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of cases, and work out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
ability. . .
What better and more striking illustration of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, Inver
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent continent, Australia, have
than the enormous practice of the famous practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
doubted masters of the classes of diseases and complaints
which have been the study of their professional lives.
When we speak of electricity we think of Edison. Why ?
Because he dwarfs other men in this branch of science.
In the same way, when we talk of specialists, we at once
associate the. names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
tute. So it is in every branch of scientific medicine;
some one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows. .
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
he pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker with a host of complaints about which
they can have but a hazy notion, and superficial know-
ledge. To use a hackneyed but expressive phrase, they
are “Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never
since the time of Ricord, the noted French Specialist,
have any men conducted so gigantic and successful a
practice as that conducted by the Freeman-Wallace
Electro Medical and Surgical Institute.
of hard work than any other medical men ; we do it in
the best interests of sufferers. We don’t keep them
waiting longer than we can help, and we suffer many in-
conveniences for our patients’ sakes.”
Noble sentiments, thought the writer, as the time came
h i his departure, and the Specialists returning to their

At the recent annual South Melbourne
competition, or whatever they call it, there was a
reciting tournament for “ ladies who have not won
a prize.” And the piece chosen for those un-
successful strugglers to recite was “ Maternal
Love.” Hopeless spinsters were asked to come
forward in stale white frocks and deliver their
rival notions of “ Maternal Love.”
Looks as if Q. Chief Justice Griffith is
about to move upwards. Want and Pilcher went
to Brisbane last week with, it is said, a certain
object in view, and now Attorney-General Rut-
ledge is bringing in a bill lowering the salary of
the next Q. Chief Justice by £lOOO per annum.
“A mere precautionary measure,” he explains.
As if one couldn’t see the wheels go round; also
what a smack for either Real or Cooper, who will
want the vacant chair ! The salary was increased
£lOOO a year by Griffith, and then Griffith took
the billet himself, but he is to be the only £3500
Chief Justice in Queensland.
William Arnott, the well-known Sydney
biscuit manufacturer, who died the other day at
Homebush, Sydney, is said to have received of
late years, an income of £lO,OOO a year as his share
of the profits of the biscuit business which he ran
in conjunction with his sons. There are many
stories about the old man. One is that he
attributed all his prosperity to the fact that, after
becoming bankrupt and paying a few shillings in
the £, he never rested until he had discharged his
liabilities to the uttermost penny. Another is
that 10 years ago he was going to sell the (even
then) great biscuit business for £86,000, but was
dissuaded by his wife, who said: “What are you
going to do with the boys if you sell the busi-
ness ? ”
One chief ground of Tweedle-Lee’s motion
of censure on Tweedle-See is the enormous length
of time the latter kept N.S.W. Assembly closed
—231 days. Yet that included the Federal and
Ducal celebration periods, when most politicians
were more or less drunk ; the Federal Election
period, when the Assembly couldn’t have been got
together for love or money, because half or more
of its members were away electioneering and the
other half were helping ; the Federal Parliament
opening, when everybody was away in Melbourne,
and N.S.W. General Election. It is alleged that
Lyne promised to call the old Parliament together
before the dissolution, but if he did (which is none
too certain), when the time came for fulfilling the
promise there was hardly a sober M.L.A. left in
Sydney to call together. The Reid Ministry, of
which Lee was the sagging tail-end, once closed
up the Assembly for 193 days, and there was
no general election and no drunks or cele-
brations included in that time to serve as an
excuse. Parkes, the great Freetrade leader,
closed up the Assembly for 245 consecutive days
in 1881-82—with no earthly reason save that
it suited him to run the country by himself for a
while,
Editor Bulletin. —Re your par. (13/7/’01).
Grenfell was not the only N.S.W. electorate m
which the war question was the dominant issue.
We had a similar battle in Waratah. The three
local Labor members voted against sending troops
to the Transvaal, but when the Jingo tornado was
howling two of them got on a balcony to welcome
the first lot of returned sick swaddies —and climbed
down—said they “ did not know the true facts or
they would not have opposed sending troops,” &c.
Only Griffith stuck to his guns—told the chairman
of an indignation meetingun his constituency that
“ this war was the foulest blot in English his-
tory,” that it was “a Dreyfus case expressed in
international terms,” and that he “ would rather
lose his seat than vote to send Australians to take
a hand in it. ” When the election came along his
opponent (Sharp, late Mayor of Newcastle)
flooded the electorate with choice selections from
Griffith’s red-hot speech in the Assembly, and had
a couple of returned cohentingenters speaking for
him throughout the electorate. Griffith adhered
to his position, read portions of his speech from
Hansard at his meetings, and won by over 300
votes.—E. J.

“Gunner”: The Boer war has produced
much of the silly talk about rifle-shooting. Seems
to be supposed that any ordinary man with a good
rifle can knock over another 500 or 1000 yd. off.
Now, a 6ft. man at 500 yd. from the observer looks
exactly the same size as an image 7 l-sin. high
would look 50 yards off. Also, make a mark fin.
long and |in. wide on the wall at one end of a
room 15ft. long, then go to the other end and try
how often you can hit that mark with a saloon-
rifle. Yet, that mark approximately represents
the apparent size of a man 500 yd. off, while a
man’s size at 1000 yd. would be represented 15yd.
off by a mark of but half the above-given size.
Consequently, the man who oan even see another
1000 yd. off—with all the disturbing influences,
such as haze and surrounding objects, has un-
commonly good sight. Current contingent-yarns
about single men being picked-off at 1000 yd. are
sheer blatherskite.
The Duke has finally gone. As a frost, he
was the biggest show that ever toured Australia.
As a person of intrinsic unimportance he monopo-
lises the pastry. As a destroyer of Australian
reverence for Monarchy lie was an unparalleled
success. Next time the English authorities send
along a commercial-traveller in the King business
they will, unless they aren’t fit to be outside
Colney Hatch or Tarban Creek, show us some-
thing much longer, and with a bingey. Australia
doesn’t necessarily respect blue blood, but it is
always prepared to tolerate Brawn.
Mr. A. E. Leplastrier, Manager of the Central
Mining and Tramway Appliances Prop., Ltd., 40 Hunter
Street, Sydney, reports that his Company has supplied
several tramways during the past six months, and he
mentions the following lines as among the number.
Magnet Silver Mining Co., Tasmania: 9 miles 2ft. gauge
track, 301 b. per yard rails. Rolling stock consisting of
goods waggons and 2 locomotives. North Mount
Farrell Mining Co., Tasmania : 8 miles of 2ft. gauge track,
rails 201 b. per yard. Rolling stock includes goods wag-
gons, passenger cars, locomotives, &c., &c. In Queens-
land, sugar estates were equipped to the extent of 8
miles. Those in need of tramways should communicate
with Mr. Leplastrier, and as Railway Engineers are
attached to his Company’s staff, reliable technical in-
formation will be forthcoming upon receipt of necessary
data, and if required flying, and permanent Surveys, will
be conducted.*
