Some far-sighted persons can project their gaze
a considerable distance into the future, whilst others are
so short-sighted as to be unable to see an opponent’s
argument, though it be as big as an elephant and twice
as weighty. Those with any kind of defective physical
vision, who are mentally clear-sighted enough to call at
Wood, “ The Optician,” 95 Elizabeth-street. Melbourne,,
can have things presented to them in a light as clear as
the best scientific advice and appliances can make it.*
* * * •
Mr. John Kearns, of Akron, Ohio (U.S.A.), a
well-known figure in the American rubber manufacturing
industry, has arrived in Melbourne to take charge of the
big rubber plant and mills now being laid down by the
Dunlop Tyre Company, at South Melbourne, at a cost of
£14,500. Mr. Kearns, who has had over 20 years’ experi-
ence in the rubber trade, ranks as one of the cleverest
rubber experts in America.*
* * * *
The United Typewriter and Supplies Co. has
obtained an order for 500 New Century Typewriters from
the Austro-Hungarian Government Railways, after severe
tests against all other makes of machines. The order will
eventually be increased to 2000 machines. This is the
largest order ever placed for typewriters. The Australian
branch of the company has been appointed suppliers of
typewriters to his Excellency the Governor-General.*
* • * *
The Adelaide Advertiser of 13th April last,
has an interview with Mr. E. A. Gilbert, the Australian
manager of The Bile Beans Manufacturing Co. Mr.
Gilbert told the Advertiser man that 250,000 doses of
Bile Beans are consumed every night throughout the
world, and that the pamphlets issued by the co. in Aus-
tralia during 1900, would if pasted leaf side by side with
leaf, cover a distance of 60,000 miles.*

Ever the eternal squabble over the bill 1
The celebration banquet on the day Mr. and Mrs.
Cornwall and York galloped through Ballarat
cost some £7O, and the council wrangled for hours
as to whether it should come out of the mayoral
allowance or out of the rates. Of course, the rate-
payers had to pay in the end. To get over
this and similar illegalities, Minister M'Culloch
already has before Parliament a Bill “to make
such expenditure by councils legal, notwithstand-
ing anything contained in any Act to the con-
trary.” What is the use of making Acts to
prohibit councillors misappropriating other
people’s money if they are to be abrogated every
time the prohibited thing happens ?
The Mining-Warden and P.M. at Wil-
oannia and White Cliffs (far-west N.S.W.) has
during the past three years travelled over bush
tracks on official business between 15,000 and
16,000 miles ! And now the Public Service Board
has sent him to Moree for a change.
For years a pew has been reserved in St.
Paul’s Anglioan Cathedral, Melbourne, for the
use of the Mayor and corporation, but as that
soulless body has never used the accommodation it
is now given over for the service of the Governor-
General. Hopetoun, however, is a potentate of
the Kirk of Scotland, and puts his threepenny in
the plate of that serious denomination ; so again
the pew appears to have fallen on barren ground,
so to speak.
An Engineer in Difficulties!
TRIES, LIKE MANY MORE, HEAPS OF
SO CALLED REMEDIES
Meets with Final Success.
fhe Case of
MR. JAMES BURNS.
(By Our Reporter.)
J\° invalid is so difficult to deal with as
the one who has “never known a day’s
sickness. ” For the most part he resolutely
puts away the suggestion—“ That his time
has come at last, that his smooth working
machinery has broken down, and that active
repair is at once required.” No ! the
chances are the sufferer battles on, getting
daily worse and worse, till final collapse lays
him on his back.
“My husband never would have that
anything was seriously the matter with him,
that he was bilious, or that his liver was not
as sound as it ought to be,” said Mrs. Burns
as our reporter interviewed that lady and
Mr. Burns. “ Not till he was really bad
and desperately ill would he give in, and
admit that something was radically wrong
with him.” Mr. Burns, it may be men-
tioned, is an engineer by profession, living
at 107 Stanley-street, North Melbourne,
and well known in that locality. Some time
ago he was engaged in a very arduous
undertaking, where he was exposed to all
kinds of weather, continually knocked up
by excessive travelling, and left for long
periods with poor and irregular food. In
the pride of good health and an iron con-
stitution, Mr. Burns thought nothing of
these hardships at the time; but later on,
like many more before him, he had good
cause to recollect. “ I fell,”said Mr. Burns,
into a low lethargic condition of body
and mind. I had pains all over me. Ido
uot think there was a part of my frame free
from an ache of some kind, I was bilious
with an excess of bile that seemed to numb
all my faculties. I did not care to work, to
move about, to do anything. Yet when I
braced myself to active exeroise I felt better
—that is, while the work lasted ; but when
the excitement and stimulant of exercise
was over I was done, prostrate and desti-
tute of all hope and energy. I suffered
from a pain below my neck and between
Buy shoulders, that cramped up my body.
At the least axoitement my heart was
deranged, and went thump, thump, thump
against my ribs like the beat of a hammer.
My stomach and digestive organs behaved
very strangely, as though they had gone on
strike and quite given up work. No sooner
had I eaten a meal than I had to run to the
back yard and vomit it all up just as I had
swallowed it; so that my assimilative pro-
cess was absolutely paralysed. At times I
would go giddy in the head, like a child
that turns round and round; I could not
stand on my feet, and felt the sick deadly
sensation of one about to faint. If I had
been resting any time in one position, and
went to get up, I was held fast by a stiff
gripping pain in the small of the back.
Gradually I lost all taste or inclination for
food ; I would go for two or throe days con-
tinuously without eating. This starvation
greatly added to my weakness, and at the
«*me time I rapidly lost flesh, and became
reduced to a shadow of my former self. My
nervous system suffered with the rest of
my body, and I became morbidly sensitive
to the least sound. A loud knock at the
door would make me start and jump as
though a cannon had suddenly been fired
close by. My temper became very irritable,
1 was vexed and upset at the least thing
1 slept well, in fact I was always drowsy
but the sleep appeared to do me no good I
was never rested. Sleeping for one hour
was aa good as eight for all the benefit I
derived, and in the morning I would be
more tired than at night. A terror that
haunted my night’a reat wan nightmare.
Brea when I awoke, or, what would often
happen, my wife woke me, I still for a time
experienced the cruel pains of suffocation
and strangulation. This general upsetting
of my whole system affected my eyes ; there
were times when they were quite dull, my
sight becoming blurred, and it was with
difficulty that 1 oould see to read. When I
stooped down there followed pains across my
loins like the piercing of sharp instruments.
M y palate always had a nasty flavour, my
tongue was parched and dry. I became
wonderfully changed in outward appearance
and was depressing to look at, thin, worn
and emaciated, a skeleton of what I had
been.
“ What remedies did you try, Mr. Burns?”
enquired the pressman.
“Heaps of things, various makes of bitters
and blood purifiers, all warranted to cure
oases like mine and all useless, also a
number of.patent and much lauded cure-alls
with a like result. None did me the slight-
est good, in fact I became day by day
steadily worse. It was about this time,
when I concluded that there was no medi-
cine in the world that would relieve my
sufferings, that a book was left at the door
descriptive of the virtues of Clements Tonic.
“ I wonder,” said my wife, “ if that would
do you any good, let us have one more try.”
She went over to our chemist and asked
about Clements Tonic, and that gentleman
told her she could not go wrong in buying
it, that it was the best remedy he knew of
that his sales of Clements Tonic were large
and increasing, and he had received the very
best reports from his customers who had
purchased it, and that there was not the
shadow of a doubt that Clements Tonic did
all that it claimed to do. After that, you
may be sure I purchased a bottle of Clements
lonic, for I know that chemists are not
naturally inclined to give credit to proprie-
tary medicines without good reason. My
health began to mend from the very first
bottle I took. I gained a good appetite, and
my stomach retained the food. My sleep
refreshed me, the pains all over my body in
my back, head, and heart were all subdued.
1 regained energy and flesh and lost my
sallow appearance, my nerves were strength-
ened, and at night I slept soundly, undis-
turbed by dreams ; and very soon my oure
was complete.” J
“I report this for the papers, Mr. Burns,"
said our representative.
“lam glad to hear it, and I hope it will
be read and do good ; it’s all true, and you
can publish it how you like.”

To be made a bishop is the most sump-
tuous form of Christian burial. ” —Mark Pattison.
Queensland Labor-party includes in its
programme for the coming session State fire and
life insurance, land-tax, and a State liquor-mono-
pol y . All good things, but it is much more likely
to get them from the Federal Parliament than
froin the 1 hilp-bossed Legislature of the Piebald
Land. It can’t be too often reiterated that there
is power enough in the hands of the Federal Par-
liament to crush all the corrupt little State olig-
arches, if only the power is rightly used. The
Federal Parliament can create a graduated inoome-
tax, absentee-tax (whereby it can squelch the
foreign land-grant railway syndicates and land-
jobbing syndicates), graduated land-tax (under
which it can establish a great system of resump-
tion of big estates for closer settlement), Federal
bank, Federal assurance office, Federal drink-
monopoly (its power over the customs and excise
can bring that about) —in fact, there is hardly
anything the Federal machine can’t do, if the right
man is at the handle.
Minister Kingston was careful in his
Customs Bill to give the Federal Government the
power of dealing with ships hovering about the
Australian coast for no ostensible purpose. Britain
has long been considering how to deal with the
Dutch “ coopers ” —boats from Flushing and other
Hollandish ports loaded up with liquors, cigars,
tobaccos, scents, and other articles upon which
Britain collects heavy duties. The “coopers”
cruise about off the mouth of the Humber and
oyer the fishing grounds, and do a roaring trade
with the fishing fleets, and also with the coasters
when the latter are going any distance. As long
as they keep outside the three-mile limit the
British revenue cruisers can do nothing, and as
hundreds of outward-bound ships buy large sup-
plies of dutiable articles from the “ coopers,” the
loss to the revenue is very considerable. Kingston
knew his subject pretty well when he put in that
clause, giving power to deal with the “cooper”
when he turns up in these waters.

The Journalism of Jessica,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She asked in her innocent girlhood whether things
were starched before or after being ironed, and
also propounded a theory—alleged to have been
discovered in a medical book—that tea was more
wholesome made of water that had not boiled.
When the family noted Jessica’s haziness on all
ordinary domestic questions they concluded that
she must be a genius, and shut her up in a
room to write poems and things, as the drawing-
room badly wanted a new carpet.
Jessica wrote ordinary cheerful poems during
her early teens. Then she invested in a Cat, which
sat on her manuscript, and breathed softly in her
ear, and brought in lots of dishonestly-acquired
fish to eat off her best poem.
When Jessica was eighteen years old the Cat
died, whereupon its mistress wrote poems of such
anguish that the editor who read them said she
must have suffered much, and the man ought to
be horsewhipped.
After the Cat had quitted her life, leaving her
hearth desolate, Jessica filled the vacant spot in
her affections with a Man, and the Man treated
Jessica shamefully. She kissed him once—it was
on a Manly steamboat —and after six months he
left off being grateful. She then wrote such
bitter verses on the perfidy of the sex that
another of the species got excited, and became
exceedingly angry with still another man who
hadn’t spoken to Jessica more than three times
and had had no connection with the Manly inci-
dent whatever.
Then Jessica arose and took unto herself a hus-
band, alleging that it would be convenient for her
to have on the premises something legally entitled
to be written-up. She conveyed the man to a
good home—which she kindly furnished for him
with his own money—gave him good food and
raiment, and then waited for him to do bad things
which she could mention in print.
But the man did not rise to the occasion at all.
He did not go out at night, or drop love-letters, or
kiss the servant-girl, though she had got him quite
a good-looking one. He merely reposed by the
comfortable parlor fire, and was ordinary. Jessica
sat by her pile of manuscript. She had just begun
her novel with an elaborate description of her
husband, and an intimation that he was a charac-
ter whose conduct was going to be disgraceful.
It struck her that married life was a failure.
She was a patient woman, however, and resolved
to give William time to become disgraceful. She
knew that she must not be too hard on him. Her
mother had told her that all men were trying.
And at last her patience was rewarded. It was
early Spring—just three months after her annexa-
tion of William—that she noticed a change for the
better in that good man. He began to go out at
night, not boldly and bravely, but slipping out by
gradual movements from one chair to the other,
till from the seat nearest the door he passed forth
into the world of temptation and iniquity. Some-
times he returned quite early—say in an hour or
so; sometimes he was later than that. But
always when he returned he had the appearance
of a man who had gone far. His hat was on one
side; his face wore a hot and excited look. Once,
as he furtively took a seat by her side, she noticed
a patch of something on his left shoulder, and her
heart died within her—at least she hurriedly made
a note to that effect. It was a patch of white
powder.
Then Jessica arose and went to her room and
wrote. She indited burning words on the unfaith-
fulness of husbands. She described her blonde
hero as he came home with the patch of
powder on his left shoulder. She noted his snake-
like curvetings out of the room to escape obser-
vation. At the end of that chapter the interest
was fearfully worked-up. .
As the days passed, her novel grew m force and
volume. She wept bitter tears with difficulty over
the perfidy of William, and then wrote-up her
tears in the next chapter. Her wifely heart
throbbed with indignation and her journalistic
soul with exultation. She throbbed sympathetic-
ally either way. Also the eyes of the heroine m
her story glistened with sorrow.
One evening she was sitting m her room, pen in
hand. Her heart, as usual, was full of grief and
enterprise. Just the other afternoon she held seen
William beckon the servant-girl on one side and
had caught his whisper, “Don’t let her go there,
whatever you do.” She noticed that he had a
small parcel under his arm, of which he said no-
thing when he re-appeared later in the drawing-
room She wondered that a man could dare to
bring jewellery for his other love into the very
house of his innocent and deceived wife.
She had barely finished that last word when
she heard the voice of the deceiver. She came
out of her room ; his face beamed brightly upon
her. “Dear, will you come a little walk with
She obeyed. She felt that the farce must .be
played out. He took her arm, as she noted with
bitter scorn (to be expressed in the next chapter),
and led her into what was euphemistically
termed the garden. It was a poor stony tract of
earth ; to be fit for planting, the ground (some-
body had said) would have to be “ made.
“ To-day is your birthday, dear, said William.
“Is it?” He could remember her birthday,
yet forget so much else. It was a good point to
make the unfaithful husband remember his wife s
birthday—it showed up his perfidy with force of
““And so,” he went on excitedly, “ I thought I
would make you a present.” He opened a side
gate newly cut out of the high hedge, and Jessica
looked into what had lately been but a bare plot
of ground. Now it was covered with newly-
planted vegetation—flowers on one side, vegetables
on the other. , ~
“I did it in the evenings, when no one could
see me,” said William, beaming anew. As he
spoke a tall lily bent forward and bespattered his
left shoulder with pollen. “Of course, these are
transplanted ; I brought some packets of seeds
home to-day. 1 did it for a surprise for you You
seemed so moped. Well, ” and he beamed for the
third time, “ what do you think of it ?
Jessica was silent. Then she suddenly formu-
lated an epigram on the man who bound up his
wife’s broken heart with cabbages and consoled
her aching soul with a potato - patch She
mentally sketched a story of William under his
new aspect, and then and there, with a true
woman’s generosity, forgave him for his want of
Jessica’s novel was a great success. William’s
reputation was completely lost, and she took him
to the Plue Mountains on the proceeds.

A Canadian Vengeance.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ip you want to hear yarns, of the sort that never
get into the papers, the convalescent camp at
Pretoria is the place to hear them.
There are all sorts there—Australians chafing to
get out, and Tommies hoping to stay in; Cana-
dians with their broad-brimmed hats, the crown
dented in on four sides; Yeomanry, commonly
known as “ De Wet’s Own ” ; Guards, Royal Ar-
tillery, Lancers—all sorts, in fact. They have a row
of white stones round the place, and military police
to keep you from passing beyond them. In such
a mixed crowd it is not like being with your own
lot, and you must accordingly sing small. The
“ M.P.’s ” have the best end of the stick, and if
they do not catch you when you try to get out for
an evening in town, they do when you are coming
back, so it is all the same; you are up for “ office ”
when the defaulters’ call goes in the morning. It
makes you tired to do nothing but sit on a garden
chair and watch the fire-flies of a night, while
your corps may be going out on the veldt again
any day. But you hear plenty of yarns.
There was a Canadian one night holding forth
to me on the pusillanimity of the British in
general, and of General Hutton in particular.
“ W’dn’t let us help ourselves to a few chickens,”
he said; “ called us gar-dam lootin’ redskins,
next door to it. Don’t think it made much differ-
ence to the chickens, pard. There ain’t too many
chickens left in this section, and I guess a few
came our way.”
Had he heard of the two Canadians who were
caught re-selling rifles to the Boers, and who
received a sentence of ten years apiece for it ?
He had ! They had gone off from their column
in the promiscuous way peculiar to colonials, and
had issued receipts signed “ Lord Roberts ” for
rifles, which they had sold back to the enemy.
They had at length been caught, and one specific
charge of selling two Mausers for £8 was sheeted
home to them. “Now, wasn’t it a gar-dam sil'y
thing to do, stranger?” was his comment. “if
they had made a stake out of it! But a measly
forty dollars ! ” And he spat his contempt.
What about the hanging of those Boers out
Greylingstad way? “I rather guess I was in
that, stranger,” he said. “It was like this, borne
of our outfit went up to a farm to sample the
chickens. There was a white flag up to the ridge-
pole, and they nacherly guessed it was right; but,
damn me, stranger, if there wasn’t a mob of Boers
in the corral near by, and they let drive and
knocked over a sergeant and another, and the rest
got away. Waal, next day some of the squadron
were out on patrol, and a few slipped off and made
for this farm, and grabbed four Boers. They gave
in and held up a white flag when they saw there
was no getaway, thinkin’, mebbe, that nothing
more than a few months’ free board at St. Helena
would happen to them. But they was mistaken
there. We took ’em right out to one o’ them
arrangements they hang meat on, and strung em
up right away, and pumped ’em full o’ holes with
our shooters. ,
“Your officer,” I inquired casually, was he
there too?” ,
“ Waal, stranger, he was, an’ he wasn t. When
he missed us in the morning, he nacherly guessed
where to find us, and he came there right away,
just in time to see us put the ropes round their
necks. ‘Stop that right here,’ says he, ‘and
bring those men along to camp.’ We’lowed wr
couldn’t do it. Told him to keep his head shut,
’cause there was plenty more rope, and the mea,
fixin’ would bear more’n four men—it would carry
an extra one easy.”
“ And were the four Boers you hung the same
who shot your men ? ’’ .• , ,
“Waal, stranger, I couldn’t rightly say that.
But they was Boers, anyway.”

All through its travels the Yic. Labor
Commission seems to have heard the one tale—
that there is no sufficient apprentice system, or
system of technical education, in Australia ; the
rising generation seldom has a chance to learn its
trade thoroughly, and a half-finished, “slummocky
race of workmen is growing up. The worst thing
said about the Yic. minimum-wagelawisthat it has
crushed out a large number of the inefficient —the
inefficient much more than the aged. The em-
ployer who has to pay a good day’s wage wants a
good day’s work, so the inefficient man must go.
It is rough on him, but it is better for the com-
munity in the end. In a better-ordered com-
munity there should be no large proportion of in-
ferior workmen to be crushed out.

question: —
H J. Wrixon, who has succeeded Zeal as President of
Vic Lev Council, is an Irishman who was educated at
Trinity College, Dublin. There, and afterwards while
reading for the Bar, he did much journalistic work. His
father was County Court Judge (or Chairman of General
Sessions, as they were then called) for the N.W. district
of Victoria 40 years ago. Wrixon’s connection with the
press accounts for his kindliness to all pressmen—who
approach him with case and confidence.
All the same, it was this same approaohable
Wrixon who drafted a Bill prohibiting the publi-
cation of Sunday newspapers in Victoria, and per-
suaded the Leg. Assembly to pass it. The Council,
of course, needed no persuasion. Sunday news-
papers are necessarily radical and hateful m

The Bulletin receives this letter from
Narrabri: — .
The timber-cutters and teamsters engaged in building
our national railways are very indignant at what they
consider an infringement of their liberty in depriving
them of their right to buy a “ Tattersall s ticket, if they
chose to do so, and propose to hold an indignation meet-
ing to protest against being interfered with in such an
autocratic and tyrannical manner.
The only possible reply is that the timber-cutters
and teamsters seem to have waited till it is too
late. They didn’t protest against the infringe-
ment of their liberties when “Tattersall s inno-
cent gamble was ejected from N.S.W., and they
can’t complain of the Federal Government doing
what they allowed their own State Govt, to do.
Apparently they didn’t care twopence for
the infringement as long as they could get
the ticket. The outrage on liberty was
iust the same when “Tatt.” was driven out
of Sydney as it will be if he is driven out of
Hobart —only Hobart is his last place of refug© in
the Commonwealth, and if he goes from there the
ticket, which has been getting more and more in-
accessible' for years, may disappear altogether.
Five out of six States refuse to deliver
letters to “Tattersall” in their own territory
—largely ~ because the community was too
tired to protest in time. If the Common-
wealth takes the action of five out of six States
as expressing the will of the majority, it is dim-
cult to see what the timber-cutters can do. Why
get so mad about the rumor that Tattersall may
be shut out of Tasmania, while not worrying m
the least about the fact that he is and long has
been, shut out of N.S.W. ?
A Question
Answered.
If the Pianola ent,.
bles those who
have absolutely no
musical knowledge
to play the piano,
of what interest is
it to those who are
able to play ?
This is often asked, and when asked in our showrooms, generally results in the
purchase of a Pianola ; but more often the question finds expression only in
thought, and offers no opportunity for an answer.
PADEREWSKI HAS A PIANOLA injiis home.
ROSENTHAL HAS ONE.
SAUER HAS ONE.
and only recently has ordered two more for friends in Germany, who are themselves
eminent musical critics. This certainly disposes of the question, although it fails to
give the reasoi- The reason is equally clear.
The Pianola increases the
repertory of even these musi-
cians from twenty-five to fifty
compositions which they can
play in concert form, to prac-
tically everything they may
desire to play. It increases
the repertory of every skilled
musician, and gives a reper-
tory to the novice.
The Pianola does not mar
the piano, as it may be moved
up to or away from the key-
board at will.
The Pianola does not mar
the playing, as artistic taste
receives full opportunity for
expression to which the player
can give his undivided atten-
tion.
The Pianola offers relaxa-
tion from the technical and
enjoyment of the musical.
It is true, since the player
controls the expression, the great musician obtains a more artistic result from the
Pianola than the novice, because he is more critical, but in this very point lies the
secret of the Pianola’s fascination.
The player, whoever he may be, gives his own individuality to his rendition.
This causes the musical taste to develop, and it is not long before the veriest novice
finds his own playing preferable to many of the pianistic efforts in the average concert.
Expression-marks appear on the music as it unrolls, for the guidance of those who
may be unfamiliar with the selection.
In this article there has been little attempt to explain how the Pianola is manipu-
lated, the principal object being to show the desirability of the Pianola for every one
who owns a piano, whether he be skilled in its use or not.
We shall be pleased to mail a complete description of the instrument upon request,
although we urge all who have the opportunity to hear the Pianola for themselves.
Can be bought by instalments if desired. Send for Catalogue C.
Pianola being placed in position to play Upright Piano.
HENRY M. BIRGE & CO.,
Sole Agents for Australasia.
323 GEORGE ST., SYDNEY.

Dropping the Cross.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sea is calm and the winds are low,
And so slowly the great ship moves
That a man might think she was loth to go
From the last of the South she loves ;
And thought speeds over the sea and the sand
To our pleasure and toil and fight,
For the latest link with the sunny land
Goes down with the Cross to-night.
She has lighted our mountains, slope and spur,
She has shone on our golden plains,
Our poets have fashioned their best for her
As she guided their horses’ reins ;
She has lightened toil, she has listened to love,
She has sung to us, gain or loss,
And our hearts have borrowed the dark above
Because we are dropping the Cross.
The moon rides low on the rail,
And the stars of the North blink cold and white,
But they speak no word of the dear South Trail,
And the Love, the Home-Love will not fail—
Hats off to the Cross to-night!
In the long night-watches, drear and lone,
When the footsore cattle lay,
We have seen her beam in the sky and known
Of a friend till the breaking day.
In the wild night-rides when the winds in haste
Set the great gum-boughs a-toss,
By beam and yard she has held us straight
And the bushmen trust the Cross.
She knows the track to a Barwon home
And the ford by a red-gum tree ;
She knows the gleam of a snow-white foam
Where the Three Kings bluff the sea.
With the gray moonbeams on a Coorong reach
She has roosted the wild-fowl down,
And she knows of love on the Manly Beach
And the last boat back to town.
And the moon rides low on the rail,
And the stars of the North blink kind and white,
But they speak no word of the dear South Trail,
And the love, the Home Love, will not fail—
Hats off to the Cross to-night !
We have taken her lead on the coastal run,
We have followed her over the Bight,
We have knotted the sea-miles, one by one,
At the feet of her, night by night ;
From the falling dark we have picked her shape,
We have dropped her out with the day,
We have worn her chains from the Otway Cape
To an anchor in Table Bay.
Some day, perhaps, if the gods are good
We shall break from the Channel rain,
And take the road that a rover should
And lift the Cross again ;
And lift the Cross with her golden spars
Flung wide to the open blue,
And drop the old world’s guiding-stars
For the guiding-stars of the New !
U.K.
WILL H. OGILVIE.
“Tu Quoque!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jack took in the situation at a glance. If his lips
hadn’t gone dry suddenly he would have whistled.
As it was, he said “ damn,” but under his breath,
for Patience was just the kind of girl before whom
no man would care to experiment in language.
She sat quite still with the letter spread out on
her knee. The writing was so large and black and
straggling that there were only a few words on
the page beside the signature, “ Mona Monroe,”
but those words were instinct with all the fire of
unrestrained passion, and to anyone with ordinary
understanding told the whole story. Patience had
more than her fair share of brains ; further, one
could never quite tell how she would act under
given circumstances. There was a mystery or
reserve —call it what you will—in her nature
which was not the least of her charms, but it all
helped to intensify her fiance’s discomfort just
now.
Hers was a face with little claim to beauty if
you picked it to pieces, feature by feature, but
she was more than pretty all the same, and with
a terrible tightening of the heart-strings the man
realised all that he stood to lose by this stupid
mistake. He had given her the wrong letter—
that was all, and instead of the note containing
phrases of conventional congratulation from Mrs.
Monroe on his engagement, Patience had read
that lady’s last letter of passionate farewell—a
letter he had been mad and vain enough to save
from the bonfire necessary before the girl’s arrival
in Bordertown.
That was not the worst of it, either.. Of course
one or two busybodies had found it their painful
duty to acquaint her with the facts of Jack’s
desperate flirtation with that “awful Mrs.
Monroe ” before she had been twelve hours under
the roof of the hospitable P.M.’s wife. But Jack
was clever enough to have guarded against this
by telling Patience beforehand the whole
story of their harmless friendship, from his
own point of view, with a laughing denial
of the rest, and the informers shared the
usual fate of those who came ’twixt the bark and
the tree. _ At the same time he intimated that
the lady in question was not one whom he would
care for as a close friend of his future wife’s ; and,
apart from his fear of confidences and revelations,
he was sincere in what he said. Mona Monroe,
with her unscrupulous conscience and lawless
desires, was no fit mate for this sweet girl, who
had been kept unspotted from the world for his
sake. Whether or no the same character might
have been applied to himself, and the same deduc-
tions made therefrom—he never stopped to think.
He was a man, and therefore incapable of seeing
that the cases were parallel, and he felt
very virtuous as he bade the girl “avoid” the
woman as best she could within the bounds of
chill politeness.
And yet, after all this, she must see the accusing
letter which proved him a liar—and the house of
cards, built of the happiness so nearly within his
grasp, tottered as she spoke !
“ So—it is all true, Jack ? ” she said.
Being no fool, he spoke the truth, which Hone
would serve with a girl keen-witted as she \ *as,
and answered, “Yes, it’s all true.”
She did not speak, and so after a pause he con-
tinued : “ But you’ve a right to hear me, Patience,
before you judge. You have seen enough of this
place to know what a desolate one it is. The only
nouse that was open to strangers was the Mon-
roes’—there was absolutely nothing to do in this
God-forsaken wilderness. She played and sang
and made things passable, and then—though this
you may not take as an excuse—she is very attrac-
+L Ve s^e 1086 to set her fancy on me to pass
the time. She doesn’t really care two straws
about me, so there was no dishonour on my part
to her Women of her type are really incapable
of real love. She is very beautiful and .”
Yes I think I understand,” she said, inter-
rupting, but what of the dishonour to me.
Jack—we were engaged—all the time.”
Jack had expected reproaches, tears, anger—
anything in fact, but this calm, anxious question-
ing, but if she were open to argument so much the
better for him, and he threw his whole mind into
the case for the defendant,” battling to keep
what was dearer than life to him.
He spoke of the difference between real love and
that which Mona Monroe had desecrated by the
name; he spoke of the fear of losing his dear one’s
regard, which had prompted the lie. But most of
. ,®. Jwelt upon the fact (for Patience being a
girl with brains, the truth told best with her) of
the strength of the temptation.
~ Jhere was every excuse for a person,” he said,
who was put to so great a trial as I was —every
circumstance combined to throw us together. She
fascinated me for the time being. I own it,
Patience—but my love for you—the real love, my
little girl was undisturbed. You are just, and
so few women are. Think over all I have said—
and forgive me.”
Then you think,” said Patience, letting a
small white hand rest gently on the dark head
bent before her, “that the one has no right to
blame the other. If two people really love each
other, and one is put into unusual temptation,
ai \< $ lv ® s wa y it, the other should forgive-——
“Can’t you see it yourself, sweetheart, the un-
forgiven one would be ruined—the lives of both,
probably,” he pleaded.
Patience turned her head to look out of the
window. Her small face softened, and her dark
eyes deepened with the shadow of retrospection.
Yes, I see it—l really do,” she said. “ I have
thought it so often myself. I felt just as you
Jkl—afraid to tell you lest I should lose you—and
yet, I would do just the same over again, under
the same circumstances, I think. ... He
wasn t handsome—only nice-looking—but his eyes
compelled me to look. They were full of light,
and used to half-close when he was thinking. You
were so far away, and I was very lonely. Cir-
cumstances persistently threw us together. I will
tell you everything if you care to hear. . . . When
he kissed me I felt so mean to you, and yet—and
yet I let him kiss me over and over again.”
Jack rose to his feet, with an oath he took no
trouble to slur over this time. Hot words of
passionate anger and reproach rose to his lips;
but they died, birth-strangled, before the clear-
ness and force of the arguments which had
afforded him so much joy only a moment before.
He took Mona’s letter from his sweetheart’s hand
and tore it in fragments.
“I don’t want to hear any more,” he said. “I
don’t know what you think on the subject, but it
seems to me it wasn’t worth while. Let us for-
give and forget. ”
.“ Nothing like this is ever wholly forgotten,”
said Patience.
“No,’’said the man, gravely. “And that is
why it wasn’t worth while. Don’t you think so ? ”
But Patience only smiled.
ETHEL MILLIS.
Concerning Vic. Artists’ Winter Exhi-
bition :
. The show is perhaps the best yet held. But the lack of
imagination of Australian artists generally, and Vic-
torians in particular, is still manifest, for the subject-
pictures are weakly conceived, poorly composed, and
altogether lacking in their essentials. It seems extraor-
dinary that a country which suffers from no lack of
imagination or self-assurance in other arts should be
practically destitute of those qualities the moment it is
able to draw and paint. In technical skill with pencil
and brush, in harmony and color, in the decorative treat-
ment of what is commonplace to the man without the
temperament, Australia is able to stand against the
world ; but, when it comes to putting together a subject-
picture—a matter of brains and study, as well as manual
dexterity and instinctive taste, one has only to glance at
the mass of scholarship pictures to see how utterly lost is
the artist here as compared with his brother of other
lands. Broadly-speaking, there is not a subject-picture
in this exhibition worthy of the name. Portraits there
are in plenty, drawn and painted, some of them with a
freedom and skill that lead one to wonder why the artist
has not a world-reputation. Landscapes are even more
numerous and just as meritorious. Black-and-white is
handled excellently, and applied art in metal and wood
can well hold its own. It is only the subject-picture—
the only picture that reaches the great heart of the
public—that is left undeveloped. There are several
attempts based on such hackneyed themes as “ Good
News from the Front,” but all fail despite meritorious
technique. Others are merely landscapes or studies with
a few figures thrown in to try and pass off the effort as a
picture. M'Cubbin’s “Autumn” is one of these—a
decorative landscape pure and simple, and a very fine
landscape too. Bernard Hall, director of Melb. National
Gallery, exhibits the only nude, a study finished with all
the care of a picture, as all his works are. But where
are Hall’s pictures ? He has shown us none yet.
Fine draughtsman and painter though he is, he
seems as lacking in imagination as the native
artists. Perhaps it is his influence that accounts
for some of their shortcomings. His picture, on this
occasion, has a peculiar effect—stripes across the back, as
if the nymph in question had done 25 at the triangles of
Pentridge on some more or less remote occasion. Among
the portraits, the two pictures of children by Leslie
Wilkie (a young gallery student), stand out above all
others. Wilkie—a descendant of the celebrated English
artist of that name—has by these pictures jumped into
the front rank of Australian portraitist. McCubbin’s
portraits are fine bits of painting, especially that of
Loureiro, while Vincent Brun, Violet Teague, and Ina
Gregory exhibit good work. Tom Roberts’s portrait of
Marshall-Hall is an excellent likeness, but destitute of
warmth or sympathy. Portia Geach has an ambitious
figure of a girl seemingly of heroic size and 8£ heads high,
which falls short in achievement. Her decorative work,
however, is admirable, and her portrait of Donald
Macdonald is a very well-painted picture of the war-cor-
respondent in one of his stern moments—probably when
the roast dog of Ladysmith had been served up under-
done. Landscape is represented by a legion of artists,
John Ford Paterson’s mystic hazy canvasses of the
Gippsland bush being the most striking. These
are distinctly superior to his gallery picture, and
though caviare to the general stand as tribute to
Zola’s definition of art being nature seen through the
medium of a temperament. Clara Southern’s is a sort of
work that instantly arrests attention by its bold and suc-
cessful handling, and the Hampton pictures of Mr. and
Mrs. Colquhoun are good examples of impressionism in
its best sense. Percy Lindsay shows, unfortunately, but a
couple of his large collection of bush pictures. Lindsay
is an artist who has quite as much of the true feeling of
Australia as any other man. He is, in a sense, an artistic
Captain Cook, and it is a pity that his two exhibits,
excellent as they are, were not flanked by four or five
more. Miss Louisa Swan (Tas.) meets the visitor on enter-
ing with a boldly-designed and well-carved clock, and
shows her versatility further with two charming little
pictures illustrative of bits of old Sydney and Hobart.
Hal Waugh, a Geelongite, is strong on horseflesh, and
paints a draught gee-gee extremely well. Streeton gets
away from himself for a moment in a low-toned, striking
picture of Sydney harbour, all grey and white, but the
same old Streeton, more Streetonish than ever, turns
up in the water-color section in a couple of green and
scarlet splashes, clever as of yore, but essentially “ long-
range” works. Blamire Young’s decorations are very
fine . Mather, Poole, Lionel Lindsay, and May Vale show
good water-color work, and Nuttall, “Sass,” and W.
Dyson in the black-and-white section. Cobb and Sher-
love have some powerful etchings, and Tranthim Fryer
has sent along a series of repousse panels that would
stand comparison with the best. t
